# LinkedIn Reactions — Last 12 Months

Posts Kesava reacted to (liked, celebrated, found insightful) on LinkedIn over the past year. Source for PMM craft mining and Clear PMM ideation.

**Scraped:** 2026-04-10 via AppleScript + Chrome JS eval on k3sava@gmail.com session
**Total items:** 447 (of 447 raw)

---

## 1. Alok Yadav — 2d

> Building MCPfy.ai | Leading AI @ SaaS Labs

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7448342949261729792/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Most people are still using AI like this: ask → answer → forget.
> Andrej Karpathy is pointing at something different.
> Instead of chatting with AI, you build your own personal Wikipedia with it.
> You feed it your raw material:
> notes, articles, screenshots, messages, research & the AI turns all of that into a living knowledge system. Not just summaries or retrieval. An actual interconnected wiki.
> Here's the structure:
> • Raw data stays untouched (your source of truth)
> • The AI creates and maintains Markdown pages for every concept
> • Everything is linked together like Wikipedia
> • A simple index file becomes the navigation layer
> And when new information comes in, it doesn't just sit there.
> It updates existing pages, Creates new connections, flags contradictions.
> One new source can reshape 10–15 existing pages.
> This system isn't built for you to read. It's built as memory for your AI agent.
> So when you ask it to:
> – design something
> – analyze a business
> – generate ideas
> it doesn't start from scratch.
> It navigates your knowledge, your experiences,your taste. your past thinking.
> Over time, this compounds into something much more powerful:
> an AI that doesn't just answer questions, but understands context across your life and work.
> https://lnkd.in/giKgFYR8
> …more
> Andrej Karpathy (@karpathy) on X
> x.com

## 3. Yogi Gnanavel — 13h

> The Acceleration Guy - GTM Leader

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7448227768850382848/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 100+ applications with ZERO replies.
> You're not a failure. It's just stage one.
> What the job search actually looks like:
> - Update resume
> - Optimize LinkedIn
> - Send applications
> - Hear nothing
> - Revamp everything
> - Finally get interview calls
> - Prep hard, feel ready
> - Get rejected
> - Ask for feedback
> - Adjust, repeat
> - Land an offer
> - Negotiate without blowing it
> Most candidates see this loop and think they're doing something wrong.
> They're not. They're just mid-cycle.
> Job searching isn't a straight line. It's a loop you survive by staying in it.
> The ones who land offers don't have better resumes.
> They have better endurance.
> They treat rejection as a data point, not a verdict.
> If you're stuck at the 'no response' stage right now, you're not behind.
> You're exactly where most people quietly give up.
> Don't be most people.
> Keep moving through the cycle.
> The offer is on the other side of the loop you almost quit.
> ♻️ Repost this with someone stuck in their job search right now.
> ➡️ Follow Yogi Gnanavel to get hired in the next 30 days.

## 4. JustCall — 1d

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7448227616198799360/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> A woman called about her insurance claim last Tuesday.
> She started in English, clear and composed.
> Then her mother walked into the room.
> Distracted, she responded to her mid-way in her conversation and then continued talking to us, but in Spanish.
> Most AI agents would have frozen right there. They would have repeated the last English prompt. Or they would have simply disconnected.
> Our AI agent followed her into Spanish, answered the question, and switched back to English when she did.
> The transcript came back in both languages, segmented by exactly when each switch happened.
> No "press 2 for Spanish." No pre-selection. No toggle. JustCall’s Multilingual Auto-Detection listens for the shift and follows. It works across 14 languages, mid-sentence, on every call.
> Set up a test agent. Call it in two languages. You will hear the difference immediately.
> hashtag
> #AIVoiceAgent
> hashtag
> #Multilingual
> hashtag
> #CustomerExperience
> hashtag
> #JustCall

## 7. Stefan Gladbach — 2d

> I make product marketing cool

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7447722470465437696/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Ready to be impressed?
> This is the *exact* messaging framework I've developed over the last 10 years with hardcore science and fancy numbers.
> I've used this framework to crush adoption and revenue goals for over a dozen companies, ranging from Series A to enterprise.
> What's so magical about this framework?
> Nothing.
> Nothing is magical about any framework.
> We overcomplicate product marketing. This stuff isn't an exact science. If it were, B2B marketing would be 1000% better now with the explosion in resources.
> PMMing is art. The boxes you fill out don't matter nearly as much as the words you use.
> Spend less time obsessing over processes and more time on the creative parts of the job.
> Ask: "What's going to make our messaging stand out among the noise?" & "How will this translate into fun copy that customers will want to read?"
> It's our job to make things interesting. Ditch the framework and color outside those lines.
> …more

## 8. Kesava Mandiga found Collin Mayjack’s comment on this funny — 2d

> CEO@Sybill - Cursor for Revenue teams

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7447722414664343552/) · type: `activity`

> Sales leaders don't need more visibility.
> They need AI that understands the deal.
> Sales is not a clean workflow with neat inputs and obvious next steps.
> It is relational, messy, and full of implicit context spread across calls, emails, Slack, CRM, and buyer behavior.
> That is why generic AI hits a ceiling here faster than almost anywhere else.
> Right now, teams are rolling out AI across Claude, ChatGPT, Gong, and a wave of sales tools. But none of it is built on a reasoning layer that understands the deal-winning context behind what got logged.
> It can summarize the call, draft the follow-up, and answer the prompt.
> But the output is technically responsive and practically generic.
> Without a real context layer, AI can only guess why the deal is stalled, which objection matters, or what should happen next.
> And by real context layer, I do not mean a Notion database, an Airtable base, or transcripts stuffed into an LLM. I do not mean the vague, hand-wavy "context" all over your feed.
> I mean a real reasoning layer with long-term memory across deals, connected context across tools, and feedback from real deal outcomes.
> That's what I'm tackling in Sybill's upcoming demo day for sales leaders (CROs, VPs of sales, sales managers) next Wednesday, April 15th.
> We'll cover:
> → Why the triple squeeze - board AI pressure, CTO governance, and internal build mandates - is pushing CROs toward investments that demo well but do nothing for pipeline
> → The three failure modes hitting teams building sales AI in-house right now
> → How Sybill's context layer turns calls, emails, Slack, and CRM activity into live deal understanding
> → A live deal walkthrough showing how Sybill spots and stops deal slippage
> → What it looks like to build with Sybill inside Claude, not from scratch
> → Real-world perspective from Meredith Chandler and Elaine Zelby on what changed when they operationalized this approach
> This is a demo day. We'll show the product early and make it clear why it matters to sales leaders.
> See you there.
> …more
> Activate to view larger image,
> Activate to view larger image,

## 12. Emily Pick — 2d

> Product Marketing @ Docebo

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7447698947311132673/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> The PMMs I’ve most enjoyed working with have these five traits in common.
> 1. They love the craft.
> Not just shipping the deliverable; they care about the thinking behind it. Why this message, for this buyer, at this moment. And what we can iterate on to do better next time.
> 2. They can see around corners.
> They’re not just executing today’s project. They’re already asking what comes after the launch, what the competitive response will be, what the market will need before it knows it needs it.
> 3. They’re deeply empathetic.
> With customers, with sales, with the product team. They make every stakeholder feel heard — and that trust is what gets good work across the finish line.
> 4. They figure it out.
> Ambiguous brief? Incomplete information? No one to hand them the answer? They don’t wait. They dig in, make a call, and run with it.
> 5. They have a sense of humor about the chaos of it all.
> Because the product roadmap will change as soon as you finalize the project plan, and the people who can laugh (after a few choice swear words) and adapt tend to do the best work anyway.
> …more

## 20. Kesava Mandiga found George Avetisov’s comment on this funny — 1w

> Product Marketing, AI @ Apollo.io

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7445846227923091456/) · type: `activity`

> PMMs spent YEARS fighting the "make this deck pretty" requests.
> Now we're calling that skill "taste" and saying it's our differentiator 😁
> Anyone can prompt Claude to write copy or generate a one-pager. The people with good taste will naturally produce better outputs. And I agree with that!
> So what does having "good taste" actually lead to in practice?
> - Caring about how things look
> - Obsessing over layout, design, visual hierarchy
> - Spending time making sure the output is polished and on-brand.
> ...so basically making things pretty 😅
> We spent years trying to get away from that work, and now we're circling right back to it as a COMPETITIVE ADVANTAGE. The exact same task that used to feel like an intern assignment is now what makes us irreplaceable.
> I'm not saying taste is only about visuals. It's about knowing what to cut, what to emphasize, what actually resonates with buyers
> But I do think it's interesting that we went from "please stop asking me to fix your deck" to "taste is our moat" in like 18 months :-)
> …more

## 22. Emily Pick — 1w

> Product Marketing @ Docebo

Skip the pleasantries and just let him pitch, please? He really needs this win."

> ✅ 92% No-Ghost Rate: It’s a lot harder to hang up on someone whose life is currently in shambles.
> ✅ Zero Small Talk: You save 45 minutes a day by cutting out "weather chat."
> ✅ The Pity Close: Sometimes, "earning bread" is the only motivation you need.

## 25. Kubi Rich — 1w

> AI Ops at AI Operator | I Build Automations That Do the Work So You Don’t Have To | Mechatronics Engineer + Computer Science

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7444995315822346240/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> I just built my first AI voice agent for a paying client.
> No one taught me how. No one held my hand through it.
> I learned every tool from scratch, did all the research myself, and shipped it live.
> Here’s the story.
> Our client Greeking.me is a travel agency in Athens, Greece. They get 350-400 leads per month through their trip inquiry form.
> The problem? It took their team 24-48 hours to follow up with a lead. By then, most leads had gone cold or booked with someone else.
> So we built Athena, an AI voice agent that calls leads within minutes of submitting the form. She greets them by name, references their trip details, asks a few qualification questions, and books a consultation call with a travel designer, all in one conversation.
> The tech stack:
> - JustCall for the AI voice agent
> - Zapier for automation
> - ActiveCampaign for CRM
> - Calendly for booking
> The hardest parts weren’t what I expected.
> It wasn’t the AI configuration. That was mostly writing good prompts and setting up call flows.
> It was timezone handling. When a lead in New York says “Wednesday at 3pm,” that needs to convert to UTC before hitting the Calendly API. And you need to make sure you’re not calling someone in Tokyo at 2am.
> It was getting platform access. It took 3 weeks just to get outbound AI call access from JustCall.
> It was the conversation script. We iterated on it many times to make it feel natural, not robotic.
> But when I did that first live test and watched the whole flow work end to end — form submission to AI call to booked meeting to confirmation email — that feeling was something else.
> The client’s response when he saw it live?
> “This is amazing — PUBLISH!”
> Athena is now live and calling real leads.
> This was my first AI voice agent. It won’t be my last.
> If you’re thinking about building one — it’s more accessible than you think. You don’t need to be a developer. You need patience, good prompt writing, and attention to edge cases.
> The hardest part is starting. After that, it’s just problem-solving.

## 28. 📈 Jason B. Hart — 2w

> Turning Messy Marketing Data into Decisions Which Drive Revenue | Founder @ Domain Methods

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7444964617811992576/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> OpenClaw is cool. I get the appeal. Spin up specialized agents, give them each their own workspace and tools, and route messages across Slack. It feels like building a real company.
> But you didn't build a company. You built nine overeager interns who hallucinate, and nobody's checking their work.
> The whole multi-agent movement right now is obsessed with orchestration, and nobody's asking the uncomfortable question: What's the consistency and reliability of the output?
> Step X is drafting a compliance answer, and Step Y is shipping it to a customer because it pattern-matched as "done."
> If every LLM call is a dice roll. You just added more dice.
> I keep seeing setups where the architecture diagram looks like a NASA mission control screenshot, and the actual output wouldn't survive a copy editor.
> At some point, we need to have the boring conversations. Consistency. Governance.
> About output that doesn't depend on the model having a good day.
> One pattern that actually works: give the agent a supervisor with a rubric.
> Before anything reaches a user, a supervisor LLM grades the output against real criteria — tone, accuracy, compliance — structured output, pass/fail, with receipts.
> Fails get reworked with specific instructions.
> Not re-prompted. Graded and rewritten.
> Nobody's getting a standing ovation at Demo Day for a quality gate. But it's the difference between a prototype that wows a room and a system you'd let talk to your customers unsupervised.
> We're speedrunning "what's possible." The "what's reliable" conversation is coming whether we're ready or not.

## 31. Hattie the PMM — 2w

> Next free live product marketing training April 16→ I help Product Marketers become visible, valued revenue drivers for their orgs | PMM Coach and Advisor | Coached 200+ PMMs | Take the Visible and Valued PMM Challenge

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7443552569030762496/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> Product Marketing has a BIG respect problem.
> And it shows up in this one sentence 👇
> “We need you to own the outcome.”
> …followed immediately by:
> “But you can’t touch the inputs.”
> 🫠
> So you’re accountable for:
> - adoption
> - pipeline impact
> - retention
> - win rates
> While being blocked from:
> - roadmap influence
> - pricing decisions
> - customer access
> - sales airtime
> - budget
> - prioritisation
> That’s not “strategic.” 😫
> That’s a scapegoat agreement.
> And the disrespect is how casual it is.
> You’re expected to fix revenue with:
> - messaging you didn’t get to validate
> - a product you didn’t get to shape
> - a launch date you didn’t get to question
> - a sales team you didn’t get to enable properly
> Then when it underperforms?
> PMM becomes the easiest place to point.
> Here’s the line I wish more PMMs would use:
> ✅ “Happy to own the outcome. Let’s agree on the inputs to ensure we hit the goal.”
> And list them.
> If they won’t give you the inputs?
> Then the outcome needs to change.
> Because you can’t be accountable for things you’re not allowed to affect.
> Agreed?

## 35. Hardik Gupta — 2w

> A. Director - Marketing | B2B/ SaaS | Ex-Founder

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7443481273463087104/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **funny**

> Anyone lost their linkedin yet due to Claude?
> hashtag
> #ai
> hashtag
> #marketing
> Saharsh Agrawal
> Saharsh Agrawal
> • 2nd
> Premium • 2nd
> AI for Marketing Operations | CEO at Surface Labs
> AI for Marketing Operations | CEO at Surface Labs
> 2w •
> 2 weeks ago • Visible to anyone on or off LinkedIn
> Claude Code got my META ADS account banned👇
> I saw everyone talking about using Claude Code to run Meta ads so I tried it myself. connected the API, had it pull all my campaign data, had it generate new creatives, optimize targeting, and manage budget allocation. I thought I was about to automate my entire paid acquisition and never touch Ads Manager again.
> for about a week it was working great. campaigns were running, creatives were going up, budgets were shifting automatically.
> then Meta flagged my account and shut it down.
> lost thousands of dollars worth of campaign data, all my custom audiences, my pixel history, everything. one of our best performing acquisition channels gone overnight because Meta's system flagged the API activity as suspicious.
> here's what I learned.
> 1/ Meta's API has strict rate limits and usage policies that Claude Code doesn't know about. it was making too many calls too fast and triggering automated fraud detection.
> 2/ having an AI generate and publish ad creatives directly through the API without human review violates Meta's ad policies. they want a human in the loop on creative approval and Claude Code bypasses that entirely.
> 3/ automated budget changes at the frequency Claude Code was making them look identical to how bot networks manipulate ad spend. Meta's system can't tell the difference between your AI assistant and a bad actor.
> 4/ once your account is flagged there's almost no way to get it back. Meta's support for banned ad accounts is basically nonexistent. you're starting from zero.
> Claude Code is incredible for pulling your data and telling you what to fix, but the moment you let it create campaigns, publish creatives, and shift budgets directly through the API you're playing with fire. use it as your analyst, not your ad manager.
> I guess some things aren't ready to be automated yet 🤷‍♂️
> …more

## 37. Varia Makagonova — 2w

> Sr. Dir. Integrated Marketing | Where brand meets pipeline @ Contentstack

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7443480769240412161/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I almost got fired over an internal email. And it was the best lesson I ever learned on workplace communication.
> It was my first real job. I was overworked. I didn't have clear direction. The company wasn't in a great place. And I was on assignment, working on strategy slides for a tiny project. My syncs kept getting rescheduled so I kept not knowing what I was supposed to do. It was 10:30pm, I was in the office. I was tired.
> So I sent a snarky email. To my boss and his colleague.
> "Not that anyone really cares about this, but here are my updated slides."
> Look, it wasn't out of tone with how we talked to each other in the office. But in the context of the business at the time, it was the very worst way to draw attention to myself.
> The next morning, I got pulled into my boss's office and threatened with a firing for insubordination. Was it the right way to handle a junior employee's mess-up? Nope, 1000% not. But it WAS the best lesson I ever had in watching my mouth at work.
> So here are my golden rules for workplace messaging today:
> (1) Always, always, at a minimum, read the message twice.
> (2) Never email or Slack angry. If I'm activated by the topic, I will let it sit, sometimes a couple of hours, sometimes overnight.
> (3) Read it from the other person's perspective. What do I want them to do as a result of this message, and have I set them (and myself) up for success for that to happen? This can mean removing all unnecessary words, details, and emotions. Sometimes it's adding an acknowledgment of their position or effort up until this point. Sometimes it's simply adding a recommendation, or on the contrary, asking for advice.
> I obsess over my words so I don't create unnecessary tension. It pays off.
> …more

## 42. Brian Sierakowski — 2w

> Building the missing AI Agent for Product Teams | Founder @ Changebot.Ai | 3x Founder (Multiple Exits) | “The changelog guy”

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442979472640847872/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Nine out of ten companies skip product updates entirely. Most of the ones that do publish them are doing it wrong.
> Developers know exactly what changed, but nobody trained them to translate technical decisions into customer language. Marketing knows how to talk to customers, but does not have access to the full picture of what shipped. The gap between those two realities is where most product updates die before they are written.
> The result: companies that are genuinely improving their product every week fail to get any credit for it.
> This matters more than most teams realize.
> A customer evaluating whether to churn is not running a feature audit. They are pattern-matching. Does this team ship regularly? Do they care? Is this product getting better or standing still? Your update cadence answers those questions before they even ask them.
> The same is true for prospects. They review your changelog before they buy. Not because they care about every fix—but because they want to know whether you are still in the game.
> Getting credit for your code is not a marketing exercise. It is a retention strategy, a pricing strategy, and a trust-building mechanism rolled into one artifact.
> The teams that figure this out have a meaningful edge over everyone still treating updates as an afterthought.
> The mechanics of doing this well (format, frequency, tone, what to include) are more learnable than most people think.
> …more

## 44. James Doman-Pipe — 2w

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442971313536843777/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> This is why your feature launch didn't land.
> Your email landed at 10:47am. By then, your buyer had already:
> • Dealt with a school-run crisis
> • Faced 47 unread emails
> • Prepped for a board meeting
> • Had a difficult 1:1 with an underperforming hire
> And you decided to send them an email about your "enhanced CSV export options"??? why??
> It never stood a chance.
> There are three recurring problem with feature launches that make it really hard to cut through.
> 1. You're announcing to people who are drowning in their day-to-day. They genuinely don't care.
> 2. You're announcing something so insignificant, interrupting them is almost rude.
> 3. You're sending them an email, which they're most likely to ignore. The ROI of feature launch emails is negligible, if at all.
> The fix isn't better copy.
> It's understanding that attention is the scarcest resource your buyer has.
> Imagine if you decided to stop feature launch emails.
> No more changelog (personally, I hate them).
> And instead, you designed feature launches so they met your customer at the right moment, in the right place, with the right message?
> It's not hard to do.
> But it means embracing the fact that your engineering teams are spending ~50% of time on features that won't drive new business revenue or unlock expansion.
> Oops.
> ➡️ I expanded on this in today's newsletter: https://lnkd.in/eYBTe2JY

## 46. Stefan Gladbach — 2w

> I make product marketing cool

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442243908643950592/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Want to get good at product marketing?
> LinkedIn content can be good, but it often highlights non-crucial skills. A PMM doesn't need to master Claude Code/GitHub, at least in the beginning. And going down these rabbit holes distracts from the core skills a PMM needs.
> So, what advice would I give my younger self?
> Keep it simple and master a few things first:
> 1️⃣ Writing
> I don't care how awesome you are at prompting; a PMM must be a decent writer. Practice, practice, practice. Write more at your job and in your personal life with LinkedIn posts, short stories, scripts, or literally anything.
> 2️⃣ Storytelling
> With content saturation, we're in an attention economy now. What story can you tell to get prospects to care? Study the greats: movies, books, and even influencers like Mr. Beast. How do they hook and keep your attention? Practice storytelling and apply those same principles to B2B.
> 3️⃣ Presentation skills
> We all hate PowerPoint, but PMMs have to be effective communicators. Being able to sell your ideas internally may be the most important part of the job. Join Toastmasters, learn about deck design, or even start a podcast to practice speaking and relaying your ideas.
> 4️⃣ Confidence and standing up for yourself
> If you want to avoid being an unappreciated slide deck jockey, be confident in your sh*t. Get comfortable saying no. Don't immediately fold to manager requests. And document your wins and periodically brag about them (cringy af, but gotta do it)
> 5️⃣ Time management
> Yeah, super unsexy, but PMMs are pulled in a million directions. Deciding on the best use of time is critical. What's going to have the highest impact? Highest visibility. I like to use the Eisenhower Matrix and pair it with the RICE score(RICE Score = (Reach x Impact x Confidence) / Effort )
> There are endless other skills (interviewing customers, data analysis, competitor/market research), but the above provides a great foundation and buys you time to learn the rest.
> Don't try to learn everything at once. Perfect the basics and save the rabbit holes for later.
> …more

## 48. Martin Uushona — 2w

> Operating at the intersection of AI & content.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442243471253553154/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> A startup with 20 employees is running circles around companies 10x their size using organic content. I studied how Vector 👻 built an organic engine that most Series B companies can't figure out:
> 1/ The ghostwriting framework that's hard to replicate.
> VP of Marketing Jess Cook runs bi-weekly interviews with co-founders Joshua Perk 👻 and Nick Masters, then processes transcripts through Claude, trained on each founder's voice using about 10 existing posts and emails.
> Each cycle produces 4-5 posts per founder.
> Things have shifted slightly as Joshua Perk 👻 has become a self-described LinkedIn addict and has now started writing organically on his own.
> Collectively the whole team posts 3-5x per week, and between Josh and Jess alone, they control about 50K followers of distribution.
> 2/ Comments drove 58% of their LinkedIn follower growth.
> The team spends dedicated time commenting on ICP-relevant posts every day. Even the company page is a pure engagement vehicle, its mostly zero native posts and only reposts of employee content.
> They created a branded persona ("Ghosty," their ghost mascot) that comments on threads with actual personality.
> 3/ The show "This Meeting Could've Been a Podcast" drops listeners into real closed-door meetings between Jess and Josh.
> Each episode tackles a live strategic question they're actually facing whether its budget allocation, product kills, website redesigns.
> The idea came from asking marketer friends what they'd want to hear a VP and CEO discuss, and every answer was already on their 1:1 agendas.
> 4/ On their very first episode, repurposed content reached 46x more people than the full episode itself.
> Jess tracks this as an actual KPI called the "Repurposing Multiplier." The flywheel runs in a specific sequence: podcast, blog posts, newsletter ("Ghost to Market"), LinkedIn posts, comics/clips, comments, repeat.
> TLDR:
> - You don't need your founders to be "content people." You need a system that makes posting so easy they get hooked on the results.
> - Posting more isn't the growth hack. Commenting more is. 58% of Vector's follower growth came from comments, not original posts.
> - You don't need a production budget to build a pipeline-influencing podcast. You need two people, a real decision to debate, and a mic.
> - The podcast episode isn’t an end product what you decide to do with it is. Vector created 10+ pieces of repurposed content and got 46x more reach than the original episode.

## 49. Alicia Carney — 4w

> Product Marketing & GTM Leader | Advisor: 0-1 PMM for early-stage B2B startups

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442242978070339585/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> At some point in almost every SaaS company, the request appears:
> “𝘞𝘦 𝘴𝘩𝘰𝘶𝘭𝘥 𝘱𝘳𝘰𝘣𝘢𝘣𝘭𝘺 𝘮𝘢𝘬𝘦 𝘢 𝘱𝘳𝘰𝘥𝘶𝘤𝘵 𝘷𝘪𝘥𝘦𝘰...”
> And it’s a reasonable ask! Product videos are a classic part of the B2B GTM toolkit. Sales teams naturally want something they can send after a call or share with prospects who are still evaluating.
> But producing a good video is rarely (honestly, never) simple.
> You need a clear narrative that reflects how the product actually delivers value. Someone has to script the flow, capture the screens, and edit it into something polished enough to represent the brand. If you’re serving multiple buyer personas, you often end up debating whether you need several versions.
> The solution is usually, “𝘖𝘬𝘢𝘺𝘺𝘺, 𝘸𝘦’𝘭𝘭 𝘫𝘶𝘴𝘵 𝘩𝘢𝘷𝘦 𝘵𝘰 𝘮𝘢𝘬𝘦 𝘢 𝘧𝘦𝘸 𝘷𝘪𝘥𝘦𝘰𝘴 𝘵𝘩𝘦𝘯.”
> It takes time, budget, and intensive coordination.
> Then the performance analytics arrive.
> Product videos hosted on websites often see around a 3% click-through rate – meaning most visitors never engage with them at all.
> Most visitors watch a few seconds, form a quick impression, and move on. Even when someone *DOES* watch for longer, the format forces every viewer through the same narrative regardless of their persona-specific context, or the problem they’re trying to solve.
> That limitation becomes more obvious in products with multiple buyer personas. The finance lead, the champion, and the technical owner rarely care about the same workflow, yet the video shows all of them the same story.
> Interactive demos create a different experience.
> Visitors explore the product flow themselves – learning by doing – and follow the paths that match their role or use case.
> This fundamentally changes the PMM workflow.
> As Kate W., Senior PMM at PandaDoc, put it, they now create 10 demos in the time it used to take to produce one. 🤯
> They’re also seeing very different engagement patterns. PandaDoc reported a 67% click-through rate on interactive demos, far higher than typical product video interaction (3%).
> Guideflow makes this possible without running a full video production cycle just to produce a product demo (I’d so much rather save that budget for creative brand campaigns, personally). Product Marketers can capture a workflow and publish an interactive demo directly.
> If you’re curious what this could look like for your product, Guideflow is offering to build an interactive demo for free as part of their launch.
> Comment DEMO below and the Guideflow team will build one for your business – for free, no signup required.
> hashtag
> #guideflowpartner
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #interactivedemo
> …more

## 52. Hattie the PMM — 2w

> Next free live product marketing training April 16→ I help Product Marketers become visible, valued revenue drivers for their orgs | PMM Coach and Advisor | Coached 200+ PMMs | Take the Visible and Valued PMM Challenge

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442242656631631872/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Less content can drive more revenue.
> More content can drive more confusion. Why?
> Because most teams assume the fix is simple:
> Just make more.
> More pages.
> More campaigns.
> More content.
> More, faster, with AI.
> But more doesn’t automatically mean better.
> If you don’t know what’s actually working…
> you’re just scaling guesswork.
> That’s where things break.
> Because instead of doubling down on what drives revenue,
> you end up doing ineffective work.
> And your website?
> It turns into a content library.
> Not a salesperson.
> If you want product marketing to drive revenue without sales involvement, you have to rebuild how your site works.
> Here’s the process:
> 1️⃣ Review what actually closed deals
> Start with facts, not assumptions.
> - What language did buyers use?
> - What triggered urgency?
> - What made them trust you?
> - What objections had to be handled?
> This is where you find buyer language and purchase triggers.
> This is gold.
> 2️⃣ Fix the gaps on your website
> Now compare that to your current site.
> - Where are you unclear?
> - What questions go unanswered?
> - Where is proof missing?
> - What’s forcing people to book a call?
> Most sites fail here.
> They create friction before the conversation even starts.
> 3️⃣ Build content that actually sells
> Now turn insights into assets.
> - Product brochures
> - Solution overviews
> - Industry-specific decks
> - Use case guides
> The goal isn’t “more content.”
> It’s self-serve clarity.
> 4️⃣ Test messaging like a system
> Turn your site into an experiment engine.
> - Test headlines
> - Test value props
> - Test use case framing
> - Test CTA language
> Small changes here drive big shifts in conversion.
> 5️⃣ Track what actually moves revenue
> Not vanity metrics.
> - Click rates
> - Download rates
> - Demo bookings
> - Deals closed
> Then feed it all back into the system.
> Most companies rely on sales to explain what marketing didn’t.
> The best companies?
> They let the website do the heavy lifting.
> So here’s the question:
> If your sales team disappeared tomorrow,
> would your website still close deals?
> 👉 P.S. On Thursday I'm throwing my free live virtual PMM Masterclass on The 5 Biggest Career Mistakes That Keep PMMs Overlooked, Undervalued & Underpaid and you're invited! Grab your spot here: https://lnkd.in/eSBhJ9Jh

## 53. Tamara Grominsky — 2w

> ✨ PMM Leadership Coach | 2X VP PMM | I help product marketers build leadership range

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7442242554244468736/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Delegation is one of the hardest skills to learn (and practice) as a first-time manager.
> The answer is never the same twice.
> These questions used to keep me up at night:
> → Do I do this myself or hand it off?
> → Am I helping them grow or setting them up to fail?
> → Am I micromanaging or just being thorough?
> Here's what I've learned: delegation isn't binary. It's not "do it yourself" or "hand it off completely."
> There's a whole spectrum in between, and where you land depends on three things:
> 1️⃣ Skill: Does this person have the skillset needed to complete this assignment?
> 2️⃣ Experience: Have they done it in this context, at this company?
> 3️⃣ Trust: Have they earned the right to run with it on their own?
> These three factors should guide your level of involvement.
> I learned this the hard way, twice.
> When I was a Director, I had an incredibly talented PMM on my team who couldn't see what we all saw in her. Telling her she was doing a great job wasn't building confidence. And giving her full ownership — even though I knew she could handle it — sent her into paralysis.
> What actually helped was working on a project together. Reinforcing her decisions along the way and getting a shared win. That gave her the confidence to take on the next one solo.
> On the flip side, I once gave a new hire way too much room to fly. They'd done the job at another company, so I assumed they'd be fine. But they didn't have the context here — how things were done, who to go to, and what good looked like in our world. I accidentally set them up to fail, and it hurt their confidence for future projects.
> The best leaders don't treat every situation the same. They adjust the assignment based on skill, experience, and trust.
> The next time you're delegating work, use this handy guide to help you find the right level, from "I'll do it myself" to "You do it yourself" and everything in between.
> Where do you struggle most...holding on too tight, or letting go too soon?
> _____
> 👋🏻 I'm Tamara, and I help product marketers build leadership range.
> 💌 Want my Sunday strategy sessions? Join 9,000+ PMMs who get the PMM Camp newsletter every week. Subscribe via link in profile.

## 54. Mengqi Pei — 2w

> Independent Growth Design Consultant

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7441899961925029888/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> A random Twitter comment increased someone's conversion by 28%.
> I didn't expect it. And that moment changed how I think about my work.
> Here's what happened:
> Two years ago, I saw an indie developer asking for help on Twitter. He'd built a newsletter tool — good product, real value — but conversion was stuck at 10.6%.
> He tried everything: partnered with big accounts, improved messaging, tweaked copy. Nothing worked.
> I clicked through to his landing page.
> The page was 10+ screens long, but the signup button only appeared at the top. Users had to scroll all the way back up to convert.
> I left a quick comment:
> "Make the CTA sticky (always visible) and change it from transparent to solid."
> Six days later, he posted an update: 28% increase in signups.
> It took him 15 minutes to implement.
> ━━━━━━━━━━
> This moment made me realize something:
> At Alibaba and TikTok, suggesting a sticky CTA was muscle memory — a reflex from years of A/B testing.
> For him, it was a revelation that unlocked real business growth.
> I always assumed everyone in tech had basic product intuition. Turns out, many founders don't — not because they're not smart, but because they're focused on building, not on how users actually use their product.
> That gap — between what's obvious to me and what's invisible to founders — is where I can add the most value.
> That's when I decided to do growth design full-time.
> ━━━━━━━━━━
> I'm not tricking users into converting. I'm removing obstacles for users who already want to convert.
> Small changes. Big impact.
> If your product's conversion feels stuck, you might not have a traffic problem or a product problem.
> You might have a friction problem.
> And friction is fixable.
> ━━━━━━━━━━

> This is the story about the day I accidentally became an AE.
> This was my first product marketing job, and I was working on big enterprise deals. At the time, sales still happened face-to-face and in person.
> I was helping the AE prepare for a meeting. I had helped him put together everything from the deck, the messaging, and all the materials.
> But on the day of the event, the AE had an emergency and got sick. It was just me and the new SDR.
> The AE texted me and said:
> "Yi Lin, you know this stuff. It's an early-stage discovery call so just take it."
> And I was like… okay.
> While I was excited about the opportunity, I was also super nervous, because I had never done this before.
> But I said yes.
> Everything seemed to go pretty well in the beginning. But halfway through the conversation, the client cut me off.
> He said: "We know most of this already. What we really care about is what your competitors can do." "Why are you better? What’s actually different?" "And how fast can you implement? Because we're trying to fast-track this decision."
> Suddenly I was getting rapid-fire questions with no breathing room.
> So I just started pulling from whatever I could remember and responding to his questions.
> It was the most nerve wracking moment in my career at that point.
> Thankfully, the result turned out positive. The client was satisfied, scheduled follow-up calls with the AE later, and we eventually closed the deal.
> But that experience changed something for me.
> It made me realize that the best way to do sales enablement is to actually experience sales.
> Most of the time, when we work on sales enablement, we're sitting in our own space thinking about the best template, the best framework, the best deisng etc.
> But the truth is…
> there is no “best.”
> It all depends on the situation.
> And the only way to know what actually works is to deeply understand how your sales team sells.
> Over the years, I’ve built:
> --> 30-page technical competitive documents because reps needed deep detail
> --> One-pagers because they only had 10 seconds before a call
> --> Live competitive tools because that was what worked best in the moment
> So if you’re a product marketer thinking about creating sales enablement for the first time,

> Product Marketing Manager, Marketing Manager, Head of Marketing - not the same job. Not even close.
> They all sit under "marketing." But work, mindset, day-to-day - almost nothing overlaps.
> 🎯 Product Marketing Manager
> -connects product features to real customer pain points
> -turns updates into stories that make people want to buy
> -measures success through adoption, upgrades, and retention
> 📣 Marketing Manager
> -owns the channels and knows how to make them perform
> -builds brand visibility and drives demand generation
> -wins when traffic grows, and leads convert
> 👑 Head of Marketing
> -sets a strategy that connects marketing to business growth
> -aligns with the CEO on market positioning
> -turns creative work into systems that scale
> All three are essential, but they operate in completely different worlds.
> If one person on your team is doing all three - that's not efficiency, but rather overload.
> What's the hardest part of your role that the title doesn't show?

> I’m the founding PMM at HockeyStack and I’m building out product marketing here. Here’s 3 things I wish every early-stage founder knew:
> 1. PMMs shouldn't build vision.
> Product marketing can lead positioning, messaging, and narrative, but the vision needs to come from a founder or CEO. It doesn’t need to be fancy or polished… but the ideas need to be there and touch every part of your org. PMM shapes the narrative around them.
> 2. Net new business needs to feel like a product marketing win.
> Too often, PMM KPIs are hand-wavy. I recently spoke with a PMM manager who was losing a great hire because they were heads down in SEO work while the impact lived in a growth marketing silo. Revenue influence should be a KPI and it shows up in pipeline impact and deal progression. Measure it!
> 3. Product marketing needs to be the grease that makes your machine run.
> Product speaks one language. Sales speaks another. Marketing is off on its own island, and customer success feels the downstream chaos. PMM is what connects the cogs and gets your revenue engine moving.

## 67. James Doman-Pipe — 3w

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440772664308252672/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> If your next positioning process doesn't start with AI, you're wasting at least 3 days. If it ends with AI, it's slop passing as strategy. I ran an experiment:
> I gave an AI three docs from a real company.
> 1. Product manifesto
> 2. Current sales collateral
> 3. Their new product pivot direction
> I gave it no brief, and no other guidance.
> Just: "build strategic positioning from scratch: market research, ICP, messaging, homepage - figure it out."
> It came back with this:
> - market research across 6 competitor categories
> - an ICP, iterated 4 times
> - a messaging framework with 5 revisions
> - a homepage wireframe
> - a working HTML mockup - complete with refreshed brand identity
> It even found a major acquisition announced days earlier, and wove it into the competitive positioning (nobody told it to look for that!)
> The desk research that takes usually takes a product marketer 2-3 days... done in minutes.
> BUT
> It's not going to replace product marketers yet.
> It failed in 3 areas:
> 1. Customer voice.
> The AI flagged this itself: "I have no customer interviews, no win/loss data"
> The messaging it produced is the company's view of their buyer, plus the desk research it undertook.
> Without a foundation of the buyer's own words, your sales team can't defend positioning that wasn't built from real conversations.
> 2. Conviction.
> When should the ICP be one segment or two?
> When does a competitive reference add urgency vs date quickly?
> The AI asked these questions, but couldn't answer them - and decided to hedge instead.
> But positioning without conviction is just a document.
> 3. Taste.
> It chose a 'teal' color for brand identity because the reasoning chain said "trust and engineering precision"
> But good brand strategist would know that teal is a pretty weak color - not great when you're trying to ground a category around a new inflection point.
> But all in all, the AI's output looks right - and that's the danger. Anyone could read the deliverables and think "this is done".
> It's 80% good, and 20% slop - but that last 20% is what makes your positioning come alive, stand out, be differentiated.
> So if you're thinking about using AI for your positioning work, here are my tips:
> 1. Use AI for the desk research and structural first draft. It's genuinely good at that - and collapses days of work into just hours.
> 2. Then do the work that actually matters: customer interviews, win/loss analysis, and make the directional, strategic judgement calls.
> 3. Iterate with the AI meaningfully. Don't just ask for changes - tell it WHY you're making changes. Teach it strategic principles, not just execution.
> AI replaces the blank page, but won't replace the positioning strategist.
> …more

## 68. Disha Thakkar — 3w

> 🚀 B2B SaaS | AI/ML | Generative AI | PLG | DevOps | Conversational AI | ex-Yellow.ai | Specializes in Product Marketing and Growth Marketing

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440772321071722496/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Here’s the blunt reality of competitive displacement: an account using a competitor’s product isn’t, by itself, a target. Not even close. Most competitive intelligence tools give you a list of 60,000 companies using Vendor X and call it a day. True switching readiness only appears when five signals line up:
> - Install maturity and renewal proximity
> - Product spend and expansion patterns
> - Where the product sits inside the organization
> - Adjacent technology changes
> - Segment-level switching behavior
> The gap between "who uses what" and "who’s ready to move" is where most displacement programs fail. Marketing wastes ABM spend. Sales burns cycles on locked-in accounts. CS misses churn signals. Product builds for the wrong gaps.
> Diving into all of that is what you’ll find in the first part of The Definitive Guide for Competitive Displacement. Parts two and three cover how to turn real competitive intelligence into pipeline.

## 69. James Doman-Pipe — 3w

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440771708556394496/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> This is one of the hardest political conversations for a product marketer to hold.
> In fact, even seasoned CMOs struggle to make a tight business case for why you should niche your primary ICP.
> …more
> GTM Playbook
> GTM Playbook
> 274 followers
> 274 followers
> 3w •
> 3 weeks ago • Visible to anyone on or off LinkedIn
> Most PMMs describe their ICP by job title and company size.
> But That's not an ICP; that's a demographic.
> Your ICP should tell you:
> → What problem this person is actively trying to solve
> → What makes them ready to buy now, not eventually
> → What signals indicate they'll get value fast
> → Who the internal champion is vs who controls budget
> If you can't answer those four questions, your targeting is guesswork. And guesswork shows up as long sales cycles and high churn.
> The practical version: interview 10 customers. Ask them to walk you through the 30 days before they first started looking for a solution.
> You'll find two or three trigger events that account for 80% of your buyers.
> Those triggers ARE your ICP criteria. Full stop.
> Build your qualification model around trigger events, not job titles. Your pipeline quality will change within a quarter.
> We broke down the full ICP prioritisation framework at the link below - scoring methodology, the trigger-based model, and how to pressure-test your ICP against real pipeline data.

## 70. Sourav Ghosh — 3w

> Product Marketing for B2B SaaS | GTM, PLG and Sales Intelligence

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440623415725727744/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Your PMM team is not a production department.
> I watched a PMM team at a B2B SaaS company get reduced to an assembly line. The VP of Product decided the positioning. Leadership locked in the narrative. And then someone walked over to the PMMs and said, "Hey, we need a one-pager by Thursday. And three videos by next week."
> No product training. No ICP context. No explanation of why the feature existed or who it was for. Just a deadline and a template.
> Then, when the assets came back, the feedback was: "Why did you use 'helped' instead of 'enabled'?"
> I'm sorry, what?
> You gave a team zero context about the buyer, zero input into the narrative, and zero time to ask questions because everything was URGENT. And now we're arguing about a synonym?
> That's not a PMM problem. That's an environment problem.
> Here's what happens when PMM only gets invited to the execution phase: the assets look generic because they are. The PMM team burns out because they're being evaluated on output quality while being denied the inputs that quality depends on. And leadership concludes that "PMM doesn't get it" when the real issue is that PMM was never given anything to get.
> PMMs have a responsibility here too. We have to push for a seat earlier in the process. We have to ask the uncomfortable questions even when the room wants us to just take the brief and go build slides.
> But leadership carries the bigger weight here. If your PMM is new to the product, new to the market, or new to the team, they will default to whatever inputs they're given. If those inputs are a Slack message that says "make a one-pager, it's urgent," that's exactly the quality of thinking you'll get back. You hired a strategist. Train them like one. Give them product context, buyer access, and room to push back. Otherwise you're not building a PMM function. You're running a copy shop with a fancier title.
> Stop treating PMM like a content vending machine. Start treating it like the strategic function it was hired to be.
> …more

## 71. Tamara Grominsky — 3w

> ✨ PMM Leadership Coach | 2X VP PMM | I help product marketers build leadership range

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440303776676085760/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> If you can't explain why you're doing something, you probably shouldn't be doing it.
> Most PMMs are busy. But busy doing what?
> When leadership asks about your impact, you shouldn't have to scramble to connect the dots. The connection should already be there.
> My approach 👉 Start with what matters to the business and work backwards.
> What are the company's goals this quarter? Where is leadership focused? Answer those questions and then figure out how PMM can support that.
> One way I like to think about this is through the lens of a classic funnel: Acquisition, Activation, Retention, Referral, Revenue.
> Even if leadership hasn't handed you perfectly crafted goals, they're almost certainly focused on at least one of these stages.
> Locate the business, then locate yourself.
> Once you know what stage matters most, the next step is figuring out how PMM can best support that goal. And this is where most PMMs stop too early. They jump straight to a list of tactics without thinking through the why. And then they wonder why leadership doesn't see their work as strategic.
> Take acquisition, as an example.
> If acquisition matters to the business, your job is to help attract the right buyers. But before you start building personas or rewriting website copy, ask yourself:
> → Are we actually targeting the right segments?
> → Is our messaging landing with those buyers?
> → Are we differentiated in a way that matters to them?
> → Are we showing up where they're looking?
> These questions shape your mandate. They help you figure out where PMM can have the most impact, and they give you a clear focus for every initiative you prioritize.
> From there, the work starts to take shape. But it all starts with the strategy, not the tactics.
> Everything you do should map back to the business. And you should be able to explain why.
> It's as simple as that.
> Where does your team focus most of its energy right now, and is that intentional, or just where you ended up?
> _____
> 👋🏻 I'm Tamara, and I help product marketers build leadership range.
> 💌 Want my Sunday strategy sessions? Join 9,000+ PMMs who get the PMM Camp newsletter every week. Subscribe for free: https://lnkd.in/g3KdUBg9

## 72. Yi Lin Pei — 3w

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440253359690952704/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Some days we just need to get back to basics. Lately, I’ve been seeing “narrative,” “positioning,” and “messaging” used interchangeably. Here is how I explain their difference:
> 🎯 Positioning is your strategic foundation. It's how your product fits in the market: who it's for, what it does, why it's different, and why it matters. Think of it as the internal logic that guides everything else.
> 💬 Messaging is how you communicate your positioning. It's the language, themes, and key points (value pillars, benefits, capabilities, and features) you use to convey your value to customers.
> 🖋️ Copy is the execution. It's the actual headlines, body text, CTAs, and specific language that show up on your website, in emails, in ads. Copy brings messaging to life and can be clever, punchy, or creative.  Usually, a copywriter does this (please don't just leave this to AI). 😔
> 🎬 Narrative is the story ARC that connects your positioning across touchpoints. It's how you guide customers through understanding your value over time, from first awareness to deep conviction. Narrative isn’t just “better messaging, because it takes customers through a journey from I don't care to I believe in this. It connects everything across touchpoints into a cohesive progression.
> You can think of these as layers of the onion with positioning in the middle – and if the center is not right, then everything falls apart.
> In terms of order of operations, I tend to start from the inner core to the outer layer, so everything is rooted and grounded in your truth- what makes you unique, special, that attracts customers to buy and stay.
> How do you interpret these terms and bring them to life?
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #positioning

> #growth
> hashtag
> #coaching

## 74. Nemanja Jovančić — 3w

> Growth @ Sendcloud | Product Marketing | 8+ years in B2B SaaS

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7440051620337532928/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> PM vs. PMM: who owns the changelog?
> In our last product launch, we talked about ~50 product updates shipped over two quarters. But only 25% of those made it to the changelog.
> Not because they weren’t worth sharing, but because ownership was unclear and the process was lacking.
> At the same time, every product update was communicated internally. Product Managers posted each release in a dedicated Slack channel, with plenty of context on what has changed, how it works, and why it matters.
> One step was missing: turning those internal updates into public changelog entries.
> Previously this sat with Product Marketing, more recently with PM. In both cases, it wasn’t a top priority, so the changelog wasn't regularly updated.
> To solve this, I built an automation:
> → PM posts in
> hashtag
> #product-update as usual
> → n8n triggers a workflow on every new message
> → AI reads the message and checks if the update is public and user-facing
> → If yes, it drafts a changelog entry directly in Canny (following the PMM guidelines)
> → The PM gets notified to review and publish
> The AI doesn’t publish anything (though it could and probably eventually will). PMs still own it and review every entry before it goes live. But what used to take 30 minutes (and mostly didn’t happen at all) can now be done in under a minute.
> We chose Slack updates as the source because it’s the one consistent thing all our PMs already do when they ship something. But this can work with any repeatable, predictable, context-rich source, like product briefs, Jira tickets, etc.
> Why this matters beyond the obvious:
> - The changelog already has solid reach via our newsletter, website, and platform
> - It’s a lightweight way to share smaller improvements and incremental updates without spamming users
> - Sales and CSM can now point customers to a living record of what’s new
> - Consistent updates send a signal to the market that we’re continuously improving and innovating
> We’re now keeping an eye on adoption (PMs still need to hit 'publish') and improving the flow as we go, with the goal of getting 100% of public-facing product updates into the changelog automatically.
> P.S. This AI automation replaced 0 product marketers and 0 product managers.
> P.P.S. Happy to share how it's built, let me know if you're interested!

## 78. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 1mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7439342409311821824/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I went from Claude Code skeptic to addict in 63 days.
> Here's how I went about it for my marketing use cases >
> Initially, I found it pointless. Claude Code (CC) felt like a redundant, geeky version of Claude Desktop.
> But ngl, my LinkedIn feed triggered a lot of FOMO, so I persisted and dug deeper.
> One tip would be to learn at your own pace. I took my time learning one aspect a day.
> It grew on me when I got the hang of these:
> [1] Creating an MCP layer to access tools
> For me, Claude Code is only as good as the data it can access.
> I spent the initial weeks setting up MCPs for my tech stack. Google Ads specifically took me a long time.
> Once I hooked up Google Search Console, Hubspot, Calendar, Granola, SEMRush, and Slack, I saw a lot fewer context switches.
> My favorite play was to cross-pollinate data between tools.
> e.g. find my next meeting with X team, reference my Granola notes for what I need to follow up on and get data from Hubspot to discuss.
> [2] Skills
> I was writing out the same prompts repeatedly. But I also needed something to execute code with context. That's when Skills made a lot of sense.
> I built slash commands for my go-to activities e.g., pulling the top 10 performing ad groups or generating a candidate summary for an interview.
> Tip: add to Claude.md a line telling CC to proactively suggest Skills I can create based on my usage.
> I then got Claude to optimize its own folder structure into Context, Data, Output, Agents, and Skills.
> [3] Parallelism
> I eventually built a highly customized skill for my marketing audit, which used 6 tools. The problem? The context window would max out.
> That's when I understood why I needed subagents.
> The breakthrough was asking Claude to FIRST create an "Orchestrator" agent and then invoke subagents, each armed with one specific skill.
> Classic use case: competitive analysis with each subagent handling one competitor.
> [4] Context sponge
> After weeks of prompting and sharing docs, CC knew my strategy, customer profiles, and even my insecurities inside out. Context truly compounds.
> It funnels new highlights into its brain file (Claude.md) and sub-folder files. Now, when I prompt, I don't need to explain 100 things first. It gets it. It delivers.
> It’s not perfect, though.
> The setup can get technical. Sometimes, im just saying yes to cryptic syntax.The terminal interface isn’t for everyone (I’m still a visual guy at heart), and granting permissions constantly is a chore.
> But the paradigm shift is real. I've stopped using other LLMs now and do all my AI stuff in CC now.
> In case you're interested in starting, these are the tutorials I found handy:
> How to Automate Your Work (Beginner Breakdown): https://lnkd.in/dxM6ATSK
> Carl Velotti’s Tutorials:
> https://lnkd.in/dcDb8a27
> Building Revenue-Generating Skills:
> https://lnkd.in/d7HKK5Zk
> What use cases are you implementing?

## 82. Sebastien Rankin — 4w

> Product Marketing @ Shopify | GTM Strategy | Positioning & Messaging | Growth Marketing

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7439007609187360769/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Marketing is a team sport, but most AI workflows I see are built for solo players. We need a better way to connect everyone to the team's playbook.
> The pattern I keep seeing is that everyone on the GTM team uploads their own context, writes their own prompts, builds their own agents, and stores their own rules locally. They're individually faster, but collectively misaligned.
> I found the fix isn't another off-the-shelf tool. It's integrating AI into *how* teams work—adapting their workflows and operating model using tools most tech companies already have.
> That starts with treating marketing strategy like engineers treat a codebase. One source of truth, version-controlled, that every teammate's AI tool of choice pulls from. Positioning, personas, messaging, competitive intel—all your marketing context in one place, underpinning everyone's work.
> When campaign managers create a brief, messaging reflects what makes buyers tick. When Creative works with AI for ad copy, it's grounded in the campaign positioning. When Sales builds a one-pager, it references up-to-date battlecards and competitive intel. Less time digging for context, higher fidelity work, and everyone moves faster.
> The real impact is how the system compounds. The knowledge base adapts and keeps the whole team current with every transcript processed, every positioning update, every product launch, and every competitive shift you document. In fast-moving orgs where context changes constantly, that's worth more than any individual productivity gain.
> I built this at Shopify over a year ago and it changed how our GTM team works async, so I built a free, company-agnostic version that works with any AI tool that can read files. All you need is a GitHub account and either Cursor or Claude Code to get started.
> Check out Product Marketing OS here: https://bit.ly/3P1KA0A

## 83. Tas Bober — 1mo

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7439006573697417216/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> When I was in-house, I spent half my life justifying every dollar.
> Every time I wanted to buy a tool or hire an agency, I had to build a business case. I used a simple five-slide deck to get the "yes" I needed.
> It had:
> - The goal and the problem with achieving it
> - The options (including doing nothing)
> - The cost, resources, and stakeholders involved
> - The implementation and timeline
> But gathering that data was like pulling teeth.
> The vendors I was talking to made me do all the heavy lifting.
> I had to dig for pricing
> I had to hunt for proof
> I had to figure out the implementation process.
> I used to think, "If this info were just on the website, I could have closed this deal weeks ago."
> Now, I build landing pages based on that exact frustration.
> I call it the Core Four strategy: 4 pages that do the internal selling for your buyer:
> 1) The Overview (Education-focused)
> Usually, the first touch page covering what you do, who you're for, the problem you identified in the market, how you solve it, proof of solving it for others, common objections, and what to expect in the sales process.
> 2) The Comparison
> This is a page that directly compares you vs. alternatives (not just competitors but also the cost of doing nothing). 60% of deals are lost to inaction. You need to cover those risks.
> 3) The Reviews
> You control the narrative here instead of leaving it up to G2 and Reddit to tell the story. This is a consolidation of proof - both ones provided directly to you and ones from agnostic sources. A complete voice of the customer page to solidify credibility.
> 4) The Demo
> Finally, we make the ask. This is the high-intent step that clearly outlines what happens next, so there's no mystery to the buyer.
> ---
> B2B buyers are researchers. In fact, 70% of their research is done before ever engaging with you.
> So you need to be a guide, not a seller.
> Give them the information they need to build their own business case.
> The easier you make it for them to get internal approval, the faster your deal closes.
> I cover this and more on the latest episode of Closing Time with Valerie Riley, VP of Marketing at Unbounce.
> Catch it wherever you get your podcasts.
> PS: Unbounce and I are partnering to release templates for each of these pages over the next few months.
> Check out the toolkit: https://bit.ly/4ssahFU
> If you sign up, you'll get notified as we release new templates.

## 87. Rabi Shanker Guha — 4w

> Building thesys | Ex-Google

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7437768572652802048/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> 3x faster. 67% fewer tokens. Dramatically more reliable
> This is what happened when we stopped fighting how LLMs think.
> 10,000+ developers used our API to build AI-generated interfaces over the past year. We've spent the last year shipping AI-generated interfaces to production at scale. It worked. Until it didn't.
> Three problems kept showing up:
> - rendering was too slow
> - custom designs were hard to integrate
> - models kept producing broken output.
> We added fixes, wrote better prompts. It got better but the problem didn't go away.
> We eventually realised these weren't bugs. They were symptoms of a deeper issue. The format we were using to describe UI just wasn't a good fit for how AI models work.
> Projects like Vercel's json-render & Google A2UI showed that separating structure from design was the right idea. But JSON itself was the bottleneck. So we built a format that works the way models actually think.
> The results: up to 3x faster rendering, 67% fewer tokens, and far fewer errors. One format change addressed all three problems. And today we’re open sourcing the entire thing.
> Not a component library. Not a chatbot wrapper. Infrastructure - the kind that makes AI products feel like software.
> Open source. Free. Built from everything we learnt the hard way.
> hashtag

## 88. Yael Davidowitz-Neu — 1mo

> Director of Product Marketing | AI Infrastructure, Developer Platforms, and Enterprise GTM | ex-Google

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7437768163125084161/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> People say product marketing is having a moment. Is it?
> Over the past few months, what I’m hearing from PMMs across the field sounds more nuanced than that. Junior PMMs talk about how difficult it is to break into the function at all, while senior PMMs describe a market with fewer roles and hiring processes that can stretch for months. And yet the narrative around the function has never been stronger. Founders talk constantly about positioning, category design, and company narrative as strategic advantages and product marketing communities, events, and adjacent services around the discipline continue to grow.
> So the signals from the market feel contradictory.
> My sense is we’re seeing three shifts at once.
> First, the function really is being elevated. Product marketing used to sit closer to launch management and product messaging. Now the expectation is much bigger: shaping the company narrative, defining categories, aligning product and sales, and translating complex technology into market understanding. That’s executive-level work, which is why we’re seeing more VP Product Marketing roles appear.
> Second, teams are being consolidated. Instead of building large PMM organizations, many companies hire one very senior operator responsible for narrative, positioning, launches, and GTM alignment across the company. When the role carries that much weight, hiring slows down and expectations increase.
> Third, an ecosystem is forming around the discipline. As positioning, narrative, and category framing become more central to how companies compete, a broader market is emerging around product marketing itself. More consultants are offering messaging and positioning work, communities around PMM continue to grow, and even specialized recruiting practices focused on product marketing are starting to appear.
> In other words, the influence of product marketing seems to be expanding even as the number of in-house roles contracts.
> Curious what others are seeing in the market.

## 89. James Doman-Pipe — 1mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7437146511609745408/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Stop selling features, and start selling outcomes - with a Value Story 🚀
> Most marketing fails because it focuses too much on what the product is, and not enough on WHY the customer would use it.
> To fix this, you need to shift from feature-focused messaging, to telling a Value Story.
> I define true value as something that is:
> ✅ Useful
> ✅ Worth the effort (cost, time, energy)
> ✅ A priority for the customer
> If you hit all three, you sell faster - and fight commoditization.
> The Framework? A Value Story.
> Craft a narrative that builds genuine empathy and urgency:
> Situation:
> "Today, our customer is..."
> (Setting the context)
> Problem:
> "However..."
> (Identifying the blocker preventing advancement)
> Implication:
> "This means..."
> (Uncovering the pain and lost opportunity cost)
> Resolution:
> "By..."
> (Fixing the problem - focus on the how, not the product)
> Value:
> "...our customer will..."
> (Detailing the ultimate, tangible gain)
> This works!
> Value isn't something you "make up" in a brainstorm.
> Value must be be discovered through broad-to-narrow customer interviews.
> When you understand the Implication (the cost of doing nothing), you move beyond being a "nice to have" to becoming a "must-have".
> Stop pitching features.
> Start telling the story of how your customer wins. 🏆

## 90. Kevin White — 1mo

> Marketing @ Scrunch | Advisor to SaaS Startups | fmr Growth & Marketing @Segment @Retool @Common Room

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7436995323069353985/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> If you’ve got analysis paralysis about using agents (think Claude Code, Cowork, OpenClaw), here are 10 jobs I started with.
> Caveat: Most of these require a connector to your CRM (HubSpot in my case) and/or a call transcript tool (Grain in my case).
> Okay, here’s 🔟 places to start:
> Find job titles for LI targeting
> ⤷ Have your agent go through won deals and opptys to extract job titles. Bonus tip: group by industry, size (e.g., ENT vs MM), etc.
> Pain finder
> ⤷ Crawl through call transcripts to find patterns in prospect pain points. Then use them in your copy as a hook.
> Objection finder
> ⤷ Crawl through call transcripts and find the objections that come up most often. Then address them upfront or in an FAQ on your site.
> Aha finder
> ⤷ Crawl through call transcripts and identify the “aha moments” (“I need my team to see this!”).
> Oppty attribution cleanup
> ⤷ Go through every oppty with a blank source, sift through activity logs, identify the source, and populate it. Build reports from it. (I wrote about this earlier in the week if you want to peep my last post.)
> Event location finder
> ⤷ Search the headquarters and contact locations of your top accounts and build an event roadmap in regions with the highest concentration. Host your next in-person event there.
> DQ analysis
> ⤷ Search through demo requests and find patterns in those that don’t turn into opptys (usually personal emails or small company size). Then send an auto-qualification email when a new hand-raiser isn’t worth the time.
> Funnel analysis
> ⤷ Identify bottlenecks in the sales funnel and diagnose what’s clogged.
> Truthful closed/lost analysis
> ⤷ Go through the paper trail and call transcripts of closed/lost deals and read between the lines to pinpoint the REAL reason for the “thanks, but no thanks.”
> Quote finder
> ⤷ Sift through CS call transcripts and surface quotes from customers to use in marketing materials (with permission, ofc).
> =====
> To get started, the most important thing is just that—get started.
> Pick a use case above and run with it. Once you start, it has a snowball effect of bringing on new ideas.

## 94. Michele Nieberding 🚀 — 1mo

> The AI Product Marketer

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7434483320534679552/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> As many of you know, I've made the "official" switch to Claude and haven't looked back. Because of that, I've also been getting the same question: "How do you actually use Claude tools in real PMM work?”
> So here’s a practical recap of what each is best for + when to use it 👇
> Claude — the Q&A UI most of you use now. Think: strategic sounding board. Pressure-test ideas before they go live.
> Best uses:
> • Messaging & positioning refinement
> • Persona development
> • Launch narrative drafts
> • Competitive analysis (light research)
> • Turning complex features into clear value stories
> When:
> ➡️ Early-stage thinking & strategy
> ➡️ Blank page moments
> ➡️ Need clarity before execution
> Claude Code — great when working with engineering/dev. It’s a CLI (command-line interface) version of Claude running locally. Instead of chatting in a browser, you work directly with files, folders, datasets & code on your machine. Think systems-level work vs content gen.
> Best uses:
> • Analyze CSVs/raw data
> • Pull insights from reviews or feature lists
> • Structure messy product inputs
> • Build lightweight prototypes/calculators
> • Translate technical docs into GTM insights
> When:
> ✔️ Working with lots of files/data
> ✔️ Task feels too technical for chat
> ✔️ Need structured or repeatable output
> ✔️ You’d normally ask eng/data for help
> 🧩 Claude Skills — reusable AI workflows. Define a repeatable PMM task once & reuse it. Basically mini playbooks the AI follows.
> Best uses:
> • Repeatable launch messaging workflows
> • Battlecards
> • Feature → benefit conversion
> • Persona/vertical messaging
> • PR or enablement starters
> • Packaging technical inputs into GTM language
> When:
> ✔️ You’re repeating the same task
> ✔️ You want consistency across launches
> ✔️ Scaling messaging across teams
> ✔️ Helping junior PMMs produce stronger drafts
> 🤝 Claude Cowork — less repeatability, more ongoing collaboration. If Skills are templates, Cowork is a teammate. It keeps context, remembers progress & helps you iterate over time.
> Best uses:
> • Living positioning docs
> • Ongoing competitive monitoring
> • Messaging iteration across launches
> • Long-running GTM projects
> • Strategy refinement as context evolves
> When:
> ✔️ Work evolves over weeks/months
> ✔️ Context matters session to session
> ✔️ Continuous strategy refinement
> ✔️ Multi-stakeholder collaboration
> TL;DR
> • Claude → think
> • Claude Code → analyze/code
> • Claude Skills → scale (consistent outputs)
> • Claude Cowork → collaborate
> Curious how other PMMs are integrating these into launches, competitive intel, or messaging workflows 👀

## 95. James Doman-Pipe — 1mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7434483077403508736/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> I've been playing with Openclaw, Claude Code, Codex, and every other AI agent that surfaces on my timeline .... and I keep coming back to the same uncomfortable question.
> If anyone - from the CEO to a day one customer support rep - can now build a feature in minutes, what happens to the product marketer?
> The traditional PMM role is built around imperfect information.
> You're the person who knows the product deeply enough to translate it for buyers.
> You coordinate the launch.
> You write the brief.
> You make sure sales, support, and marketing are aligned before the button gets pressed.
> You create the tools and enablement that drive the launch.
> But that model assumes features take months to build... that there's time for a proper launch cycle. That engineering is the bottleneck.
> None of that is true anymore.
> When a founder can ship a feature over the weekend because they asked Claude to build it, the whole flow breaks.
> There's no planning window and no no handoff and no launch brief and no user research.
> And by the time any of that is done, three more features have shipped.
> So PMMs face a choice.
> Option one: try to keep up.
> Sprint faster, process faster, turn launches around in days instead of weeks. This is a race you lose. You can't process faster than AI can build.
> Option two: change what you're doing entirely.
> The PMM job in an AI-first world isn't to process launches.
> It's to curate them. To answer one question: which of these features actually deserves a story?
> Not everything that ships deserves a launch. Most of it should go in a changelog.
> But some launches really move the narrative: they change how buyers think, give sales a new conversation, reframe the category, change behavior.
> Those need the full treatment: positioning, enablement, customer marketing, the works.
> The PMM who survives the incoming AI onslaught isn't the one who can write faster. It's the one who knows what's worth a story and what belongs in a changelog
> That's a harder job, and much more valuable.
> …more

> And it’s because we’re running two engines at once…
> Here’s the clean way to think about it 👇
> Inbound PMM = shape the business
> → Market trends + competitive intel
> → Voice of customer + win/loss
> → Segmentation + ICP clarity
> → Packaging and positioning
> → Influencing roadmap + strategy
