> → GTM planning + launch orchestration
> → Messaging + campaigns
> → Sales enablement + demos
> → Adoption, expansion, upsell
> This is downstream work, you’re turning strategy into pipeline and growth.
> You’ll be embedded with Sales, Demand Gen, and Revenue teams.
> —
> Let’s be honest, most PMMs are juggling both. But knowing where you lean matters.
> Because it shapes:
> → The skills you double down on
> → The stakeholders and connections you make
> → The career path you design
> Inbound builds clarity. Outbound builds momentum.
> Thank you Tamara Grominsky for this amazing breakdown!

## 97. Sourav Mohanty — 1mo

> Not yet a Product Marketing Leader | B2B Positioning & Messaging | ARR is not my son but I’ll raise it

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7434483009837477888/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Last post I talked about how AI compresses your interviews and why the output always looks more confident than it should.
> Here are the four things that changed how I approach this (1/2)
> ─
> 1. A quote is only as good as the instruction that produced it
> Most people ask AI for quotes and assume "verbatim" means what it means to them. It doesn't. AI fills every gap with its own judgment
> Where the thought begins, where it ends, whether the hedge in the middle stays or gets trimmed, whether two sentences from different parts of the answer get stitched together because they make a cleaner point.
> None of that is visible in the output. What you see is a quote in quotation marks with a name attached.
> It looks sourced. It reads confidently. It may have never existed as a single sentence.
> The shift: before you ask for quotes, decide what you'd need to see to trust them in a positioning review. Not "verbatim" — that's too vague. Something specific enough that AI can't fill in the gaps without you noticing.
> Lock the constraints: copy one uninterrupted customer turn only, no merging or cleanup, preserve hedges/fillers, include speaker label + timestamp + the 2 sentences before and after — if any word is added, removed, or reordered - mention the change. explictly"
> ─
> 2. The frame you give AI becomes the filter everything gets read through
> Two PMMs. Same product. Same batch of transcripts.
> One is looking for churn signals. One is building the case for a new feature.
> They get back completely different insights from identical data.
> This isn't a quirk. It's how AI works. It doesn't read your transcripts neutrally.
> It reads them relative to the job you've given it. And the job most prompts give it is far too open-ended to produce anything useful.
> The frame that actually changes the output isn't about background. It's about stakes.
> Tell AI what decision this research has to inform.
> Tell it what you're trying to prove and what would disprove it.
> Tell it who each participant is and why that matters
> because a churned competitor migrator and a loyal power user saying the same words are telling you opposite things, and AI has no way to know that unless you do.
> When AI knows what it's deciding, it reads the data differently.
> When it doesn't, it summarises it. And a summary is not an answer.
> ─
> (1/2)

## 99. Natália Kimličková — 2mo

> Product Marketing @Userpilot

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7434231045195046912/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> I spent 5 years using Userpilot as a customer before joining the team as a PMM.
> In my previous role, we faced what every PLG team faces: how do you guide self-serve users to their "aha" moment before they churn? We solved it with Userpilot and lifted activation by 10% in under 2 months.
> Here’s what we did:
> 1. Mapped activation points (the setup moments users must complete to feel value) and correlated them to free-to-paid conversions
> 2. Built a welcome modal with a CTA that nudges users straight into the first action.
> 3. Added an onboarding checklist where every item launches an interactive walkthrough.
> 4. Set up progressive nudges based on behavior (so users get the right prompt when they’re actually ready).
> 5. Connected onboarding emails to those activation points to pull users back in outside the app. We used to run this through Intercom, and now I can do it directly in Userpilot (in-app + email, triggered by real-time behavior and tied to product metrics 🙌)
> 6. Measured impact in the Activation dashboard + used path reports to see if users follow the intended journey.
> 7. Watched session replays to see where users hesitate, miss steps, or ignore onboarding. We used to rely on Hotjar for this, and now it’s built into Userpilot too!
> A successful onboarding strategy is about connecting Analytics → Engagement (in-app + email) → Session replay + feedback → fast iteration (without waiting on dev cycles). Bonus points if all of this can be done from one platform.
> If improving activation is on your PMM roadmap for 2026, let's talk! Book a 30-minute call with me: https://shorturl.at/1sSBI

> But here’s the irony - as the entry barriers to shipping software are getting lower and lower with Lovables of the world (and the competition is getting more and more cutthroat) ease of use and Time to Value are the strongest factors in determining whether someone will continue using your product, or start shopping around again at the slightest hint of friction. > 👉 Yes, you could hard-code your onboarding experiences. But you know you won’t.
> 👉 Yes, you can use the legacy product adoption platforms…but that’s the modality of the word ‘can’. If you can’t ship what you need in < a few hours without developers’ / designers’ help - you won’t.
> 👉 You could buy 5 different solutions for lifecycle email, in-app communications, and product analytics - but you know the data will be falling through the cracks and you don’t use it consistently between the different tools. Your use communication will be broken.

> I created an AI agent on Agent .ai that turns blog posts into Avatar videos.
> The problem I'm trying to solve: I write a lot of content, but most of it reaches only a subset of its potential audience because many people prefer video.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7432447580854317056/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I’m four months into a new role and leaning into the fundamentals that build a high-impact, scalable product marketing function.
> That comes back to three things: strong partnership with Product and GTM teams, clear positioning that translates capability into commercial value, and simple process that helps us move faster and make better decisions.
> One practical tool I dusted off recently (and am rolling out with the new team) is an Intake Interview Guide to kick off a conversation between Product Management and Product Marketing. It’s designed to quickly extract the critical information PMM needs to do the marketing work well and orchestrate effective GTM.
> Before we build messaging, launch plans, or enablement, we use the Intake tool get clarity on the things that matter most to shape our approach and investment — the real customer problem, what’s truly launch-ready, who will care first, and what success looks like. It consistently surfaces assumptions, exposes unknowns, and gives us the inputs we need to make smart GTM decisions early.
> A lightweight version of the structure is a series of questions about:
> Launch timing & key milestones
> Top commercial use cases at release
> Scope boundaries & known limitations
> Early adopter hypothesis
> Success criteria for the first release
> Launch recommendation (soft rollout vs. broad activation)
> It’s obvious we need this information; the interview approach is easy - it creates alignment quickly and sets up the shared knowledge and foundation for great positioning, messaging, and launch plans. Share it in advance and use it in confidence - it's simple, and it works!

## 106. Victoria Penker — 1mo

> Head of Product Marketing | GTM Strategy | Positioning & Messaging | Product Launches | B2B SaaS | AI | HealthTech

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7432447120520994816/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> There is no such thing as a “GTM strategy.”
> As Product Marketing Managers, “GTM” is one of those words that follows us everywhere we go.
> It can be found in every job description.
> Sneaks into every PMM assignment.
> Every strategy meeting.
> Every launch conversation.
> “(Just) build the GTM.”
> “Refine the GTM.”
> “Vicky, what’s our GTM strategy?”, I’ve been asked time and time again.
> As if it’s a single, static strategy you can neatly construct out of fairy dust and hand over to your boss.
> In reality, GTM is a stack of smaller, interdependent strategies that together form an ecosystem.
> Just like you don’t build a product in isolation, you don’t construct one monolithic GTM plan.
> You layer it. Then you interconnect it:
> – A content strategy to solve messaging and positioning, supporting both your colleagues in Sales and other marketing functions.
> – A brand strategy to define the voice and close the awareness gap that inevitably exists when the narrative isn’t sharp yet.
> – A partner or distribution strategy to leverage someone else’s reach and customer trust.
> – A focused lead generation strategy.
> The mistake isn’t choosing the “wrong” strategy.
> It’s getting lost pretending that one of them is enough — or sharp enough — to carry everything.
> When you're asked to “build the GTM,” what are you actually being asked to fix?
> hashtag
> #ProductMarketing
> hashtag
> #GoToMarket
> hashtag
> #GTM
> hashtag
> #Strategy
> hashtag
> #PMM

## 108. Mariana Racasan — 1mo

> 3x founding PMM in B2B SaaS | Most first-time PMMs get ignored or fired, I help you become neither.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7432446726629752833/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I've been the first PMM hire at multiple companies. And I'll be honest - proving value when everything needs to be built from scratch? Brutal.
> You're expected to deliver immediate results while building long-term foundation.
> Most days it feels impossible. But here's what I learned:
> The secret isn't choosing between foundation work OR immediate impact. It's doing both strategically.
> Here's the 30-60-90 framework that's worked for me:
> 🎯 First 30 days: Listen & Quick wins
> • Interview 5-8 customers (yes, even before you "know the product")
> • Audit existing sales materials and identify the biggest gaps
> • Create ONE piece of sales enablement that solves an immediate pain point
> • Document what you're hearing everywhere - this becomes your foundation
> 🏗️ Days 31-60: Build the foundation
> • Define your ICP based on actual customer conversations (not assumptions)
> • Create your core messaging framework - but keep it simple
> • Launch one small experiment to test messaging (new sales deck positioning slide, homepage value prop test)
> • Start the competitive research that will inform everything else
> 🚀 Days 61-90: Scale what works
> • Refine messaging based on your experiment results
> • Build repeatable processes for launches, sales enablement, customer research
> • Create the documentation that will help the next hire get up to speed fast
> • Measure and communicate your impact (pipeline influence, conversion rates, win rate by segment)
> The magic happens when your "quick wins" feed into your foundation work, and your foundation work amplifies your tactical execution.
> What's worked for you in your first 90 days as a PMM? Share your survival tips - other first-time PMMs need to hear them.

## 109. Aniket Parihar — 1mo

> Senior PM and ex-founder who ships.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7432355432788131840/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> I can't code, but my project Claude Spend now has 104 stars on GitHub, 19 forks, and 7 PRs. I didn't have a GitHub account until January this year.
> Last week, I built an analytics tool that helps you understand your Claude Code usage. It got a lot of attention. But what I am proud of is getting support from engineers.
> As a product manager, I have worked very closely with enggs. I believe they are one of the smartest species on earth. And it's mainly because of their strong logical reasoning background. Building software at scale definitely requires a whole different set of expertise. And they are the ones who have that.
> This is why, as a non-coder, getting applause from engineers is amazing to say the least.
> But here's another thing I discovered with these GitHub stars.
> As a non-coder, I can now:
> A) Build a small piece of software using AI-assisted tools that users actually use (my LinkedIn and Reddit posts are proof).
> B) The code generated by these tools is understandable enough that other engineers can contribute to it.
> I understand the tool is rather small in terms of codebase, but considering we are at the start, I believe this is a turning point in the software industry.
> If you are a PM or a non-coder building tools, I highly recommend asking all the questions about architecture, scalability, security, and other aspects of the software product you are building. Understand the concepts and go deep in the rabbit hole. Here's an example:
> Claude Spend uses Express. It's a JavaScript package my tool uses to serve data to my browser (all those metrics on the dashboard). But when I learned that Express replaces about 100 lines of low-level networking code with 3 lines, I asked Claude Code to explain how.
> The answer is abstraction. Someone writes 100 lines once, packages it up, and now everyone else writes 3 lines forever. And this is how ALL software works. Express is built on Node.js. Node.js is built on C++. C++ talks to your operating system. Your operating system talks to actual electrical signals in your hardware.
> Every layer hides the complexity of the one below it. When you type "npx claude-spend" you are standing on top of decades of layers that other engineers built. You don't need to understand TCP/IP or memory management to ship a working tool. You just use the top layer.
> I believe this understanding makes me a better product manager in the AI world. When an engineer says "it's complicated", I can ask the right questions and understand the why.
> And it also helps me to find better ideas to build as a product builder/vibe-coder instead of the small, cute little ones.
> Thank you, engineers, for all of your work.
> My Github link: https://lnkd.in/dCnAaywh

## 111. Corey Hearne — 1mo

> Founder, Rung | Marketing Consultant

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430811095142977536/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> One of the hardest transitions in marketing that nobody prepares you for:
> Marketing Manager to Head of Marketing.
> You can be brilliant at managing marketing. Running campaigns, owning channels, delivering results.
> But managing a marketing function is a completely different job.
> When I made the jump, I went from being a one-man band to having people report into me. The dynamic between colleagues changes instantly. The people you worked alongside now rely on you to direct their work.
> The hands-on work you loved usually gets replaced by strategic work. Which sounds great, until you realise nobody teaches you how to be strategic within your company. There’s no playbook. You learn it on the job, especially if you’re the first Head of Marketing and you’re building the function from scratch.
> You’re now the person setting direction. Setting reporting, deciding what the team focuses on, communicating your decisions, how they affect the business, what the expected outcomes are and why we’re doing them, etc. etc.
> And then the weight hits.
> For the first time, the entire function sits on your shoulders. People are relying on you for feedback. For decisions that affect their careers and the business.
> It can be lonely and the responsibility shifts in a way nobody warns you about.
> If you’re a Marketing Manager thinking about making the jump, it’s worth it. The job changes more than you expect, you’re out of your comfort zone, but that’s where we grow, right?
> And if you’ve already made the jump and you’re feeling the weight, you’re not doing it wrong.
> That’s just what it feels like.

## 116. James Doman-Pipe — 1mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430720363430440960/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> 1️⃣2️⃣3️⃣4️⃣ quick positioning mistakes I've made and STRONGLY advise you not to repeat ❌
> 1️⃣ Mistake 1: Consensus Positioning
> "Let's workshop this with the whole company."
> No.
> Positioning is a strategic decision, not a democratic vote.
> The CEO decides. Everyone else aligns.
> --
> 2️⃣ Mistake 2: Positioning by Aspiration
> "We want to be known as the leader in [category]."
> Want is not strategy.
> Position based on evidence: who actually buys and why.
> --
> 3️⃣ Mistake 3: Repositioning Every Quarter
> Positioning takes 6-12 months to settle in the market.
> Dn't chase every trend.
> Commit for at least 2 quarters before pivoting.
> This is why you need to build CONFIDENCE in positioning as you build it, rather than ship and hope.
> --
> 4️⃣ Mistake 4: Ignoring What You Are Actually Good At
> You want to position as "enterprise-grade."
> But all your customers are SMBs who chose you for speed, not security.
> Position for who actually buys, not who you wish would buy.
> --
> Any other lessons you had to learn the hard way?

## 118. Ian Buchanan — 1mo

> Growth Marketing | Marketing Operations @ Winning by Design

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430135402348556288/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> Just a few weeks ago, the team at Winning by Design launched the NEW winningbydesign(dot)com...
> This was my first time leading a website overhaul of this scale — which basically means I learned a lot the hard way. Here are a few of the core lessons (and trust me, this is the shortened list).
> 1️⃣ Get painfully clear on the “why.” Before design. Before copy. Before anything.
> For us, it was simple (but not easy): We aim to be THE always-on go-to-market education platform — better highlighting our industry-leading resources, events, trainings, and solutions. Also, with the introduction of a refreshed suite of offerings, we needed our site to reflect a new customer journey, clearer product suite, SPICED-aligned messaging, and a modern, unified experience — while driving real conversion improvements.
> Once that was clear, decisions had a framework to run through.
> 2️⃣ Use AI as shared memory.
> One of the smartest things we did (eventually) was build a dedicated ChatGPT project and feed it our ICPs, personas, positioning, tone, and messaging — and keep updating it.
> It became a strategic partner.
> Not because it “wrote the site,” but because it kept us aligned (and helped with drafting copy, too).
> Building out the content of an entire website takes weeks to months, which can mean inconsistencies across pages and locations if a standard is not followed and maintained. AI helps immensely in alleviating these concerns.
> 3️⃣ Obsess over migration details.
> Design is fun. Preserving search equity is not — but it matters more.
> Working with an AMAZING partner in Zabal Media, we prioritized SEO and AEO from day one so we didn’t lose what we’d built over years of effort.
> 4️⃣ Trust the process.
> If you're looking for shortcuts, you better be sure nothing is missed. With so much to be considered in a successful launch plan, the long road — though it may be tedious — is the surest way to have peace of mind post-migration that all i's have been dotted and t's crossed.
> PRDs, lo-fi, hi-fi, development, content implementation, testing & review, optimization. All stages are equally important and take time to do right.
> 5️⃣ Over-communicate internally.
> Every update kept momentum alive. A website relaunch isn’t just a marketing project — it signals where the company is going.
> I wasn't always the best with this, but keeping a steady drumbeat and sharing exciting progress ensured top-level support was maintained (h/t to Shari Johnston for being our champion, rallying input, and maintaining support).
> The new winningbydesign(dot)com went live to the public on January 28, so because we’re still early, I need to hold off on declaring wins. But the initial engagement signals are encouraging.
> ↑ Traffic
> ↑ Active Visitors
> ↑ Conversions
> If you’ve done a relaunch before — what would you tell me to watch out for next?

## 119. Ian Buchanan — 1mo

> Growth Marketing | Marketing Operations @ Winning by Design

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430135348166348800/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lov**

> Just a few weeks ago, the team at Winning by Design launched the NEW winningbydesign(dot)com...
> This was my first time leading a website overhaul of this scale — which basically means I learned a lot the hard way. Here are a few of the core lessons (and trust me, this is the shortened list).
> 1️⃣ Get painfully clear on the “why.” Before design. Before copy. Before anything.
> For us, it was simple (but not easy): We aim to be THE always-on go-to-market education platform — better highlighting our industry-leading resources, events, trainings, and solutions. Also, with the introduction of a refreshed suite of offerings, we needed our site to reflect a new customer journey, clearer product suite, SPICED-aligned messaging, and a modern, unified experience — while driving real conversion improvements.
> Once that was clear, decisions had a framework to run through.
> 2️⃣ Use AI as shared memory.
> One of the smartest things we did (eventually) was build a dedicated ChatGPT project and feed it our ICPs, personas, positioning, tone, and messaging — and keep updating it.
> It became a strategic partner.
> Not because it “wrote the site,” but because it kept us aligned (and helped with drafting copy, too).
> Building out the content of an entire website takes weeks to months, which can mean inconsistencies across pages and locations if a standard is not followed and maintained. AI helps immensely in alleviating these concerns.
> 3️⃣ Obsess over migration details.
> Design is fun. Preserving search equity is not — but it matters more.
> Working with an AMAZING partner in Zabal Media, we prioritized SEO and AEO from day one so we didn’t lose what we’d built over years of effort.
> 4️⃣ Trust the process.
> If you're looking for shortcuts, you better be sure nothing is missed. With so much to be considered in a successful launch plan, the long road — though it may be tedious — is the surest way to have peace of mind post-migration that all i's have been dotted and t's crossed.
> PRDs, lo-fi, hi-fi, development, content implementation, testing & review, optimization. All stages are equally important and take time to do right.
> 5️⃣ Over-communicate internally.
> Every update kept momentum alive. A website relaunch isn’t just a marketing project — it signals where the company is going.
> I wasn't always the best with this, but keeping a steady drumbeat and sharing exciting progress ensured top-level support was maintained (h/t to Shari Johnston for being our champion, rallying input, and maintaining support).
> The new winningbydesign(dot)com went live to the public on January 28, so because we’re still early, I need to hold off on declaring wins. But the initial engagement signals are encouraging.
> ↑ Traffic
> ↑ Active Visitors
> ↑ Conversions
> If you’ve done a relaunch before — what would you tell me to watch out for next?
> + If you're interested in seeing the new site: https://lnkd.in/gii5arHR

## 124. James Doman-Pipe — 1mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430127100897988610/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> i've seen these mistakes too many times. you've decided to pivot your product, launch, and then open up MORE problems that were supposed to be solved.
> i've lead major repositioning and pivots over the last 12 years and these are the most common patterns i see.
> most companies will
> 1) decide to pivot
> 2) set a product launch date
> 3) then 'iterate' positioning later on
> ... and then they wonder why:
> - sellers aren't pitching new product properly
> - customers aren't quite getting it
> - marketing haven't nailed how to drive leads yet
> ... meanwhile, customer support/success teams are left supporting the old world, the new world, AND the customer change management between them
> the fix: pivots need to align product AND positioning at the same time.
> just like you go to market with a committed product, you need committed positioning too.
> the three major mistakes are:
> 1. Premature launches
> Positioning is committed before the product direction. You *know* what story you want to sell, and hope that product can build it.
> = one-way ticket to chaos.
> 2. Spinning
> believe it or not, companies do decide to pivot and then just say 'we'll work it out as we go!' to both their product direction AND how they position.
> = lose months, lose momentum, lose confidence
> 3. Death zone
> the most dangerous: the company commits to a product direction, staffs up teams, and makes a bet...
> ... but doesn't make strong, clear decisions on positioning fundamentals.
> = product vision gets pulled sideways as sales/marketing try to sell to anyone
> So, how do you make sure you end up Launch Ready?
> Ultimately, it's building positioning AT THE SAME TIME as your product pivot.
> They are intertwined. Your positioning defines the product, the product defines the positioning.
> Only when you make decisions on these TOGETHER do you put your best foot forward.
> and when it happens?
> - product pivot moves faster, because the direction is niche
> - positioning feels more exciting
> - marketing becomes more targeted and more effective
> - sellers have confidence, discovery rolls easily and pipeline builds and converts
> - you get BETTER INSIGHTS FASTER, because your go-to-market is more narrow and more opinionated
> Product relaunches are not just superficial marketing activity; they are fundamentally a business change management exercise, that starts with:
> - who our customer is
> - what value they desire
> - how we will help them
> - how the customer will buy
> Decide those, then everything gets easier.
> …more

> Activate to view larger image,

## 125. Madhav Bhandari — 1mo

> Pattern Interrupt Marketing book coming soon | Head of Marketing @ Storylane

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7430127064059543552/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> IMO the AEO playbook is 90% bullsh*t. We wasted 1 year and tens of thousands of dollars figuring this out.
> So let me save you the pain.
> Everyone's selling you on:
> - Citations as a service
> - Position tracking in LLM answers
> - GEO tools
> - LLMS.txt robots files
> - Adding FAQs / Q&As to your blogs
> - Synthetic Q&A generation
> - GEO audits and scorecards
> - All this other tactical garbage
> It's noise. We tried them all and gave them enough time to work.
> It'll make you feel busy. It'll make consultants money. But it moves zero needles.
> Here's what actually works:
> 1. Build brand - get people to talk about you on the internet - talk on social, get your customers to talk on social, do external podcasts, get other people to write blogs/posts about your brand experiments etc.
> 2. Create content LLMs can't - pulling insights from your actual sales calls, showing your product in unique formats like demo-led SEO, and writing stuff that's genuinely helpful for your buyers.
> That's it. You know, the stuff we used to call "good SEO/great content marketing strategy."
> Every case study you've seen of companies killing it in LLMs?
> 90% of their success = those two things above
> 10% = the AEO "strategies" everyone's pitching
> I know this is a harsh take and people's livelihood depends on this. But please change your services to more around Search Everywhere Optimization (aka brand) and creating content that's helpful for buyers.
> Stop overthinking this. Stop buying these expensive tools. Stop chasing the shiny tactics.
> Just build your brand and create content your buyers actually need. Like the old days. But better.
> Happy to be proven wrong! If that is the case, show me a business that's killing it in LLM SEO with a direct main correlation to a GEO / AEO tactic (with proof pls - for everyone's good).

> 𝗨𝘀𝗲 𝗰𝗮𝘀𝗲:

> Install the Claude Chrome extension. That's it. This is what makes the whole thing work — Claude can see the page you're on.

> (𝘷𝘪𝘥𝘦𝘰 𝘪𝘴 𝘮𝘺 𝘰𝘸𝘯 𝘤𝘰𝘮𝘱𝘢𝘯𝘺'𝘴 𝘢𝘯𝘢𝘭𝘺𝘴𝘪𝘴 𝘢𝘴 𝘢𝘯 𝘦𝘹𝘢𝘮𝘱𝘭𝘦 - 𝘱𝘳𝘦𝘵𝘵𝘺 𝘴𝘱𝘰𝘵 𝘰𝘯 𝘢𝘯𝘥 𝘨𝘭𝘢𝘥 𝘵𝘩𝘢𝘵 𝘸𝘦 𝘩𝘢𝘷𝘦 𝘤𝘰𝘯𝘴𝘪𝘴𝘵𝘦𝘯𝘵 𝘮𝘦𝘴𝘴𝘢𝘨𝘪𝘯𝘨 😎 )

> 𝘠𝘰𝘶 𝘢𝘳𝘦 𝘢 𝘴𝘦𝘢𝘴𝘰𝘯𝘦𝘥 𝘤𝘰𝘮𝘱𝘦𝘵𝘪𝘵𝘪𝘷𝘦 𝘪𝘯𝘵𝘦𝘭𝘭𝘪𝘨𝘦𝘯𝘤𝘦 𝘢𝘯𝘢𝘭𝘺𝘴𝘵 𝘴𝘱𝘦𝘤𝘪𝘢𝘭𝘪𝘻𝘪𝘯𝘨 𝘪𝘯 𝘉2𝘉 𝘚𝘢𝘢𝘚 𝘢𝘯𝘥 𝘈𝘐. 𝘈𝘯𝘢𝘭𝘺𝘻𝘦 𝘵𝘩𝘪𝘴 𝘤𝘰𝘮𝘱𝘢𝘯𝘺'𝘴 [𝘸𝘦𝘣𝘴𝘪𝘵𝘦/𝘓𝘪𝘯𝘬𝘦𝘥𝘐𝘯/𝘎2] 𝘱𝘳𝘦𝘴𝘦𝘯𝘤𝘦 𝘢𝘯𝘥 𝘱𝘳𝘰𝘷𝘪𝘥𝘦 𝘢 𝘤𝘰𝘮𝘱𝘳𝘦𝘩𝘦𝘯𝘴𝘪𝘷𝘦 𝘢𝘶𝘥𝘪𝘵 𝘣𝘢𝘴𝘦𝘥 𝘰𝘯 𝘵𝘩𝘦 𝘧𝘰𝘭𝘭𝘰𝘸𝘪𝘯𝘨 𝘢𝘳𝘦𝘢𝘴. 𝘉𝘦 𝘴𝘱𝘦𝘤𝘪𝘧𝘪𝘤, 𝘤𝘪𝘵𝘦 𝘦𝘹𝘢𝘮𝘱𝘭𝘦𝘴, 𝘢𝘯𝘥 𝘯𝘰𝘵𝘦 𝘱𝘢𝘵𝘵𝘦𝘳𝘯𝘴 𝘳𝘢𝘵𝘩𝘦𝘳 𝘵𝘩𝘢𝘯 𝘰𝘯𝘦-𝘰𝘧𝘧𝘴.
> 𝘈𝘳𝘦𝘢𝘴 𝘰𝘧 𝘢𝘯𝘢𝘭𝘺𝘴𝘪𝘴: 𝘱𝘰𝘴𝘪𝘵𝘪𝘰𝘯𝘪𝘯𝘨 & 𝘮𝘦𝘴𝘴𝘢𝘨𝘪𝘯𝘨, 𝘤𝘰𝘯𝘵𝘦𝘯𝘵 𝘴𝘵𝘳𝘢𝘵𝘦𝘨𝘺, 𝘱𝘳𝘰𝘥𝘶𝘤𝘵 & 𝘧𝘦𝘢𝘵𝘶𝘳𝘦 𝘮𝘢𝘳𝘬𝘦𝘵𝘪𝘯𝘨, 𝘴𝘰𝘤𝘪𝘢𝘭 𝘱𝘳𝘰𝘰𝘧, 𝘷𝘰𝘪𝘤𝘦 & 𝘣𝘳𝘢𝘯𝘥, 𝘨𝘢𝘱 & 𝘰𝘱𝘱𝘰𝘳𝘵𝘶𝘯𝘪𝘵𝘺
> 𝘖𝘶𝘵𝘱𝘶𝘵 𝘧𝘰𝘳𝘮𝘢𝘵:𝘖𝘳𝘨𝘢𝘯𝘪𝘻𝘦 𝘺𝘰𝘶𝘳 𝘧𝘪𝘯𝘥𝘪𝘯𝘨𝘴 𝘶𝘯𝘥𝘦𝘳 𝘦𝘢𝘤𝘩 𝘴𝘦𝘤𝘵𝘪𝘰𝘯 𝘸𝘪𝘵𝘩 𝘣𝘶𝘭𝘭𝘦𝘵 𝘱𝘰𝘪𝘯𝘵𝘴. 𝘌𝘯𝘥 𝘸𝘪𝘵𝘩 𝘢 "𝘒𝘦𝘺 𝘛𝘢𝘬𝘦𝘢𝘸𝘢𝘺𝘴" 𝘴𝘦𝘤𝘵𝘪𝘰𝘯 𝘴𝘶𝘮𝘮𝘢𝘳𝘪𝘻𝘪𝘯𝘨 𝘵𝘩𝘦 3-5 𝘪𝘯𝘴𝘪𝘨𝘩𝘵𝘴 𝘢𝘯𝘥 𝘰𝘱𝘱𝘰𝘳𝘵𝘶𝘯𝘪𝘵𝘪𝘦𝘴 𝘧𝘰𝘳 𝘥𝘪𝘧𝘧𝘦𝘳𝘦𝘯𝘵𝘪𝘢𝘵𝘪𝘰𝘯.
> Step 3 📌 : Save the analysis. Repeat on another channel (LinkedIn → Website → G2).
> Step 4 📌 : Feed all your channel tear downs into a fresh Claude chat. Ask for cross-channel patterns and higher-level insights.
> (Bonus: Save your prompt as a "Skill" in Claude settings (Capabilities → Skills) so you can reuse it instantly.)
> -----------
> There, 8 hours saved.
> No longer drowning in 47 tabs, or grabbing 59 links and screenshots into ChatGPT/Claude chat/Gemini.
> AI won't do our competitive strategy.
> But it can clear the runway so we can.
> -----------
> If you try this, I want to know your experience/tips.
> P.S. I use a 24-question deep-dive prompt for each analysis area, but too long to paste here. Comment or DM if you want the full doc.
> …more

## 128. Shivangi Sahu — 1mo

> Building for performance marketers | Co-founder, Halos

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7429907304831168514/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> "Something Big Is Happening."
> Matt Shumer's tweet hit over 80 million views last week.
> Suddenly everyone I know is panicking, wondering if their job disappears tomorrow.
> But here's what I'm actually hearing when I talk to customers about AI.
> “We built a quiz tool / created a product recommender / automated data reporting. Those worked great."
> But the moment the task gets complex?
> Tasks that need real business context, multiple people collaborating, judgment about your specific market.
> AI can't deliver.
> So what's actually happening here? 🤔
> There are two kinds of tasks in any role.
> 1. Overhead tasks: the admin work that keeps things running.
> 2. Leverage tasks: the strategic work where your judgment creates value.
> The panic assumes AI is coming for both. But it's really only touching one.
> AI can handle simple overhead. Pulling reports. Formatting data.
> But most overhead isn't that simple.
> ➡️ Figuring out which ads drove revenue across different channels? Needs massive setup.
> ➡️ Investigating why a high confidence campaign tanked? Needs domain expertise.
> And then there's the leverage work.
> ➡️ Building positioning that resonates with your audience.
> ➡️ Choosing between quick wins today or building your brand for the long term.
> That requires judgment about things AI doesn't have.
> Your company's risk tolerance. Your team's capabilities.
> The competitive dynamics you've observed over months.
> And this is the nuance that these viral posts miss. 👀
> When a developer like Shumer says AI can do his job autonomously, he's working with clean inputs and verifiable outputs.
> Non-technical roles like marketing don’t work that way.
> The disruption timeline is fundamentally different.
> Yes, AI capabilities are advancing. Yes, new viral posts will keep coming.
> But that doesn't mean you need to panic.
> What it means is you need to learn.
> ✅ Learn where AI actually helps versus where it creates more work.
> ✅ Learn how to set up these tools properly instead of trying 10 and giving up.
> Because the gap between "experiment" and "replacing complex workflows" is bigger than social media makes it seem.
> Stop sprinting. Start learning. 💪
> Which overhead tasks have you successfully automated?
> And which ones did AI make more complicated than doing it yourself?
> …more
> Activate to view larger image,
> Activate to view larger image,

## 130. James Doman-Pipe — 2mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7429900752258572288/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> Here are five things that you’ll never get a CEO, CMO, CRO, and product marketer to agree on:
> 𝟏. 𝐇𝐨𝐰 𝐧𝐢𝐜𝐡𝐞 𝐢𝐬 𝐧𝐢𝐜𝐡𝐞 𝐞𝐧𝐨𝐮𝐠𝐡
> The CEO wants to keep the TAM wide for investors.
> The CRO wants every deal that walks through the door.
> The CMO wants a story that scales across segments.
> And the product marketer is begging someone, anyone, to pick a lane.
> 𝟐. 𝐇𝐨𝐰 𝐡𝐢𝐠𝐡 𝐭𝐨 𝐩𝐢𝐭𝐜𝐡
> Sales wants to talk features because that’s what closes.
> Marketing wants to talk vision because that’s what brands are built on.
> The CEO wants to talk transformation because that’s what boards want to hear.
> Nobody is pitching at the altitude the buyer is operating at.
> 𝟑. 𝐏𝐚𝐢𝐧𝐤𝐢𝐥𝐥𝐞𝐫 𝐨𝐫 𝐯𝐢𝐭𝐚𝐦𝐢𝐧
> Product says painkiller.
> Marketing says vitamin.
> Sales says whatever the prospect wants to hear.
> The positioning doc says both, somehow, in the same sentence.
> 𝟒. 𝐖𝐡𝐞𝐧 𝐭𝐨 𝐬𝐭𝐨𝐩 𝐢𝐭𝐞𝐫𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐦𝐚𝐤𝐞 𝐚 𝐛𝐞𝐭
> Everyone loves “testing and learning” because it means nobody has to commit.
> Three rounds of messaging iteration later, you’ve got six versions, no conviction, and a sales team that’s still using the old deck.
> 𝟓. 𝐓𝐡𝐞 𝐧𝐞𝐞𝐝 𝐭𝐨 𝐥𝐨𝐜𝐤 𝐝𝐨𝐰𝐧 𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐬𝐭𝐚𝐲 𝐥𝐨𝐜𝐤𝐞𝐝
> You finally get alignment.
> Messaging is signed off.
> Positioning is locked.
> Then the CEO comes back from a conference with a new idea.
> The CRO loses two deals and wants to “revisit the narrative.”
> The CMO sees a competitor launch and panics.
> Six weeks of conviction, evaporates.
> Your market never got the chance to hear a consistent story because you couldn’t sit with one long enough to let it work.
> ---
> Nobody wants to make a hard choice, because hard choices mean tradeoffs and decisions.
> Positioning = strategy.
> …more
> 62

## 134. Aditya Sriram — 1mo

> Building GoMarble || AI Agent for paid media marketers; built on your Meta Ads, Google Ads, Shopify, and GA4.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7428477916906397696/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Claude Code + Meta Ads MCP is insane!
> This combination turns your terminal into a full-blown Meta Ads command center.
> Ask questions about your Meta Ads in natural language & get performance insights, audit flags, and recommendations.
> Here's how the magic happens:
> → Claude Code connects to GoMarble MCP
> → GoMarble pulls live data from your Meta Ads account

## 135. Martin Uushona — 2mo

> Operating at the intersection of AI & content.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7427331736092639232/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> If you have a product/service that serves multiple persona's, you can spend days creating persona-specific content. CTO version needed technical depth. CFO version needed cost justification. CMO version needed ROI framing.
> I decided to flip the script and leverage Claude Code and now what used to take days and weeks takes an hour.
> Most persona-adapted content sounds exactly the same across different personas.
> It's hard for the everyday person to hold the perspectives, pain points, problems and desires for five different persona's.
> To fix this I decided to build a Claude Code workflow using 5 different sub agents. These are mini little workers that are all working towards one goal: A marketing message adapted for 5 different persona's.
> I activate one agent per persona. All working from the same source material and in parallel. This means you're not only saving time but tokens as well.
> Then a coordinator agent compares all 5 in a strict process. If any two versions are more than 40% similar in structure? One gets sent back to sharpen until it's deemed worthy.
> What you get is 5 versions of a product/feature launch that that actually speak to the specific customer segment you're writing for.
> If you want this workflow + prompt, comment "persona" I'll DM it to you.
> Once you have it, its copy and paste with your own information.

## 138. Rory Woodbridge — 2mo

> Product Marketing Director | Positioning, Messaging & GTM for European tech companies | Founder, The Product Marketer | Ex Google, Pleo, Amazon | Top Product Marketing Consultant 2025

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7425344544021495808/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 📢 A small public service announcement 📢
> You do not need to launch your product on the same day it goes live.
> In fact, there are many reasons you shouldn't.
> Most software companies follow the standard release process: Alpha → Beta → General Availability.
> But there's a crucial fourth step that gets skipped or blended into GA: your launch moment.
> There are, of course, exceptions and caveats. If you’re a well-known consumer brand, people are watching, so delaying your announcement might not be practical.
> But in B2B, this approach is not only often possible - it comes with serious benefits.
> Separating GA from your commercial launch gives you:
> 🚀 A buffer for if/when release dates slip
> 🎙️ Time to learn what customers are actually saying
> 🌠 Space for product teams to fully engage on GTM
> 🕹️ Control over the narrative instead of reactive firefighting
> In this week's edition of The Product Marketer I'm sharing my simple product release framework for keeping everyone calm.
> Check out an overview below. And the full post in the comments 👇

## 139. Alan Zhao — 2mo

> Co-founder, Head of Marketing, Product Strategy and AI @ Warmly.ai | Building the GTM Brain | #1 Context Graphs for GTM Agents & Humans

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7425344469039996928/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I've been automating our own GTM at Warmly with with Warmly + Claude Code Agent SDK and here's what you need to do:
> 1. Integrate all your systems. For us it's HubSpot (CRM activity stream), Slack (internal discussions on deals), Sybill (call recording), BuiltWith (technographics), Warmly, (signals, chat conversations, enrichment/web research). Outreach for sequencing. APIs or bust for vendors
> 2. Use Warmly's TAM Agent to find the short list of high intent ICP companies and perfect buying committee contacts (no interns, just buyers). 500 accounts, 1500 contacts. Pull into Claude Code.
> 3. Use Claude Code to build territory mapping for AEs and SDRs. 15 per AE, 100+ per SDR. Map email inboxes. Just prompt this and save as an .md file the territory mapping. Then map this to Hubspot via the integration
> 4. Use Claude Code to query my systems in step 1 and build the full timeline on each of the accounts. "Closed lost, who visited the website, when did we engage last, what pages are they looking for, what was discussed on last demo call"
> 4. Use Octave API to write the perfect email outreach with full context from Step 3. Send this automatically to Outreach with tags and using custom fields for subject and email body.
> 5. Automatically sync the contacts directly to LinkedIn Ads.
> Results:
> - Ads: 50% reduction in CPC, 3x improvement in CTR for the exact same ad creative after a week of running.
> - Emails: Will let you know next week. Aleksandar Lepidovski running it right now!
> Okay here's the problems you run into:
> 1. You are going to iterate a lot with prompting Claude Code and context degrades quickly when you use MCPs. Claude code does its best to auto-compact but I've noticed that once it gets to 85% context window the memory degradation is already severe.
> 2. It takes a long time to pull the full context on the accounts once you get a short list of accounts to do deep research on. And by the time the context pulls you realize you missed something or claude didn't pull it right... so you burn through tokens (good thing claude code tokens are cheap for now)
> 3. Agents are stateless, which means once you close the session it loses all the context, so you need a way to build memory into the system if you want to run this job again. You'll use .MD guideline files for now and you try your best to use claude.md files to stitch together where to look, but you have no idea if the AI is reading in the right context and guideline files before it runs the job. So you pray.
> 4. You have no idea how to encode this into your existing human and workflow systems and how to scale it. Human systems are more deterministic (gets the job done) but do not compound alongside foundational model improvements.
> 5. You realize that shit data in means shit outputs and that's a whole can of worms that has nothing to do with LLMs. You don't want to manage this system even though it's the foundation.
> You start looking for Agent Harnesses for GTM (check the comments)
> …more

> Not your usual LinkedIn post.
> '5 AI lessons from Crochet', would be an apt title for this post but then I will be killed by the LinkedIn vigilants.
> I saw this beautiful crochet stole and was inspired to make it. But the post just sent me down a rabbit hole of useless links, so I did what is now second nature - go to LLM and ask it to do it for me. Not the crocheting, just generating the pattern.
> The pattern came back in 30 seconds with all details including instructions on Blocking etc. and I was suitably impressed.
> But then I thought "what if this is wrong, am I willing to spend hours making this based on an AI generated pattern"?
> Just a reminder that the cost of copy-pasting LLM's output sometimes appears low, but its important to still stop and ask "Will I use this without even proof-reading if the cost was higher e.g. a few hours, my brand's reputation, my customers happiness or my product feature"

## 144. Shruti Kapoor — 2mo

> Helping early-stage founders go from mental clutter to meaningful action | Founder Coach | Ex-Clari, Wingman (YC S19), IIM-A

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7419730145973911553/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Unpopular opinion: "Also" is the most expensive word in your startup’s vocabulary.
> I was talking to a founder recently about their thesis. Every time I pushed on a specific ICP or use case, the answer was: "Yes, and in the future, we will ALSO do X."
> "We help mid-market sales teams, but we'll do enterprise also." "We solve pipeline visibility, but we'll solve coaching also."
> I get it. You don't want to leave money on the table. But here’s the reality: If your product is for everyone eventually, it’s for no one today.
> When you refuse to make a bet, your "Pain-Promise" equation breaks:
> The Pain becomes a "general annoyance" (low priority).
> The Promise becomes a "broad platform" (long sales cycle).
> The Price becomes a "commodity" (no leverage).
> You aren't protecting your upside by saying "also." You’re just diluting your signal.
> The 90-Minute (not 90-DAY!!) Audit, that could save you months: Look at your last 5 discovery calls. If you spent more than 10 minutes explaining "what else we can do," you’ve lost the plot.
> Pick one bet. Be wrong about it if you have to. But stop "also-ing" your way into irrelevance.
> What’s the one thing you’re willing to say “No” to for the next 6 months?
> …more

## 149. Ankita Agarwal — 3mo

> Claude Code pilled | GTM, SaaS | IIMA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7414519366253375488/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Apparently it’s not cool anymore (Gen Z/X? 👀) to use Duolingo
> But I'm a millennial learning German, so I downloaded it anyway and made peace with my irrelevance ✌️
> Duolingo gets talked about a lot for its gamification.
> Some say it’s fun but doesn’t actually help you speak.
> My verdict: it’s definitely helped me pick up some Deutsch, but here’s how it could get even more useful 👇
> 1️⃣ For languages like German, where every noun has a gender, remembering that Kaffee is "der Kaffee" and not "die Kaffee" is harder than it looks - say it wrong and your coffee is grammatically dead.
> A glossary that includes gender — plus the ability to sort by category (food, travel, verbs) — would massively improve retention.
> 2️⃣ I was able to skip an entire level without studying it, and also translated “Is Chicago loud?” about 15 times 😅
> Adaptive difficulty > repetitive reinforcement. Or just give me difficulty settings!
> 3️⃣ More grammar-focused lessons would reduce confusion later, right now I'm just vibing with patterns and bothering my best friend, ChatGPT
> 4️⃣ Longer practice tests clearly mapped to A1 / A2 levels would help me benchmark my progress
> P.S Can there be a setting to turn off the 'bi-ding?' (iykyk) 🙈
> 👀 @Duolingo — just friendly 🦉 product feedback from a user who’s trying very hard to order einen Kaffee correctly.

## 150. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 3mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7411041175417106432/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I once earned a $25K contract by answering a single question on Quora.
> This was years ago. Someone asked a specific, technical question about scraping. I didn’t write a novel. I gave a short, direct answer pointing to the right resources.
> The DMs started rolling in. One turned into a client.
> For years, I told myself I won that deal because I was helpful.
> Well, not really.
> I didn't win because I had the information.
> I won because people perceived I had the scars.
> If that client asks that same question today, ChatGPT gives them the answer in 0.4 seconds. The "Information Gap" I exploited back then has closed.
> So, as we stare down 2026, we have to ask the question:
> If the "answer" is now a commodity, what are we actually selling?
> I feel we are no longer in the business of information transfer. Marketing that banks on that will fail.
> We are now in the business of experience transfer. We need to explore the valley of undocumented journeys.
> I asked myself why I subscribe to Paweł Huryn’s newsletter or listen to Peter Yang and Aakash Gupta.
> Are their insights novel? Yes.
> But it's the validation that counts for even more.
> When they share a process or interview a guest about their specific setup, we want the reassurance that someone relatable has actually walked the path, rolled up their sleeves, and survived the implementation.
> So, as I plan for my 2026 content strategy, I'm working on a simpler playbook:
> → Don't just answer questions. Validate the anxiety.
> Mine your sales calls for where the customer repeatedly talks about the same specific pain and sounds unsure. Write to that. This means attacking the long-tail ideas.
> → Kill the "Ultimate Guide."
> The world doesn't need another generic overview. It needs specific war stories. I'm in event tech, so I'd replace "How to run an event" with "How we salvaged an event when the wifi died."
> → Visuals as proof, not decoration.
> Use screenshots, messy diagrams, and real photos that prove a human was actually there. Yes, you can Nano Banana it but only if it's a shareable insight or a relatable insert. Not a filler graphic.
> Finally, the recommendations from AI SEOs on adding tldr, FAQs, and direct answers are really forcing functions to deliver direct value rather than ramble in a 1200-word blog padded with unnecessary bloat.
> AI slop is actually creating a market ripe for disruption.
> Audiences will eventually crave for proof that someone actually did the work, beyond what the "correct" answer might be.
> Sell them that.
> …more

## 154. James Doman-Pipe — 3mo

> Positioning & Launches for B2B SaaS & AI | Co-founder, Inflection Studio | Product Marketing Consultant | Ex-Remote

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7410286522924560384/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> A lot of founders end up rewriting their website homepage themselves 🤦
> “I’m not happy with how we’re explaining this.”
> “It's not landing.”
> “This isn’t how I want us to sound.”
> Those thoughts keep coming back around, and yep...
> ... they might be valid.
> But when the homepage gets rewritten again and again, it is RARELY a wording issue.
> It's a sign that you, the founder, haven't made a call with confidence, hasn't been communicated clearly, and held consistently.
> - Who this is really for?
> - Which problem matters most?
> - What are you willing to deprioritize?
> Without those decisions, positioning drifts because everyone has input.
> Marketing has context.
> Sales brings objections.
> Product brings features.
> ... it's all useful, but it doesn't set direction.
> So the copy changes and the story shifts.
> Not because you learned something new, but because the direction is still open.
> Go-to-market is a bet.
> A bet needs time to play out.
> If you keep resetting the language, you reset the learning.
> The founders I've worked with who make the most progress do one thing differently:
> 1. They get close to the messaging early.
> 2. They make the call.
> 3. They let it run long enough to teach them something.
> So if you're considering another website change... maybe it's time to revisit your GTM fundamentals:
> - Ideal Customer Profile
> - Positioning
> - GTM strategy

## 159. Vijay Jay — 3mo

> Head of Product, AI @ Provarity AI | 1x Founder

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7408356195469549568/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I built an entire B2B SaaS product all by myself in 2 weeks, and here are three mental models that helped me get there!
> When you have no engineers, no designers, and no PMs, things get chaotic fast. I learned that the only way to keep the product moving was to think in structures, not tasks.
> The Friction Mapping Model
> Before designing screens or flows, I resolved friction, not feature requests. Every hesitation, repeated action, memory burden, and unnecessary click became design input.
> Friction reveals truths that UI doesn't show. It exposes where people lose momentum or mentally tap out.
> A moment that changed everything came while deploying Securepost. I watched a store owner bounce between three screens to find a contact. The friction was cognitive. He had to remember which screen handled which workflow. One focused prompt rebuilt the workflow so the system, not the user, held the context. Training time dropped to almost zero.
> The Prompt as a Colleague Model
> Clarity came when I stopped treating prompts like instructions and started treating them like teammates with real responsibilities.
> I began setting goals, defining what data each prompt could use, and establishing simple dos and don'ts. That is when AI stopped mimicking answers and started contributing to the build.
> The QA analyst prompt became the most important of all. Its responsibility was simple: track each iteration and surface every assumption I had not stated.
> The QA prompt surfaced a subtle risk: duplicate tracking number was allowed, however, the tracking id was unique. So I redesigned the intake screen to improve clarity. One small change removed confusion and improved user experience.
> The One-Way Door Decision Model
> Momentum came from knowing which decisions were reversible and which ones would trap the product in the future.
> Notification rules made this clear. Each iteration added new conditions, overrides, and timing rules. Complexity kept stacking.
> I scrapped the entire engine and went back to basics. Designed just three templates that worked better than the fully customizable version. Less configuration. Less confusion.
> These three models are the reason the product shipped cleanly, even with no team behind it.
> Now I am curious.
> Where do you feel the most friction in your builds?
> If you used AI as a teammate, which role would you assign first?
> …more

## 161. Ciana Abdollahian — 3mo

> Customer marketer navigating a LinkedIn identity crisis | Unsolicited job search advice, AI experiments, and all things customer marketing

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7408051323704741888/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I vibe-coded a tool to solve the most iconic customer marketing request:
> “I need a customer who…” 👩‍💻
> If you work in customer marketing, you hear this all the time.
> - Sales needs a customer who moved off X competitor.
> - Marketing needs a customer who fits Y industry
> - CS needs a customer who is using Z product.
> So last week, I decided to go all in on building lightweight internal tool to make this easier. The screenshot below is v1.
> What I did:
> After a recent Cursor workshop, I pulled data from one of our Tableau dashboards, organized it into a structured CSV, and built a simple interface that lets someone type in exactly what they’re looking for.
> I started to build with prompts that I've heard from GTM teammates like:
> “Midmarket & enterprise customers using Customer Hub I can name-drop”
> “Beauty brands with published case studies”
> “Customers over $100M ARR using Email + SMS”
> The tool then returns a list of matching customers including health score, ARR, products used, case studies, and more.
> What worked:
> Even in this rough prototype, the tool is able to surface:
> - customer health
> - products used
> - industry
> - case study availability (with links)
> - annual revenue
> What I still need:
> 1️⃣ A dynamic, always-current data source
> Right now, it’s powered by a static CSV stored locally. Great for testing and refine. not great long term. For this to work, it needs a direct connection to our live Tableau data.
> 2️⃣ A secure way to deploy it internally
> At the moment, I’m the only one who can access it. The next step is figuring out how to host this securely so employees can access it.
> None of this is surprising to me. This exactly the part people skip when they say “just use AI for that.” 😏
> Future vision:
> If the foundational pieces come together, this could evolve into a more powerful discovery layer for GTM teams, including:
> - previous reference activity
> - deeper product usage signals
> - customer spend (where relevant)
> - signals for potential reference candidates
> - a more complete, searchable view of the customer base
> Tools like Deeto will support formal reference workflows, but there’s still a gap for quick, early-stage customer discovery -- the type of request that starts with “I need a customer who…”
> A lot of people in the CMA community have been curious if I’ve made any progress on my AI experiments, so wanted to update you with the latest.
> We’re definitely not at the finish line, but the opportunity is still there and I'm feeling optimistic!
> More to come...

## 166. Akshaya Sampath — 4mo

> Outbound Architect Building Complex B2B Sales Motions | $5-$20M ARR | Systems Thinker

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7407950020244668417/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> “The job market is pathetic. Half the market deserves to be unemployed.”
> A founding team member of a startup made this statement last week.
> All because his CXO shared his direct phone number and expected 100+ candidates to cold call him.
> Only a few called.
> 200+ messaged instead.
> And that somehow became “proof” that the market deserves to me unemployed.
> The job market isn’t lazy. It has evolved.
> Whereas the hiring expectations haven’t.
> And guess what?? Data backs this shift:
> 71% prioritise work-life alignment over compensation.
> 60% want context before committing energy to a call or application.
> Authentic, frictionless roles → attract better talent than “prove you’re hungry” tests.
> If candidates aren’t chasing your vacancy, it’s not a “talent problem.”
> It’s a value articulation problem.
> Top teams have already updated their playbooks:
> Sell the role, not the grind (the founder cold calling videos ain't doing the job for you)
> Reduce friction, don’t add hoops.
> Offer something candidates can resonate with, not fear.
> Build roles worth fighting for — not roles that test desperation.
> If you're the one
> hashtag
> #hiring then remember this:
> It’s not charity.
> People aren’t lining up to be “grateful.”
> A company has to pitch why the role matters — just like it expects candidates to pitch why they matter.
> The moment leaders assume talent will chase them by default…
> that’s when they start mistaking disengagement for incompetence, and rage bait for leadership.

## 167. Shivangi Sahu — 3mo

> Building for performance marketers | Co-founder, Halos

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7407949795916517376/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I ran a product demo with a real prospect in my first week of joining Wingman.
> No shadowing. No sandbox. Just the very real risk of things going horribly wrong on a Friday evening Zoom call.
> Sounds terrifying? It wasn’t. Well, not as much as I thought it would be.
> You know why?
> I was given exactly what I needed to prepare 👇
> -> Context on people: who owns what
> -> Context on pain points and product: what and who are we solving for, and how
> -> Context on process: what tools or resources I could use
> The context was amazing, but what really got me executing like a ninja from week 2?
> That demo.
> It gave me clarity, confidence and guts.
> And when I designed our new hire onboarding experience, I made the demo (either to a customer / to a founder) non-negotiable.
> A lot of startups delay this last step.
> That’s a mistake.
> If your new hire is lost after week 1, they will never have the time to learn again.
> Neither will their manager have the time to explain the context again.
> Front load the complexity, and the realities of executing at a young startup.
> Let them learn on-the-go.
> That feedback loop will stick. Guaranteed!
> And you’ll have an employee ready to take on big challenges, without needing context again.
> P.S. of course, you can make this fun too. Like Shruti did, when she took me hiking with the team on day 1. I understood right away what this would be like, and I couldn’t wait to get started!

> And about how leaders treat people -
> not just when things go well,
> but when things go wrong.
> 7 ways to build a people-first workplace:
> 1. Make it safe to fail 🛡️
> ↳ Share your own mistakes and focus on learning
> 2. Notice the small wins ⭐
> ↳ Recognize daily efforts, not just quarterly results
> 3. Trust people with their time ⏱️
> ↳ Stop questioning when someone needs a personal day
> 4. Respect energy levels 🔋
> ↳ Stop scheduling heavy meetings at 4PM
> 5. Give time for side projects 💡
> ↳ The next big innovation starts as an experiment
> 6. Let people choose their projects 💪
> ↳ Passion drives better results than assignments
> 7. Value people first 🫶
> ↳ Kindness and respect create loyalty money can't buy
> When you take care of your people,
> they take care of everything else.
> Tag a leader who leads this way 🌟

> What's the right size for a B2B marketing team generating $50M in revenue?

> At our Agentic GTM event last week, Alina shared how Chili Piper cut their marketing team from 22 to 2 while generating 1,400 sales-qualified opportunities in 2024. I want to break down what actually made that possible because it wasn't just "using AI."
> The first unlock was data clarity. She consolidated everything into Snowflake and discovered something uncomfortable: their high-LTV customers looked nothing like the ICP they'd been targeting. The real opportunity wasn't thousands of accounts. It was 872.
> The second unlock was redefining what marketers actually do. Her philosophy is simple: marketers get attention and orchestrate everything else. Agents handle the campaign logistics, the funnel tracking, the partner coordination. Humans focus on creativity and trust.
> The third unlock surprised me most. After all that automation, Chili Piper's biggest conversion lift came from a three-day training on trust. Close rates jumped from 22% to 40%. Not from better targeting. From teaching reps to actually connect with buyers.
> That's the part most AI conversations miss. Agents scale efficiency. Humans close belief gaps. You need both.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7406369498720632832/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **supports**

> No one tells you what happens when your body stops agreeing with your ambition.
> This year tested me in ways I didn’t expect.
> It was easier when I was building and serving my clients underground.
> The pressure was quieter.
> The expectations were manageable.
> For almost 10 months, my body had been sending signals I kept negotiating with.
> Fatigue.
> Mental fog.
> Constant panic.
> But things only truly broke after Nov 3rd — the day we launched Skopra.
> I spent three months in a state of panic I couldn’t switch off.
> My mind felt heavy.
> Even simple decisions felt harder than they should’ve been.
> Then the bloodwork came in:
> Chronically high cortisol.
> Clear signs of burnout.
> And here’s what hit me hardest:
> Somewhere in the middle of building Skopra, I had stopped being Akshaya outside of work.
> Every conversation I had was about outbound, GTM, systems, growth.
> Networking drained me, but I kept forcing myself to show up.
> I’d drifted away from my family and friends because I assumed they “wouldn’t understand my work.”
> What I forgot was this:
> They didn’t need to understand my work.
> They understand me.
> Being with them reminded me of something I had completely pushed aside:
> My worth is not my ARR.
> My importance is not my title.
> My identity is not my company.
> To them:
> My bank balance didn’t matter.
> My career didn’t matter.
> My “founder energy” didn’t matter.
> They saw me.
> And I didn’t have to perform for it.
> It reminded me of something I had forgotten:
> Your roots aren’t a distraction from your growth.
> They’re the only thing that keeps you grounded while you grow.
> This isn’t just my story.
> If you’re building something right now, especially something ahead of the market:
> Don’t lose the roots that hold your identity together.
> Your company is your creation.
> Your people are your foundation.
> I’m learning to build both :)

## 180. Varun Anand — 3mo

> Co-Founder @ Clay

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7406238188610019328/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **funny**

> I want to give every startup founder permission to hire candidates based off vibes. But I'm going to give you the vocabulary to feel better about it.
> Leaders resist "vibes hiring" because it sounds lazy. Like you’re not doing due diligence. People don’t love feelings-based decisions and revert to only what the evidence says. But recruiting is a human game and humans have feelings. That’s why you must incorporate how someone makes you feel in the decision.
> So, what does this mean in practice with candidates? It means that you pay attention to the intangible. Do you get energy from the conversations? Do they inspire you with confidence and peace of mind? Do you feel excitement not anxiety when you see their caller ID on your phone? Do you feel conversational chemistry? Do you naturally gravitate towards and want to spend more time with them? Good news - at a startup, you’re basically living with them.
> With these hires, everything just feels right. Decisions are made easily, conflict and arguments resolve in seconds not hours, and the building blocks of trust stack on top of each other. You have people like this on your team right now. You know who they are.
> Interviewing is an information gathering exercise, and how a candidate makes you feel (The Vibes) is as important as their take home assessment, behavioral interview and specific competencies.
> Even if someone is perfect on paper, you can’t get things done at the quality and speed a startup requires if you keep having those little moments of friction that fracture the trust and communication needed to excel.
> You are about to spend your life building with them. You need to enjoy the vibes!

## 187. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 4mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7403518767093280768/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> If you want to break into product marketing in 2026 and not feel like an impostor, here’s what I’d focus on if I had to start over from zero 👇
> 1️⃣ Customer obsession, not feature obsession
> Analyze call recordings. Mine G2 Reviews. Talk to customers. Every great PMM insight starts outside the building.
> 2️⃣ Positioning & narrative design
> Learn how to turn messy feature sets into a sharp story: problem → outcome → proof. If you can’t explain it simply, sales can’t sell it.
> 3️⃣ Stop treating launches like a checklist
> Plan launches like a creator, not a project manager.
> Break the pattern where your audience already hangs out.
> Ex: turn a feature launch into a 5-day LinkedIn “build in public” series with real customer clips, ending in a live teardown.
> 4️⃣ Revenue-linked metrics
> Go beyond “impressions” and “MQLs”. Tie your work to ARR influenced, win rate, expansion, and product adoption. Speak the language of the CFO.
> 5️⃣ AI-native workflow
> Use AI for research synthesis, message variants, feedback analysis, actionable competitive intelligence, and more. Go beyond prompts. Orchestrate systems.
> 6️⃣ Enablement as a product
> Just like PMs sunset features that don't work, quit pouring hours into battlecards and sales decks that never get read.
> Co-build better models with sales e.g.
> - Enablement chatbots
> - Interactive demos they can tailor in real time
> - NotebookLM audio summaries of calls & launches
> - An internal swipe file of product updates reps can copy-paste from
> 7️⃣ Collaboration superpowers
> You’re the connective tissue between Product, Sales, CS, and Growth. Clear comms, healthy conflict, and influence without authority are just part and parcel of the job.
> Bonus: Pair with Customer Success (B2B SaaS)
> Your biggest growth lever is the customers you already have. Sit with CSMs. Listen to renewal calls.
> With customer marketing, weave product stories into expansion narratives that make upselling the obvious next step.
> --
> If you’re already in PMM, which of these will you double down on in 2026?
> …more

## 189. Daniel Matias — 4mo

> Helping Founders Scale with AI Growth Systems - Freelance AI Engineer - 100K+ users funneled

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7403019594292203521/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Nick Saraev made $92K/month with his AI automation agency...
> (as a 1-man show)
> I broke down his exact sales system.
> Most agencies use the "Traditional Sales System":
> → Cold email, PPC, SEO, social media, Upwork, communities
> → Book a meeting
> → Discovery call happens
> → Send detailed proposal/SOW
> → Follow up cycle if not signed
> → Wait 30 days if not a fit right now
> → Closing call
> → Start project
> That's 60+ days from first touch to closed deal
> Too many steps. Too many drop-offs. Too slow.
> Here's what Nick uses instead:
> The One-Call Close System:
> → Same marketing channels at the top
> → Meeting booked
> → Sales meeting happens AND SOW is presented on the SAME call
> → Signed & paid?
> → Start project
> No discovery call.
> No "let me send you a proposal."
> No 30-day follow-up limbo.
> One call. Close or move on.
> Oh, and the follow-ups? Automated.
> The lead nurturing? Automated.
> The proposal generation? Automated.
> He built the system once. Now it runs while he focuses on closing.
> Why this works:
> → Fewer steps = fewer drop-offs
> → Prospects are hottest on the first call
> → Automation handles the boring stuff
> → No chasing. No waiting. No pipeline purgatory.
> The difference between struggling agencies and $92K/month?
> It's not more leads. It's a tighter system.
> I put together the full breakdown of this funnel.
> Inside you'll find:
> → The One-Call Close system (for high-ticket inbound)
> → The Traditional Agency flow (60+ day sales cycle)
> → The Two-Call Close system (for enterprise deals)
> → When to use each one based on your offer
> → Small lead pool vs large lead pool outreach strategies
> Want it free?
> 1. Connect with me
> 2. Comment "FUNNEL"
> And I'll send it to your DMs.

## 193. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 4mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7402390846165458944/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Most AI products die quietly in infancy, but not because the model failed. But because of realities like:
> → Nobody trusted it enough to use it.
> → Not enough people knew about it.
> → Few wanted to pay the price for it.
> We've spent the past 3 years obsessing over what models can do.
> But commercially viable AI products are a different beast compared to cute AI demos and n8n tapestries.
> The next wave is figuring out how to distribute AI products and get people to actually adopt them.
> To help understand this better, I was lucky enough to partner with the folks at Product Faculty and the legendary Miqdad Jaffer (Product Lead at OpenAI) to create something rare: a complete crash course on building, pricing, and launching AI products that people actually use.
> This isn't about silver-bullet prompts or theory-ranting. Miqdad goes super deep about the commercial reality of shipping AI that survives contact with real users.
> Here's what you'll learn from Miqdad in this piece:
> → The 4D Method (Discover, Design, Develop, Deploy) for building AI products with trust engineered in from day one.
> → Why most AI pricing models kill activation before users see value, and the 9 tests to run before launch.
> → The psychological triggers that separate AI products people try once from AI products people can't live without.
> → How to position AI products when "general purpose" is a race to zero. (make sure you check the examples)
> → The launch playbook for getting users to trust your AI before competitors replicate it.
> Yes, this is a publicly accessible guide.
> No paywall.
> No "comment to get it" BS.
> It's available on aatir.substack.com.
> If nothing else, I highly recommend that all product leaders at least give Chapter 2 a read. It breaks down the 4D Method of building AI products.
> It also has a system prompt you can steal and use immediately. It's the clearest framework I've seen for thinking through AI product work.
> There's also a special offer in the newsletter for the Behind Product Lines community :)
> Hope it helps.

> Have you ever had that strange phase during a job transition where everything suddenly feels… slower?
> Your routine loosens, the days feel lighter than they should, and your mind starts wandering into doubts you never had before.

> When you’re packing up, the old place slowly stops feeling like home.
> But you haven’t reached the new one yet either.
> You’re standing in the hallway between two spaces, not fully in the old, not fully settled into the next.
> Job transitions feel exactly like that.
> The in-between space can be unsettling.
> The quiet makes your thoughts louder.
> The lack of structure feels heavier than the work ever did.
> And even decisions you were confident about can suddenly feel shaky.
> So here’s a genuine question:
> During a job move, do you also feel this “hallway phase” where nothing is wrong, but nothing feels familiar either?
> Because I’m realising it’s normal.
> Transitions are emotionally weird, even when the next step is right.
> And just like moving houses, the moment you step into the new space, hang the first photo, or open the first window, everything starts to make sense again.

## 199. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 4mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7400184479404916736/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Most SaaS products are lucky to have 1-2 growth loops that last. But AI app builders like Lovable and Replit are built differently.
> They have an entire cluster of compounding loops working simultaneously.
> And once they hit critical mass, they spread like wildfire.
> Here's what's different:
> Traditional products might have a referral loop or a virality mechanism. User enthusiasm to refer these products or share them with their network fades over time.
> On the other hand, AI builders have loops stacked on top of loops.
> The concentration of which ones activate varies, but they feed off each other. That inherent virality is driving hockey-stick growth curves.
> Will they last forever? Perhaps not. But the network effects will help them build moats that will protect them from mass user migration.
> Some growth loop mechanics at play:
> Collaboration Loops
> → User builds something → invites teammate to remix → teammate needs account to edit → becomes new user
> Attribution Loops
> → User ships product → people notice "Powered by Lovable" footer → clicks through to build their own
> Social Proof Loops
> → User shares build process on LinkedIn → followers get inspired → sign up to create similar → share their own builds
> Template-based Loops
> → User creates useful tool → publishes as template → others remix/fork it → need paid account to customize → upgrade and share their version
> Inspiration Loops
> → User launches micro-SaaS → inspires others to replace expensive SaaS with owned tool → others copy the playbook
> This isn't a comprehensive list by the way.
> I haven't even gotten to sub-loops like:
> → Lenny's newsletter packaging these in the subscription
> → countless Reddit / Discord forums sharing results
> → podcasts / webinars unpacking best practices
> etc.
> No, not every B2B product category can activate all these loops.
> But one PMs and PMMs should consider as they plan their strategy:
> User-generated content loops.
> For example, one growth lever that contributed to Clay's growth was creators and individuals sharing Claybook builds and GTM workflows in public.
> The product quickly became proof of competence and built trust.
> Questions for your next strategy session:
> → Which growth loop are you betting on for the next 12 months?
> → What would it take to turn your users into creators, not just consumers?
> → How do you make sharing your product a flex, not a favor?
> …more
> Activate to view larger image,
> Activate to view larger image,

## 200. Supriya VS — 4mo

> Product Marketing - Agentic AI | Ex - Multiplier, Directi, upGrad

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7400177843676053504/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> As product marketers, we spend hours crafting decks, messaging docs, talk tracks, and battle cards.
> But here’s the real question: Do you know how it actually lands?
> With a prospect? At an event? In a sales pitch?
> One simple rule of thumb: SAY. IT. OUT. LOUD.
> Before you ship anything, run a quick, real-world test:
> 📢 Read the messaging aloud — even to yourself in a mirror.
> 🧩 Do a mock pitch with your team and listen for where the narrative slips.
> 📄 Ask someone with zero context to read it and explain it back.
> 👂 Sit in on sales calls and watch how reps use (or struggle with) your material.
> We have feedback loops through ads, sales teams, and customers — but the fastest signal often comes from you pressure-testing your own work for clarity, flow, and impact.
> A two-minute “say it out loud” check can save weeks of rewrites and unlock messaging that truly resonates.
> hashtag
> #productmarketing

> I’d never worked in a physical office. Ever.

> Plus, ThunderClap - B2B Website & Branding Agency is also fully remote, avoiding the daily ritual of traffic → pollution → exhaustion → repeat.
> I wondered, first time building a team, will culture and team bonding be the price you pay for WFH?
> But it’s not.
> There is a way that enables familiarity, comfort, and collaboration effortlessly.
> All of us met in Manali recently, to escape the GMeet rectangles and sit with each other over maggi & chai.
> No agenda.
> No speeches.
> No “quarterly planning sessions disguised as team bonding.”
> Just people. By the bonfire, in the rafts, in the ice caps. Getting closer.
> And I remember noticing a change every time we have an off-site.
> After each experience, something changes.
> Something about seeing each other as real people makes us trust each other more.
> Asking for help becomes easier.
> Leaning on each other becomes natural.
> The collaboration feels less like an obligation and more like a genuine partnership.
> That’s when we decided: 1 offsite isn’t enough. We do 2 a year now!!!
> Because easily, this is the best investment we make every year.
> How many companies would say that about off-sites? Most would roll their eyes.
> However, most companies haven’t realized that remote work and human connection aren’t mutually exclusive.
> They’re just 2 things that require intentionality to coexist.
> Hoping to end 2025 with a bang now!
