## 204. Nagarjun Reddy — 4mo

> Sales Leader | Nudge- agentic commerce platform for AI search visibility

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7400114253615132672/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> How I Interview SDRs at Nudge (aka: stop treating people like they’re disposable)
> Interviews are a two-way street.
> If you want great people, act like it.
> Here’s my actual process:
> 1. Give them full context.
> Who they’ll work with (me), what we’re building, the space, the competition, why we win, what the next few quarters look like.
> No mystery boxes. No vague promises.
> 2. Treat them like a strategic hire.
> Ask me anything - numbers, risks, culture, founder dynamics.
> I’ll tell you. People deserve the truth, not hype.
> 3. No expired interview gimmicks from 2010 -
> No “sell me this pen.”
> No out of the blue “pitch me” nonsense.
> We talk real things: quota, attainment, process, drive, career goals.
> 4. I talk pay openly - and pay WELL.
> Early-stage is hard.
> If you want people to ride the chaos with you, compensate them like adults.
> Our SDRs get a % of closed-won deals they source. Besides a solid base and variable, of course!
> Optimize for quality > spam.
> I’ve talked about fair pay and respect for salespeople for years.
> Now that I’m building the team here…
> I’m actually doing it.
> If you're an SDR who’s tired of being treated like a number, DM me.
> Let’s build something that doesn’t suck. And get paid well while doing it!

> Yes, the paratha sometimes looks like the map of an unknown country.

> That’s how character is built.
> A lot of homes are spotless because mothers silently carry the entire load.
> But I’m very clear —
> I don’t want my sons to grow up in a vacuum and then expect another woman to replicate that silent burden.
> I want them to learn responsibility now — not later.
> So even if teaching takes triple the time…
> even if dinner gets delayed…
> even if the house loses its temporary perfection…
> I know I’m planting something deeper —
> a sense of independence, contribution, partnership, and dignity.

## 206. Seepiya Sahni — 4mo

> Marketing Design Lead at Tamnoon | Driving Strategic Creative for High-Growth GTM Teams

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7399505221800009729/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Babe, wake up! There's a new software in town that can do what your existing software does, but with more ✨ease✨.
> Every few weeks there’s a new “must-try” design tool. And suddenly my feed is full of creators saying, “I don’t know how I lived without this.” Really? 🙄
> Most recently, it’s Adobe Express. It’s been out for a while, but now amazing artists and designers have started sharing their workflows on it… and I can’t help but feel the FOMO.
> But then I actually looked at the features, and I can already do all of that with my existing suite, without changing anything.
> Figma + Illustrator + Photoshop already cover 90% of my workflow with more control, more nuance, and a far higher ceiling for flexibility.
> And honestly, a lot of these creators are sharing this content as part of paid partnerships.
> So why am I feeling the urge to add a new tool to ✨revolutionize✨ my workflows?
> Why am I learning something I don’t need… just because everyone on my feed is excited about it?
> Here’s what calmed me down:
> I’m not missing out.
> I already have the tools I need.
> And I have the skills to learn anything new, if and when I actually need it.
> Not because it’s trending.
> Not because creators I admire are using it.
> Not because of FOMO disguised as inspiration.
> I can already make everything I need to make, and I don’t become better by stacking more tools.
> This is a PSA for everyone who feels overwhelmed by the new tools out there: We are okay.
> We don't need new design tools unless they genuinely solve a problem our existing tools can’t.
> …more

## 207. Sulagna Das — 4mo

> Building on P/L Skills for Marketing Head Roles | 11 Years of Expertise in Brand Marketing Strategy, Copywriting, Creative Direction | Helped Startup Agencies to Build Teams from Scratch

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7399481567540203520/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Shit that never happens in Indian advertising 😭😭😭😭
> Khurshid Alam
> Khurshid Alam
> • 2nd
> Premium • 2nd
> Founder at Pixel Street | Creative partner to India’s biggest FMCG brands, including ITC, Marico, & Coca-Cola | Generated 5 cr+ in revenue
> Founder at Pixel Street | Creative partner to India’s biggest FMCG brands, including ITC, Marico, & Coca-Cola | Generated 5 cr+ in revenue
> 4mo •
> 4 months ago • Visible to anyone on or off LinkedIn
> I never imagined a ₹50 chocolate bar could teach a ₹10 crore marketing lesson.
> But Cadbury UK just did that.
> In January 2025, Cadbury UK launched their Made to Share campaign.
> Now, most brands that would’ve run a campaign like this would’ve:
> → Hired celebrity faces
> → Spent on big-budget production
> → Bought prime-time TV spots
> But Cadbury did the opposite.
> They redesigned the product itself to celebrate moments we all experience.
> They created limited-edition Dairy Milk wrappers with lines like:
> → "Who cooked"
> → "Who cleaned"
> → "Who presented the slides"
> Each bar had labeled sections.
> Not just chocolate. A social prompt to share credit.
> Simple. Human. Relatable.
> Why it works (if I talk in terms of a 9-to-5 office culture) 👇
> 📌 Office nostalgia:
> “Who made the deck?” “Who took notes?” moments.
> Instantly relatable. Instantly shareable.
> 📌 Shared experience:
> Not just a snack. A small, laugh-out-loud moment for your team.
> 📌 Desk-to-desk virality:
> No media spend. No boost.
> Just word-of-mouth moving faster than any campaign ever could.
> Result?
> 📍 Cadbury became part of office banter, not just a brand.
> 📍 Relatability turned into engagement, and engagement turned into revenue.
> 📍 A Rs. 10 crore lesson: simple, human moments turn products into culture.
> 86% of consumers prefer authentic brands. Cadbury proved it.
> Not in an ad. On a product people were buying anyway.

## 208. Mark Kosoglow — 4mo

> Everyone has AI. Humans are the differentiators.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7399481361406971905/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> An open letter to GTM tech companies:
> Dear GTM tech CEO, this isn't an easy letter to write.
> I was in GTM tech for 10 years, so I feel like I can tell you this.
> I am noticing something that is making me angry.
> Maybe I'm late to the game, but hear this:
> MY data is *not* YOUR moat.
> I realize that you think my people in your platform creates value.
> Sorry. It doesn't.
> You keep most of the data I create in your platform.
> Our MSA clearly defines the data I put in your tool as MY data.
> You do a few things that add some value to that data.
> Not much, but enough.
> Yet, you refuse to let me take that derivative of my data and use it.
> Unless, my people go to your platform.
> No API. No export. No data share. No CRM sync.
> Trapped. Held hostage. Kidnapped.
> You just went from friend to foe, partner to pain, ally to adversary.
> That's too bad.
> I had this KILLER workflow in mind that would make that data in your platform (ahem...point solution) 10x more valuable to me.
> It makes you totally invisible to my reps, even though they'll use it every day in the place where I want them to do their work.
> But, I can't build it.
> Because you are insecure about me not being in your platform.
> You think I'm dumb.
> That I can't properly attribute value to a tool without "adoption."
> You define adoption as users logging into your platform weekly.
> You conflate adoption with value.
> I can get value even if ZERO reps login.
> You try to make me sit in on a QBR where you show me non-correlative, non-causative data (e.g. your best sellers listened to 2x more calls then your laggards) in an effort to beg me not to churn.
> All you had to do was just let me use my data how, where, and when I want.
> After all, it is MINE.
> You are a GTM tech CEO, not a kidnapper.
> You better start to wise up if you think you data is your moat because it's NOT YOUR DATA, so stop kidnapping it.
> Trust your customers. Trust your product.
> If your customers churn bc users aren't logging in when you are, in fact, providing real value, they'll come back.
> If one of your retention strategies is "we won't put that in the API or sync to SFDC so we can control that data," I'd argue that's a strategy for CHURN, not retention.
> We are in a new, ever-more-connected world.
> Winners are ones who connect systems, workflows, and data into cohesive systems that can be measured, iterated, and optimized.
> You actually PREVENT me from winning by not participating in this new reality.
> Consider this my churn notice if you continue to use my data against me.
> There are tons of new companies that will let me use my data however I please.
> Sure, they are missing some bells and whistles, but they'll get there.
> But, I'll be able to create the GTM machine of my dreams.
> I care more about that, than your slick new AI feature.
> Sincerely,
> Mark Kosoglow
> Recovering Two-Time GTM Tech Rev Leader
> …more
> 128
> 34 comments
> Insightful
> Comment

## 211. Julie Grondin — 4mo

> B2B SaaS PMM, Business Generalist, aspiring Chief of Staff

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7399480628603387905/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Many tech companies have Product Operation roles...but what about Launch Operations?
> If you're thinking 'but that's what PMM does', keep reading...
> I'm looking at Prod/dev orgs and seeing...
> - PMs who define the product/features
> - Devs who build it
> - Program managers or product operation leads managing and coordinating the process to release.
> PMM is doing it all, or attempting to do it all...
> We're defining the launch strategy with SE and campaigns
> We're building the story, materials, campaign assets
> We're managing and coordinating the process to launch
> In addition to this, we're often...
> 1) supporting multiple PMs/product lines
> 2) trying to established operational workflows and work PMM into the overall business operations.
> The value PMM can bring to the table is clear, business know they need the role - but is it actually set up for success based on the catch-all, be all, do all role expectations ?
> I think there is something to a launch operations role to enable PMM to deliver the value the role was intended to deliver...
> Thoughts?

> Your competitors.
> Then it's called competitive intelligence and apparently totally legal.
> So anyway…
> We built this n8n workflow that basically monitors every single person who comments on our competitor's LinkedIn posts and it's
> Pulled in 53 customers this month and
> Added $$$ in new ARR from people who were ALREADY engaging with our biggest rival(s).
> And I'm giving the whole thing away for free because most of you won't actually do anything with it anyway (prove me wrong 💅)
> Here's what this thing does...
> - It watches your competitor's LinkedIn like a hawk.
> - Every time they post something
> - n8n catches it through RSS feeds and webhooks.
> - Within 24 hours we're scraping every single comment using Phantombuster and the LinkedIn API
> - Plus Unipile as backup because sometimes stuff breaks and I'm not dealing with that.
> But here's where it gets good...
> Not every commenter is worth your time right?
> So we filter hardcore based on
> - Engagement depth,
> - Profile completeness,
> - Whether their comment is good or just "thanks for sharing!", then
> - We cross-reference everything against our HubSpot CRM (because accidentally prospecting your existing customers is embarrassing)
> - Enrich company data, role, contact details of every profile through Clay.
> [Swap Clay for Apollo if money is tight (i get it)]
> Now this is where it gets kinda wild...
> We run every single comment through ChatGPT to analyze the sentiment.
> Is this person dropping buying signals?
> Complaining about pain points?
> Sounding low-key frustrated with the competitor?
> The AI catches ALL of it.
> Each prospect gets scored 1-10 based on company fit, role relevance, how good their engagement actually is.
> Only the cream of the crop makes it through.
> Then we auto-generate personalized outreach that mentions their SPECIFIC comment and the competitor context.
> And every morning Slack dumps a full summary of new prospects and conversion stats straight into our channel so we know exactly what's happening.
> This runs 24/7 on autopilot while your competitors unknowingly feed you their audience.
> The numbers:
> - 53 customers acquired from mining comments
> - $20K in fresh ARR from "comment leads" 60%+ email open rates because context is king
> - 45% LinkedIn connection acceptance rate
> - 18% conversion from comment to paying customer 6+ hours saved every single week
> I'm giving the complete
> n8n workflow JSON,
> Message templates,
> Clay scoring formulas

## 226. Divyank Jain — 4mo

> Partner - The Wise Idiot©

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7398370735473246208/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> It is a very fair ask to want a non-toxic boss/manager
> Only if you also notice the toxic employees around you.
> I am an entrepreneur, but I have people around me who work in different setups. They have jobs that are remote, hybrid or in office. And they all have their own jokes, shortcuts and ways of playing the system.
> - They hate being micromanaged, but will watch a full Bigg Boss (or whatever else) episode while taking a meeting.
> - They hate when the boss asks them to switch on the camera, but will work from bed all day.
> - They hate when the boss asks why a tiny edit took seventeen minutes, but will take a seventeen minute break not-so-important-call that they would never have taken in an office.
> These are remote examples.
> In the office, there are people who arrive thirty to fifty minutes late, take time to settle in, start scrolling to order food within an hour, then take another hour to eat while watching a show, then go for a tea break or a stroll. And the moment the clock hits logout time, they are gone.
> A boss/manager sees all of this. First they ignore it, then they think of different ways to fix it, and eventually they burst. Once they reach that point and realise this is the only way that works, they default to that behaviour the next time.
> Every toxic manager has a backstory. It becomes difficult for them to get out of that pattern.
> They are usually pushed into it. There is no good reason to be toxic and I am not promoting it.
> I am simply observing how empathy erodes over time and how those who continue to show it often do so at the cost of their health.
> My rant ends here 🙏

## 227. Yi Lin Pei — 4mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7396603306464350208/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> One of the hardest lessons I learned at work is this:
> If you can’t regulate your emotions, you’ll sabotage your own influence.
> A very wise friend once told me:
> “At work, try to be 90% cold-blooded. Save the warm-blooded energy for your family and your kids.”
> At first I hated that.
> It sounded cold, robotic, even inhuman.
> But the more I observed the leaders I respect the most, like the ones who stay grounded when sh*t hits the fan, the ones other people turn to in the moment things get hard, I realized he was right.
> Lots of things at work can make us emotionally charged, for instance:
> Someone dismisses your idea.
> A stakeholder flip-flops on an issue
> A colleague steals credit for your hard work
> Instantly, your emotional system lights up from annoyance, fear, defensiveness, to rejection.
> While this is extremely human and means we care, our reaction can reduce our objectivity to not act in a way that creates the most productive outcome.
> Instead, if we learn to process those emotions, step outside the moment and see it neutrally, we can more objectively reach the right course of action.
> Interestingly, this is one of the things my clients are most surprised by in my coaching.
> They expect frameworks and templates (and yes, we go over plenty of those).
> But the real work and the largest transformation comes from mastering the soft skills through carefully reflecting and discussing the right course of action.
> So here’s the prompt to take into your week:
> --> Where can you be 10% more cold-blooded today?
> Or in other words, what conversation, decision, or reaction can become easier when you step back?
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #coaching
> hashtag
> #growth
> …more

> 28 comments

> She called leaders to act as the thermostat, not the temperature.
> I don’t think it’s so much about being cold blooded as to being level-headed and regulated so you can always be the one making the smart decisions, not the impulsive ones.
> …more

## 228. Yi Lin Pei — 4mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7396603018181287936/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> One of the hardest lessons I learned at work is this:
> If you can’t regulate your emotions, you’ll sabotage your own influence.
> A very wise friend once told me:
> “At work, try to be 90% cold-blooded. Save the warm-blooded energy for your family and your kids.”
> At first I hated that.
> It sounded cold, robotic, even inhuman.
> But the more I observed the leaders I respect the most, like the ones who stay grounded when sh*t hits the fan, the ones other people turn to in the moment things get hard, I realized he was right.
> Lots of things at work can make us emotionally charged, for instance:
> Someone dismisses your idea.
> A stakeholder flip-flops on an issue
> A colleague steals credit for your hard work
> Instantly, your emotional system lights up from annoyance, fear, defensiveness, to rejection.
> While this is extremely human and means we care, our reaction can reduce our objectivity to not act in a way that creates the most productive outcome.
> Instead, if we learn to process those emotions, step outside the moment and see it neutrally, we can more objectively reach the right course of action.
> Interestingly, this is one of the things my clients are most surprised by in my coaching.
> They expect frameworks and templates (and yes, we go over plenty of those).
> But the real work and the largest transformation comes from mastering the soft skills through carefully reflecting and discussing the right course of action.
> So here’s the prompt to take into your week:
> --> Where can you be 10% more cold-blooded today?
> Or in other words, what conversation, decision, or reaction can become easier when you step back?
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #coaching
> hashtag
> #growth

## 231. Nagarjun Reddy — 4mo

> Sales Leader | Nudge- agentic commerce platform for AI search visibility

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7396303551536222210/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Saw a founder’s post that tried to justify under-paying their SDRs with - "money is not everything".
> but here’s the part nobody says out loud:
> You can only optimize for culture, growth and everything else after your life is financially stable.
> Until then, all the “money isn’t everything” talk is just noise.
> You can’t meditate your way out of rent.
> You can’t manifest your way out of EMIs.
> Psychology calls this the Maslow threshold - you need your basics covered before your brain even has the luxury to care about bigger, softer things.
> And this is where the corporate wisdom gets shady:
> A lot of people get told to “focus on growth, not salary” long before they can afford to.
> That’s not noble advice.
> That’s compensation gaslighting.
> Here’s the real rule nobody teaches you:
> If you’re still building financial stability → optimize for pay.
> Once you’re stable → optimize for whatever actually matters to you.
> Different stage, different lever.
> Anyone who tells you “money shouldn’t matter” is usually someone who already has enough of it.

## 235. Hira Saeed — 5mo

> Product Marketing @Quickbase | GTM Strategy, Launches, and Audience Insights | B2B SaaS | Ex-founder

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7393661617768493057/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Three months in as a Senior PMM in tech. Here are some of my reflections especially for those starting a new role:
> 1. Do not underestimate the power of context. Context is your compass. It shapes how you prioritize, communicate, and even how others perceive your work. The same “launch” or “sales enablement” task can mean radically different things depending on where the business is heading. The sooner you anchor your work to that north star, the easier it becomes to plan and execute.
> 2. A listening tour is more important than you think. I used to think it was just the “honeymoon phase” of a new job, lots of chatting, not much doing. But it completely changed how I approached my work from day one. Curiosity compounds faster than deliverables. Those one-on-one conversations are where I uncovered the real notes on what drives the business.
> 3. The fastest way to ship meaningful work is by building trust with the people who make it real. Context and listening give you direction, but relationships give you lift. The magic happens when everyone feels like you’re on the same side of the table and that you’re on their side too.
> And one last reminder I keep coming back to: Don’t rush to prove yourself through output from day one. Prove yourself through understanding. Learn everyone’s world first. Early clarity and genuine relationships are the invisible work that later looks like “impact.”
> P.S. having an orange furry supervisor next to you helps your mental health a ton, so don't ignore the importance of it.

## 236. Akshaya Sampath — 5mo

> Outbound Architect Building Complex B2B Sales Motions | $5-$20M ARR | Systems Thinker

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7393661427472875520/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> There were days when I really wanted to go back to being salaried.
> I’ve often heard that a job grants you a sense of “security.”
> Because it pays you every month, on the same date, regardless of the storms you’re weathering inside.
> If you’re in sales, you already know that those hefty commissions become your motivation too.
> And while building my business, I craved that kind of certainty.
> I wanted stability. I wanted calm. I wanted to stop worrying about what the next month would look like.
> Every time I paid my team, I felt a deep hole in my pocket.
> Because I didn’t yet understand what it meant to “put money back into the business.”
> Wasn’t this money supposed to be mine?
> So that I could finally feel secure for the next few months?
> Security. It’s such a funny idea.
> You think you’d feel it the most when you have money.
> But I felt it the most when I had the freedom to build.
> Real security comes when you take a risk and think beyond your bubble.
> And I’ve loved every minute of thinking beyond mine.
> I’ve loved the uncertainty, the rebuilding, the slow burn of doing something bigger than myself.
> I didn’t build Skopra from books or borrowed playbooks.
> No frameworks were “inspired”, no templates followed (except maybe a few self-help books to keep morale alive lol).
> I built it on the field, with my clients and my team.
> Fifty milestones earned through a hundred failures.
> Navigating Slack, Trello, Notion, emails. Some days, nothing feels glamorous.
> But it feels right.
> It feels me.
> So much so that I find it hard to separate myself from Skopra, though I’ve been told founders eventually learn to do that.
> Maybe I will too, someday.
> But for now, I feel secure.

## 239. Maddy Hirshan — 5mo

> Strategic Marketing Leader, SaaS & AI @ Wolters Kluwer Health | Driving Product Launches & GTM | MBA Student

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7393660770552594432/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lov**

> Release ≠ Launch ≠ GTM
> 📦 Release: Development ships. Feature exists.
> 📣 Launch: PMM builds a business moment to drive adoption, revenue, and market perception based on PM objectives.
> 🚀 GTM: The commercial engine across all revenue teams. Launches are only one lever. Product marketing is key but hinges on business goals and cross-functional alignment.
> Where smart teams are moving:
> 1️⃣ Fewer, larger launches
> Quarterly or bi-annual packaging of multiple releases into one narrative.
> Stronger signal. Better efficiency. More momentum.
> 2️⃣ Always-on GTM > reactive treadmill
> Lifecycle comms, value-based education, evolving and consistent content strategy, routine enablement.
> Thank you Yi Lin Pei for pulling this into a visual 👊

> Building Executive Authority & Organic Pipeline | 10+ Years in Product Marketing Across B2B SaaS, AI/ML, and Enterprise | Founder @ Dipity
> 5mo
> Here's what most teams get wrong: they think more launches mean more awareness. They don't. Every launch you do trains your audience to wait for the next one instead of adopting what's in front of them. The companies that win don't launch more, they make fewer launches impossible to ignore. Always-on GTM isn't a strategy, it's just doing the work that actually drives adoption instead of throwing release parties nobody attends.

## 244. Yi Lin Pei — 5mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7392124509996355584/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Most positioning and messaging don’t fail because the PMM doesn’t know April Dunford’s framework.  They fail because the work is done in a silo…. which means:
> It never got full buy-in and therefore won’t stick.
> My solution: Make positioning collaborative from Day 1
> To do this, I run a workshop in Miro with the CEO, functional heads (product, sales, CS, marketing), and a few people with deep knowledge of the business.
> Here’s the exact flow (which I map out in a Miro board like below):
> 1️⃣ Start with real customers who love you
> I ask the group: “Who are the customers that get the most value from us?”
> This is where the first debate usually happens.
> ➡️ Some leaders realize their ICP definition is vague.
> ➡️ Others discover they don’t fully agree on the “best-fit” segment.
> That tension is the point. Until we have consensus here, the rest of the messaging will fall apart.
> 2️⃣ Narrow to the champion persona
> Once we agree on the ICP, I ask: “Within that customer, who is our champion?”
> There is usually a discussion here: is it the end user? The budget holder? The exec sponsor? You can’t build sharp positioning without knowing who the champion is.
> 3️⃣ Dig into their jobs-to-be-done
> With the champion persona clear, we go deeper to understand:
> What motivates them?
> What are they trying to achieve?
> What’s blocking them today?
> 4️⃣ Define the competitive alternative
> I ask: “If our product didn’t exist, how would they solve this problem today?”
> The answers might range from another vendor, a manual process, or doing nothing. But picking ONE is critical. You can’t position against everything, so you need a sharp reference point.
> 5️⃣ Surface differentiators
> I ask: “What makes us different from that alternative?”
> This step usually leads to these patterns:
> Some “differentiators” turn out to be table stakes.
> Others are things only one team knew about.
> And a few gems rise to the top as true competitive strengths.
> 6️⃣ Build the value matrix (after the workshop)
> I take the prioritized differentiators and build a value matrix that includes:
> Attribute → Capability → Benefit → Business Outcome
> This connects the dots from product details to the impact the champion actually cares about.
> 7️⃣ Create the messaging house
> Finally, I synthesize everything into a messaging house:
> ICP & champion persona
> Key challenges / jobs-to-be-done
> Differentiators & value pillars
> Proof points & business outcomes
> This document becomes the foundation for all GTM content. And because it came out of a collaborative workshop, the whole company feels ownership of it.
> —---------------
> I’ve run this process at every startup I’ve worked at, and now I coach my clients on the same.
> I also find using tools like timers, voting, and sticky notes in Miro makes this easy. If you haven’t tried, start a free Miro board and test one with your team. (Link in comments).
> P.S. How do you run positioning & messaging at your company?
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #positioning
> hashtag
> #strategy
> hashtag
> #miro
> hashtag
> #Mi

## 245. Akshaya Sampath — 5mo

> Outbound Architect Building Complex B2B Sales Motions | $5-$20M ARR | Systems Thinker

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7392107121703690241/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> I wanted to build the business before I even had a website.
> I had heard all the whispers around “stealth mode.”
> In all honesty, it’s brutally hard to build something without external validation.
> But that’s exactly what I needed to do.
> Because this company is born out of the failures.
> When I started Mindful Selling in 2024,
> My goal was to train early-stage SaaS founders and sales teams to sell better.
> But a few projects in, I realised training wasn’t the real problem.
> The problem was everything around it.
> The broken systems.
> The lack of hiring frameworks.
> The unclear ICPs.
> The blind “let’s just hire SDRs and get revenue in” approach.
> I had seen too many founders burn their VC money on outbound.
> That’s when I understood that sales training is just 10% of the equation.
> If your outbound system is broken, no amount of training can fix it.
> So I went back to zero.
> For the last 12 months, I worked with founders across different stages as a partner.
> I sat in their chaos.
> I built and dissolved their teams.
> We broke things, then fixed things.
> I’ve failed too many times in this process. Perhaps that’s why I was in stealth mode.
> Because who comes on LinkedIn saying, “Hey, I failed miserably today”?
> But those failures forced me to grab the whiteboard and find an answer.
> And the answer is Skopra. A full-stack outbound partner that helps B2B SaaS companies validate, build, and scale outbound as a competitive advantage.
> Today, I finally take the title I once wrote about — "Founder & CEO", Skopra.
> P.S: the website is live and it’s everything we’ve been building quietly for the past year :) link in comments.

> HubSpot: $319K per employee
> Atlassian: $359K per employee
> Storylane: $250K per employee (and rising)
> They’re publicly traded companies with thousands of employees.
> We’re 40 people. Angelstrapped. Profitable. Big Dream.
> Most startups raise $5M and immediately hire 20 people in San Francisco.
> We took the opposite path.
> Here's how we've stayed lean:
> → I only hire when my team tells me they're drowning
> Until then, we optimize processes and remove inefficiencies.
> → We hire generalists who can wear multiple hats
> Startups need people who take ownership across functions.
> → We're remote from day one
> We built to $10M ARR without burning cash on vanity.
> → We put our own money in
> When it’s your own capital, you learn discipline fast.
> Revenue per employee is one of the clearest signals of operational excellence.

## 257. Holly Allen — 6mo

> Founding AE @ FullEnrich 🚀 ask me about how to get a 14%+ connect rate on cold calls ☎️

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7387468365633679360/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 5 signs that bad data is holding your sales team back 👇
> 👉 Your SDRs are dialing harder, but connecting less.
> If activity is going up but conversations are going down, that’s a sign your contact data is outdated or inaccurate. Every missed connection wastes time, tanks morale, and slows down your pipeline velocity.
> 👉 You’re losing deals to faster competitors.
> When a buyer finally opens your email, they’re already deep in conversations with your competitor, who got their mobile number and is now on whatsapp terms with them.
> 👉 Your SDRs are demotivated and burning out.
> When reps feel like “nothing’s working,” it’s often not their effort, it’s their inputs. Poor-quality data means they’re spending their energy chasing ghosts instead of building meaningful conversations.
> 👉 You’re losing big, late-stage deals because you can’t multithread effectively.
> You’ve got the right strategy, but without accurate, real-time contact data, you can’t get the right people involved when it matters most.
> 👉 You’re seeing lower ROI on your go-to-market spend.
> From email platforms to dialers and enrichment tools... every system depends on your data. If that foundation is cracked, your ROI across the entire sales stack takes a hit. You’re not just losing connects, you’re wasting budget.
> The harsh truth is this - Even the best sales teams can’t win with bad data. ☠️
> …more

> Activate to view larger image,

## 259. Richard King — 6mo

> Talking truth on leadership, growth & product marketing | 5x founder | 3x exits |

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7383457745246539777/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> GTM is the most stressful part of product marketing.
> You're juggling:
> - Product timelines that slip.
> - Sales asking for ‘one more asset’
> - Execs wanting ‘bigger impact.’
> - Customer success hearing about it last.
> All while running toward a launch date that was set three months ago.
> And somehow, YOU'RE supposed to hold it all together.
> The truth?
> Most GTM stress comes from two things:
> 1️⃣ Managing people who don't naturally work together
> 2️⃣ Using tools that weren't built to work together
> That’s why launches spiral.
> The ones that actually work?
> ✅ Product and sales in the same room early
> ✅ Shared visibility into timelines, blockers, and signals
> ✅ Saying no without burning bridges
> ✅ Measuring impact across every stage of launch
> That’s both people and data work.
> It takes soft skills 👇
> Negotiation, active listening, managing ego (VERY important)
> But it also takes hard signals 👇
> Conversion data, engagement trends, pipeline movement (to know what’s working and what’s not.)
> Soft skills build alignment.
> Signals sustain it.
> 📌 This Thursday (October 9th), HubSpot’s GTM leaders are breaking it all down - how they plan, execute, report, and keep stakeholders aligned. 👉
> Register here: https://hubs.la/Q03LDz0K0

## 267. Daniel Zarate — 6mo

> Helping Businesses Scale with Ads + Content Marketing

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7381777634281709568/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I got tired of marketing fluff and free trials... so I asked AI which software actually works.
> Last week, I used ChatGPT to compare 4 VoIP platforms for:
> ⭐ Ease of Use | 💬 Support | 🔗 Integrations | 💰 Pricing | ⚙️ Features
> In under 10 minutes, I had verified insights, ratings, and comparison tables,
> Just real data from real customers.
> It felt like having your own software research analyst on the team.
> 🎥 Here’s the full video: https://bit.ly/3ILradu
> I’m sharing the exact framework I use, so you can bring it into your own team and make faster, smarter software decisions:
> ________________________________________
> Example Prompt:
> You are my Software Research Analyst.
> TASK:
> Compare the following tools: [ADD TOOL A], [TOOL B], [TOOL C], [TOOL D].
> These are [TYPE OF TOOL — e.g., CRMs, VoIP systems, project management tools].
> GOAL:
> I’m evaluating them for [ADD USE CASE — e.g., a small remote sales team, a customer support department, a marketing agency].
> Focus on what matters most to [ADD TEAM TYPE — SMBs, startups, mid-size companies].
> Use verified customer reviews (G2, Capterra, TrustRadius, Reddit) and vendor documentation.
> Prioritize recent reviews (past 12–18 months).
> Include 1–2 short verified quotes per platform with source links when possible.
> 🧭 Ease of Use
> Rank the ease of use for each tool. Describe:
> - Setup & onboarding time
> - UI clarity (desktop & mobile)
> - Learning curve for new users
> - How intuitive and SMB-friendly it is
> Highlight which tools can be fully set up in under 1 hour.
> Give each a **1–5⭐ rating (decimals allowed)** and include a comparison table with setup speed and ease summary.
> 🏁 Final Output
> Summarize all sections in a clean, scannable format:
> 1️⃣ 3–5 bullet executive summary
> 2️⃣ Ratings tables (⭐ decimals allowed)
> 3️⃣ Comparison summaries per category
> 4️⃣ 1–2 verified review quotes per tool
> 5️⃣ Final recommendation for the best overall option and why
> hashtag
> #SAAS
> hashtag
> #AiForBusiness
> hashtag
> #ProductivityTools
> hashtag
> #ChatGPTPrompts
> hashtag
> #SoftwareComparison
> hashtag

## 278. Christian Oland — 6mo

> Founder of RevGen Labs | Helping businesses unlock 10x growth through growth hacking and AI

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7378279287180705793/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I replaced 6 sales reps with one n8n workflow for a client.
> They have not only improved turnaround times but got better performance as well.
> Here's how:
> There sales team was living in 2015.
> Following the same old sales prospecting methods.
> I know deep research takes FOREVER.
> Teams can spend 2-3 days digging into each target company...
> Only to create campaigns that miss the mark (I know the feeling).
> And the worst part?
> Even after all that manual work, your reply rates are trash.
> So we built this n8n workflow that automated our entire GTM process:
> Step 1: Google Sheets trigger pulls new company domains and validates entries
> Step 2: Perplexity AI performs deep company research - pulls ICP analysis, pain points, competitive landscape, and market positioning
> Step 3: Claude processes research data and generates detailed company overview with target audience profiles
> Step 4: AI creates 5 differentiated campaign angles - each with specific targeting criteria and pain point mapping
> Step 5: System generates 3 compelling CTAs and lead magnets tailored to each company's GTM strategy
> Step 6: Claude writes 10 complete email sequences (2 per campaign angle) with both long and short variants plus subject lines
> Step 7: AI identifies static and dynamic lead sources - provides 10 specific scraping sources tailored to the ICP
> Step 8: Google Drive creates personalized strategy document with all campaign data, email sequences, and lead sources
> Step 9: System auto-delivers completed GTM strategy docs and updates tracking sheets
> It's a 31-step workflow, but I've compressed it into 9 steps just for you.
> Want the exact workflow? Comment "DOC" below and I'll DM you.
> P.S. Since this is something we use daily, I'm not going to send this to more than 200 people.

## 281. Kabir Uppal — 6mo

> 👉🏼 Growth & GTM Strategy | SaaS & AI | Revenue, Partnerships and Ops Leader. I help build and scale GTM Engines to drive pipeline and revenue...✨

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7377321798951997442/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Most GTM strategies fail because they're built for 2020, not 2025.
> I’ve been fortunate to speak to 50+ founders and operators in 2025 about their GTM. Here's what actually works when buyers have changed, budgets are tight, and your engineering team is drowning in technical debt that's killing your sales velocity.
> 73% of GTM leaders say their biggest challenge isn't finding prospects, it's getting their own internal systems to work together.
> Your sales team promises features that engineering can't deliver on time. Your marketing team drives leads that your CRM can't properly track. Your customer success team fights fires that better technical execution could prevent.
> Sound familiar?
> After working with 20+ growth-stage companies since the start 2024, here are the 5 strategies that separate winners from the "we'll figure it out next quarter" crowd:
> 1. Audit Your Technical GTM Stack
> Stop layering tools on broken foundations. I watched a VP of Marketing spend $180K on a new marketing automation platform while their basic lead routing was broken. Fix your data flow before scaling your outbound.
> 2. Align Engineering Roadmaps with Sales Promises
> Make your CTO and Head of Sales best friends. Revenue dies in the gap between "we can build that" and "when exactly?" One product company increased their close rate 34% just by syncing product releases with sales commitments.
> 3. Build Buyer-Centric Content, Not Feature Lists
> Your prospects don't care about your API. They care about sleeping better at night. The best performing content I've seen focuses on business outcomes, not technical specifications. Remember what your buyer persona’s pain points are.
> 4. Create Cross-Functional Revenue Accountability
> Marketing, Sales, CS, and Product should share the same revenue number. Not just "pipeline contribution." When everyone owns the full customer lifecycle, magic happens.
> 5. Measure GTM Velocity, Not Just Volume
> Track how fast prospects move through your funnel, not how many you stuff into it. Velocity metrics reveal bottlenecks that volume metrics hide.
> Many founders and operators optimize for looking busy instead of being effective. They add more tools, more campaigns, more meetings, when what they really need is better alignment between their technical execution and their go-to-market strategy.
> …more

> That changes a lot in the B2B space. Because suddenly, a SaaS that only solves one problem isn’t enough.
> > SMBs and mid-market teams don’t want 5 different subscriptions. They’d rather build their own hyper-specific tool that solves their exact need.
> > Enterprises will still pay for the “best in class” SaaS for one thing, but that’s not most of the market.
> I’ve seen this inside Factors.ai too. My own teammates are hacking together their own mini-solutions for keyword research, attribution, and even note-taking.
> Now, as a SaaS founder myself, I know that building a winning product is a lot harder than piecing together a no-code app. But the threat is real.
> If your SaaS just records data and shows dashboards, people will slowly start to walk away.
> You need to offer more value beyond the core product or risk being replaced by a lunch-break project.

## 284. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 6mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7377319791327502336/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I just spent 10 minutes getting competitive messaging intel that used to take me an hour. Here's what I did:
> [1] Downloaded Claude Desktop.
> [2] Added Brave Search and Puppeteer MCP servers.
> [3] Wrote a prompt listing out my competitors.
> [4] Specified outputs like headlines, value props, imagery etc.
> [5] Got back organized results in minutes.
> [For context: MCP is short for model context protocol - it allows AI assistants like Claude to plug into external tools and apps]
> In the example in the video, I asked Claude to analyze Sendgrid against Mailchimp and Brevo. I wanted hero headlines, key value props, pricing approaches, and visual themes.
> Then, Claude went to work.
> It used the Puppeteer tool and visited each site, captured screenshots, scraped text, and organized everything into a clean comparison table.
> To be sure, I then compared the results to the actual websites. Apart from a few minor hiccups, it mostly got everything right.
> This cuts out a lot of the grunt work. No more opening tabs, taking notes, trying to remember what competitor X said about feature Y.
> Since competitors change their home pages every once in a while, you can save the prompt and re-run the process whenever you need to.
> Worth trying if you're tired of repetitive tasks like these.
> P.S. yes, you could use ChatGPT o3 or Agent in many cases, but the advantage of a headless browser like Puppeteer is that it handles dynamic websites with a lot of Javascript, infinity scrolls & consent walls pretty well. You can also have it spawn a browser for you to help it through complex captchas. These days SaaS sites tend to get fancy :)

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7377173293860184064/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **celebrates**

> If Focus is the ultimate competitive advantage, today we're doubling down on it.
> Over the past year, we've been building game-changing AI-powered products at Klenty— from parallel and predictive dialers to SDRx (our AI SDR platform) and SchedX.
> Our mission has always been focused: leverage technology and AI to help sales professionals create meaningful conversations, drive revenue, and boost productivity across industries.
> SchedX was our most recent product launch in that journey - conversational AI voice agents for sales.
> And over the last few months, we started to see something big happen.
> We started seeing serious demand from the healthcare sector with a number of healthcare providers and payers deploying our AI voice agents to solve complex challenges around patient/ member engagement, scheduling, drive revenue and boost profitability.
> It was clear that to support this demand - would require a very different kind of vertical AI company. From team to capital to product to GTM - we would need to build a strong vertically integrated platform focused on Healthcare.
> With this goal, we have decided to spin out the Healthcare product as its own entity and give it a separate identity, life and capital of its own.
> Introducing VoxyHealth, vertically integrated AI voice agents built for Healthcare patient and member engagement. I will be taking on the role of CEO of Voxy Health, while continuing to provide guidance to Klenty from a board level.
> And Praveen Kumar (my better looking half at Klenty 😉 ) will drive Klenty forward with a sharp focus on AI Driven Sales engagement.

## 293. Anthony Pierri — 7mo

> B2B Homepage Positioning // I play 🎸 in the band Good Hangs

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7373952008258723840/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> Most employees don't lie awake worrying about their company's revenue.
> The remit of a security director likely doesn't include "increase revenue for the business."
> These managers and directors are usually the most likely champion for a B2B sale... and yet we constantly market past them.
> The reason is simple:
> → when a deal dies because the final decision-maker didn't believe the ROI story, we (mistakenly) believe the solution is to talk about revenue EARLIER in the journey.
> But this is fundamentally backwards.
> When executives don't believe the revenue story, it's not because you didn't put it in the H1 of your website hero.
> The reason they didn't believe it is because your company lacks credibility and has little to no brand equity.
> Because you aren't well-known, you are perceived as a risk to adopt.
> And how do executives try to reduce the risk?
> They demand that you produce a believable story around ROI and revenue generation.
> But again, this is putting the cart before the horse.
> The way to fix this problem is to generate more brand equity and credibility — and the way to do THAT is by winning over more and more champions.
> You earn the right to the c-suite through mass love and adoration of the champion-level role.
> And when you discount the champion by only speaking the language of the C-suite, you end up alienating the very people who will create the brand equity you need to close the sale.
> The champion has been tasked to find a solution.
> They are the ones who shop.
> They are the ones you need to convince FIRST.
> Their blessing is what gets the C-suite to join the conversation.

> on why you should pick and name your enemy
> 👇
> "Why can't we just talk about us?"
> "Why can't we just say why we're great?"
> "Why do we need to bring up the competition at all?"
> We get a version of this question very frequently when running positioning sprints with software companies.
> Interestingly enough, certain regions are less down with calling out what they seek to replace (mainly countries in Europe).
> Others have no problem with it (cough, cough, the U.S.)
> But there is a certain level of power in naming an enemy.
> Salesforce did this famously with their "end of software" campaign.
> And there are still plenty of companies today that have adopted the practice — naming their own respective villains, whether they be specific vendors, product categories, or broken processes.
> If you're going to try it, here's a rule of thumb:
> → the more specific the better
> It has to be something people can rally against, like the "next steps email" with Arrows or "terrible meetings" like with Loom.
> Even though your software replaces lots of things, it can really move the needle to place the problem on ONE of those things specifically."
> --

## 301. Dharmesh Shah — 7mo

> Founder and CTO at HubSpot. Helping millions grow better.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7372151908330844160/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> BREAKING NEWS: Seems like OpenAI may have come up with a way to dramatically reduce the hallucinations in AI models.
> Hallucinations are when the AI makes up things that it *thinks* are true -- but just aren't.
> The solution was brilliantly simple. So simple, I'm surprised we didn't come up with it sooner.
> Here's a simple explanation: When AI models go through training, there's a "reward" function. When it gets something "right", it gets a +1, and it gets a 0 when it doesn't. Over time, it optimizes for getting things right and maximizing the "reward".
> That makes sense. One issue though. There's no penalty for getting things wrong. So, if it's optimizing for the highest "reward", its best strategy is to take a guess when it doesn't really know. Even if it's likely wrong, might as well take a shot at it. Worst case, it gets zero. Best case, it MIGHT guess correctly.
> That's why standardized tests (like the SAT) have a penalty for wrong answers. They want to remove the benefit of simply guessing.
> So, the idea: Create the right incentive for the AI models NOT to just guess when it does not have high confidence and *reward* the model (partial credit) when it says it doesn't know.
> Will be interesting to see what impact this actually has.
> If it works, this could be a major improvement in the quality of results we get and reduces the risk of getting inaccurate/wrong information.
> …more

## 306. Kesava Mandiga found Sourav Mohanty’s comment on this insightful — 7mo

> The AI Product Marketer

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7371527208772964356/) · type: `activity`

> Happy Monday PMMs! For this week's
> hashtag
> #PromptOfTheWeek, I want to talk about a new button that was released on ChatGPT (on Sept 3) 👀. I heard about this from Nicole Leffer over the weekend, and she got me PUMPED!
> It's called "Branch in new chat" (web-only version).  Think parallel universes for your work. Your context (prompts, uploads, images, even Deep Research results) comes along, so you can spin off focused workstreams without wrecking the main thread.
> This means you can create clear, reusable branches so you can scale strategy → assets without chaos.
> What I am seeing (so far) as some best practices for Product Marketers
> 1) Use a stable taxonomy
> Adopt short prefixes: STR (strategy), MSG (messaging), PER (persona), VRT (vertical), CMP (competitive), VOC (voice of customer), AR (analyst relations), STG (funnel stage), PRC (pricing), LCH (launch), CNT (content), SLS (sales enablement) etc. It makes search/filters easier to understand and identify. Also easier for your custom GPTs to reference.
> 2) One purpose per branch
> If you catch yourself doing two different jobs (e.g., persona + channel), split them. Branches are cheap, confusion is expensive.
> 3) Keep a “Base Context” branch
> Pin a 5-bullet summary, glossary, proofs, ICP, and links to the master brief. Clone this for new workstreams.
> 4) Version & status in the title
> v1.0 DRAFT, v1.1 REVIEW, v1.2 APPROVED. You’ll thank yourself on approvals week. (but also, lol. Anyone else triggered? 😂 )
> 5) Attach the right inputs
> At the top, list “Inputs to carry forward”: core narrative, proof library, brand rules, design tokens, legal/compliance notes.
> 6) Add a reusable QA checklist
> Accuracy, claims & proof, voice/tone, CTA clarity, offer fit, compliance, links/UTM, localization, competitive landmines. You CAN create a brand compliance GPT, but this might work just as well, stay tuned on that.
> 7) Don’t branch for micro-tweaks
> Use inline edits for wording - branch for new audiences, channels, hypotheses, or deliverables.
> 8) Archive aggressively
> Delete dead ends or move to ARCHIVE | … to keep search clean.
> 9) Log learnings
> Close each branch with a short “What worked / What to change next time.” Feed wins back into the Base Context.
> 10) Protect sensitive info
> House competitive, pricing, and customer-identifying details in clearly labeled branches; control sharing scope.
> If you want to see how I'm trying to organize my branched chats, just send me a DM and I'd be happy to share!
> For example:
> Strategy & Narrative
> STR | Narrative | Product Vision v1
> STR | Launch Brief | Q4 Major Release
> Why: Keeps long-horizon thinking separate from execution threads.
> Positioning & Messaging
> MSG | Positioning | Core vs Alt
> MSG | Boilerplate | 100/250/600w
> Why: Lock the story before you scale assets; preserve versions for stakeholders.
> Funnel Stage
> STG | TOFU | Problem Education
> STG | MOFU | Comparison Guide
> STG | BOFU | ROI Calculator
> Why: Ensures content ladders logically toward a conversion.
> …more

## 307. Michele Nieberding 🚀 — 7mo

> The AI Product Marketer

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7371527161058480128/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Happy Monday PMMs! For this week's
> hashtag
> #PromptOfTheWeek, I want to talk about a new button that was released on ChatGPT (on Sept 3) 👀. I heard about this from Nicole Leffer over the weekend, and she got me PUMPED!
> It's called "Branch in new chat" (web-only version).  Think parallel universes for your work. Your context (prompts, uploads, images, even Deep Research results) comes along, so you can spin off focused workstreams without wrecking the main thread.
> This means you can create clear, reusable branches so you can scale strategy → assets without chaos.
> What I am seeing (so far) as some best practices for Product Marketers
> 1) Use a stable taxonomy
> Adopt short prefixes: STR (strategy), MSG (messaging), PER (persona), VRT (vertical), CMP (competitive), VOC (voice of customer), AR (analyst relations), STG (funnel stage), PRC (pricing), LCH (launch), CNT (content), SLS (sales enablement) etc. It makes search/filters easier to understand and identify. Also easier for your custom GPTs to reference.
> 2) One purpose per branch
> If you catch yourself doing two different jobs (e.g., persona + channel), split them. Branches are cheap, confusion is expensive.
> 3) Keep a “Base Context” branch
> Pin a 5-bullet summary, glossary, proofs, ICP, and links to the master brief. Clone this for new workstreams.
> 4) Version & status in the title
> v1.0 DRAFT, v1.1 REVIEW, v1.2 APPROVED. You’ll thank yourself on approvals week. (but also, lol. Anyone else triggered? 😂 )
> 5) Attach the right inputs
> At the top, list “Inputs to carry forward”: core narrative, proof library, brand rules, design tokens, legal/compliance notes.
> 6) Add a reusable QA checklist
> Accuracy, claims & proof, voice/tone, CTA clarity, offer fit, compliance, links/UTM, localization, competitive landmines. You CAN create a brand compliance GPT, but this might work just as well, stay tuned on that.
> 7) Don’t branch for micro-tweaks
> Use inline edits for wording - branch for new audiences, channels, hypotheses, or deliverables.
> 8) Archive aggressively
> Delete dead ends or move to ARCHIVE | … to keep search clean.
> 9) Log learnings
> Close each branch with a short “What worked / What to change next time.” Feed wins back into the Base Context.
> 10) Protect sensitive info
> House competitive, pricing, and customer-identifying details in clearly labeled branches; control sharing scope.
> If you want to see how I'm trying to organize my branched chats, just send me a DM and I'd be happy to share!
> For example:
> Strategy & Narrative
> STR | Narrative | Product Vision v1
> STR | Launch Brief | Q4 Major Release
> Why: Keeps long-horizon thinking separate from execution threads.
> Positioning & Messaging
> MSG | Positioning | Core vs Alt
> MSG | Boilerplate | 100/250/600w
> Why: Lock the story before you scale assets; preserve versions for stakeholders.
> Funnel Stage
> STG | TOFU | Problem Education
> STG | MOFU | Comparison Guide
> STG | BOFU | ROI Calculator
> Why: Ensures content ladders logically toward a conversion.
> …more

## 313. Hamna Aslam Kahn — 7mo

> Follow me to get insights on how to use AI at work and beyond. Join the world’s biggest AI newsletter with 1M+ readers ↓

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7370853209428414464/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 🚨 Microsoft's CEO revealed his secret AI prompts.
> 5 prompts Satya uses every day:
> 1. Meeting prep that actually works:
> "Based on my prior interactions with [person], give me 5 things likely top of mind for our next meeting."
> This is brilliant because it uses your conversation history to predict what someone wants to talk about. No more awkward "so... what did you want to discuss?" moments.
> 2. Project status without the BS:
> "Draft a project update based on emails, chats, and all meetings in [series]: KPIs vs. targets, wins/losses, risks, competitive moves, plus likely tough questions and answers."
> Instead of relying on people to give you sugar-coated updates, the AI pulls from actual communications to give you the real picture.
> 3. Reality check on deadlines:
> "Are we on track for the [Product] launch in November? Check eng progress, pilot program results, risks. Give me a probability."
> Love this one. It's asking for an actual probability rather than just "yeah we're on track" (which usually means "probably not but I don't want to be the bearer of bad news").
> 4. Time audit:
> "Review my calendar and email from the last month and create 5 to 7 buckets for projects I spend most time on, with % of time spent and short descriptions."
> This could be eye-opening for anyone who feels like they're always busy but can't figure out what they're actually accomplishing.
> 5. Never get blindsided again:
> "Review [select email] + prep me for the next meeting in [series], based on past manager and team discussions."
> Basically turns your AI into a briefing assistant that knows the full context of ongoing conversations.
> What's interesting about this:
> The guy is basically using AI as a "digital chief of staff" - something most of us could probably benefit from even if we're not running a Fortune 500 company.
> These aren't just generic ChatGPT prompts, but they're pulling from integrated data across his entire workspace.

## 325. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 7mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7368720131788562432/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Release notes ≠ Changelogs.
> But when product teams use them interchangeably, readers get a bit lost.
> Changelogs are technical records usually for developers and power users. Think detailed bug fixes, API changes, and version tracking.
> Release notes are curated stories for your actual users. They highlight what matters to customers in language they understand. Much like a product update blog.
> When I read release notes of products I subscribe to, they tend to include superfluous details better left for a changelog.
> Some notable differences:
> AUDIENCE
> Changelogs → speak to developers and power users.
> Release notes → speak to end users and stakeholders.
> FORMAT
> Changelogs → use plain text and technical details.
> Release notes → can include screenshots, GIFs, and marketing copy.
> SCOPE
> Changelogs → include granular changes.
> Release notes → bundle significant updates.
> TONE
> Changelogs → neutral and factual.
> Release notes → can be conversational and engaging.
> I'm a big proponent of PMMs writing solid release notes. They aid feature discovery and keep relevant customers informed. If PMMs message them right, these updates avoid being noisy or preachy.
> Two important notes that make a world of difference:
> [1] Packaging
> Bundle related features thematically. If you launch a reporting feature today and three others come at the end of the month, I'd wait and bundle them. Disconnected features become unwieldy to remember.
> [2] Segmentation
> Not every update suits every user. Consider admins versus regular users, different use cases, and varying pricing tiers. Route the correct update to the right person to increase read-rates.
> By the way, changelog and release note production used to be a hassle. With AI tools, they've become far easier.
> There are products that now hook into your JIRA and craft the updates you need with the right context. PMMs can use tools like Glean, ReleaseNotes, or Canny to help with that.
> Tldr:
> Use changelogs to track minor and major updates.
> Use release notes to educate & engage.
> …more

> Activate to view larger image,

> ↳ Accuracy, precision, recall, F1 score
> ↳ Tells how good an AI model really is
> ↳ Use for: ROI claims, technical messaging
> 𝐇𝐮𝐦𝐚𝐧-𝐢𝐧-𝐭𝐡𝐞-𝐋𝐨𝐨𝐩 (𝐇𝐈𝐓𝐋)

> ↳ Human approves or supervises the system
> ↳ Use for: Addressing job-displacement fears
> 𝐈𝐧𝐟𝐞𝐫𝐞𝐧𝐜𝐞
> ↳ Model making predictions on new data
> ↳ E.g., photo upload → AI detects objects
> ↳ Use for: Real-time capabilities messaging
> 𝐇𝐚𝐥𝐥𝐮𝐜𝐢𝐧𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐨𝐧
> ↳ When AI makes things up

> ↳ Processes text, audio, video together

> Some of the most useful ones:
> 𝐀𝐈 𝐜𝐚𝐥𝐥 𝐫𝐞𝐯𝐢𝐞𝐰 𝐟𝐨𝐫 𝐝𝐞𝐚𝐥𝐬

> → short, research-backed, and persona-specific - converts more prospects into conversations.

> → identifies bottlenecks, strengthens forecasting, and produces an actionable roadmap.
> These are the rules for prompting with GPT-5:
> 1/ Identity anchoring
> “You are” > “Act as.” Lock the model into a role.
> 2/ Section headers with hierarchy
> # Role, ## Task, ## Context → GPT-5 follows structure like an outline.
> 3/ Rules section
> Set non-negotiables: exclude fluff, fact-check data, avoid filler.
> 4/ Separate reasoning from output
> First tell GPT-5 how to think, then how to format.
> Want the full library of 200+ prompts?
> UPDATE: Due to overwhelming interest, I cannot send the prompts directly to everyone. Grab them for free here: https://lnkd.in/dRyFY7uE

> How to write (better) with AI.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7366859942646964224/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> People think I don't (really) use AI for Linkedin.
> So here's a non-edited screen recording of me 1) generating a viral topic 2) a caption 3) an image
> ☑ This strategy can be replicated in any industry.
> ☑ You are still the one controlling the input/output.
> ☑ You use AI as a tool - not as a one-hit solution.
> Absolutely no one complains about "AI-made".
> Because there is no such thing as "AI-made".
> There are GOOD posts and BAD posts.
> Here's my simple step-by-step process:
> ☑ Go to the new ChatGPT-5.
> ☑ Copy & paste the following prompt:
> "You are an expert at finding topics that go viral on Linkedin.
> Search the web deeply, and find 6x drastically different topics (with a title and a controversial twist attached) about "TOPIC".
> Your research must be tied to the news: we are in August, 2025.
> ☑ Select your favorite topic from the list.
> ☑ Ask for a follow-up prompt to ChatGPT.
> ☑ Prompt it to give more details/data about it.
> ☑ Once you're happy, copy the optimized topic.
> ☑ Paste it on app.easygen.io (the best for Linkedin)
> ☑ Generate at very least 3 to 7 different posts.
> ☑ Patchwork them into one mega post.
> ☑ Edit it. Add your own spice!
> Here you have it:
> 1. The right topic, selected for virality.
> 2. The right caption, generated & edited.
> Now the little bonus is to generate an image.
> Ask EasyGen to do so (it's 20 cents per image).
> ☑ Select the right quote.
> ☑ Generate an image using the quote.
> ☑ Download it and upload it on Linkedin.
> That's an entire "AI-made" post from A to Z.
> But AI was not used to be lazier, but greater.

## 341. Sachin Jha — 7mo

> Founder ONEGTMLab | Engineering GTM for Cyber and DevTools | Product Marketing, AI GTM Engineering, and Founder-Led Growth Expert | 2X Top 100 Product Marketing Influencers

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7365948967525761025/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> $18,000 AI in 17 minutes.
> That’s how fast you can spin up an AI workflow today.
> But here’s the part nobody talks about: if execution takes 17 minutes, then
> strategy, the thinking behind it is what makes it valuable.
> Because AI isn’t just:
> ⇒ memorizing GPT prompts,
> ⇒ name-dropping tools like Lovable, n8n or Relevance AI,
> ⇒ or showing off a stitched workflow.
> It’s about taking a messy business problem and turning it into a system that works.
> That’s why last weekend I didn’t spend time scrolling tool lists. I gave myself a harder challenge:
> can I stitch 5 tasks into one working agent that does actual PMM work?
> The goal
> → automate the BuyerBrain deep research + messaging exercise: a process every Product Marketer knows, but rarely has time to go deep on.
> Here’s what that looked like:
> → Started in Figma: mapped purpose, value, design, and what “good” outputs should look like.
> → Experimented with Relevance, n8n, Firecrawl: pushing them to talk to each other.
> → Built the BuyerBrain research + Messaging Agent with 5 sub-agents that:
> ⤷  Mine the voice of customer
> ⤷  Map buyer reality
> ⤷  Build POV
> ⤷  Bust objections
> ⤷  Draft core messaging
> Went deeper: wrote schemas, tested outputs with “Base44” as a sample company.
> Yes, the build was fast.
> But the strategy, the schemas, the logic, the decisions — that’s the real moat.
> The lesson I keep coming back to:
> ⇒ Prompts alone won’t help.
> ⇒ Tool names won’t help.
> ⇒ Being “AI-forward” won’t help.
> What will help is learning how to:
> Connect strategy → workflows
> Think before building
> Treat AI as a system, not a shortcut
> This is the shift.
> Execution is becoming commoditized.
> But thinking? That’s the part AI won’t do for you.
> And the PMMs (and operators, founders, builders) who master that layer — they’re the ones who’ll win.

> Here’s a full Product Marketing learning sprint using ChatGPT, Claude, and NotebookLM
> 𝐒𝐭𝐞𝐩 𝟏: Learn the Foundations (ChatGPT / Claude)
> 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐭:
> “Give me a 30-day plan to learn Product Marketing in the age of AI.
> Break it into 4 weekly themes with clear concepts, examples, and exercises.”
> You’ll get a full roadmap covering:
> → ICPs, segmentation, messaging, enablement
> → Competitive analysis, positioning frameworks
> → GTM strategies & feedback loops
> 📌 Pro tip: Upload the full roadmap to NotebookLM and ask:
> “Turn this into a mind map with links between topics so I can learn visually.”

> 𝐔𝐧𝐝𝐞𝐫𝐬𝐭𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐁𝐮𝐬𝐢𝐧𝐞𝐬𝐬 𝐌𝐨𝐝𝐞𝐥𝐬

> 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐭:
> “Give me a messy customer interview transcript for a B2B SaaS product.
> My task is to pull out the pain points and draft 3 positioning statements.
> After I submit my draft, grade it using April Dunford’s positioning framework.”
> 𝐁𝐮𝐢𝐥𝐝 𝐚 𝐆𝐓𝐌 𝐏𝐥𝐚𝐧
> 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐭:
> “Act as a PMM mentor. Guide me step-by-step to create a GTM plan for a new AI productivity tool.
> Include: ICP, messaging, launch motion, enablement, and post-launch feedback loops.”

> 𝐏𝐫𝐨𝐦𝐩𝐭:
> “Give me product marketing level 2 interview prep for the company [insert company].
> The person interviewing me is [insert role].
> Prepare me for a deep round that tests GTM strategy, positioning, ICP thinking, and product intuition.”
> Ask it to simulate a mock interview.
> Then ask it to grade your answers and give feedback.

> ⇒ Use 𝐂𝐡𝐚𝐭𝐆𝐏𝐓/𝐂𝐥𝐚𝐮𝐝𝐞 as your teacher, coach, and mock interviewer.

> ⇒ Combine 𝐥𝐞𝐚𝐫𝐧𝐢𝐧𝐠 + 𝐝𝐨𝐢𝐧𝐠 every week for 30 days.

> → Sent the right message to the right person at the right time.

> Step 1️⃣: Signal Detection
> We used Clay + Clearbit to monitor:
> → LinkedIn job changes
> → Website activity
> → Recent funding
> → Tech installs
> → Post engagement

> Clay +
> hashtag
> #Apollo stacked to enrich every contact.
> Then GPT-4 kicked in:
> → Read their recent posts
> → Analyzed tone
> → Personalized the first line + subject line
> → Matched messaging to funnel stage
> Step 3️⃣: Multi-Channel Outreach
> With n8n as the brain, we deployed outreach across:
> → Email
> → LinkedIn DMs
> → Retargeting Ads
> → Slack alerts (for hot leads)
> Every message adapted dynamically based on the buyer’s response (or silence).
> Step 4️⃣: AI-Powered Reply Handling
> Our GPT agents handled mid-thread replies:
> → Objections
> → Interest signals
> → Meeting coordination
> → No manual intervention
> Step 5️⃣: Meeting Booking + CRM Sync
> Once booked, the AI dropped a detailed buyer brief into HubSpot
> → Context
> → Pain points
> → Channel history
> → Suggested CTAs for closers
> What did we get?
> • 31 SQLs
> • $1.2M in pipeline
> • $0 on outbound headcount
> • 90% automation, 10% human handoff

## 349. Ashley Herbert Popa — 7mo

> Leading Product Marketing at Bynder

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7361237679797063680/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> In my first management role, I realized that if I wanted to help the PMMs that reported to me grow, I needed to build out what “good” looked like at each level. HR usually had a general idea of what to expect from certain departments but it was never covering the skillsets or experience level that I expect from PMMs at the Junior, PMM, and Senior level.
> So I built my own.
> Product Marketing Alliance had a great framework I used as a baseline and I tweaked and edited from there with more nuance on how to make it actionable. I’ve taken this to two other organizations in the meantime and used it with my teams to help them reflect on their current skillsets, figure out what they’re good at, and figure out how we can develop them (and the team) further - whether that means diving more into a skillset or making a path to the next level.
> Each quarter, I assign PMMs to projects that will help them level up the skillset they've identified. Because one thing I noticed in a fast-moving saas organization is that we rarely make time for our own self development. And I speak for myself on this one too. So I make it a part of the work that already needs to be done. And then we do check-ins and goal alignment/re-alignment every six months.
> This also helps me as a manager to figure out what skillsets we still need on the team, manage and estimate projects we can do each quarter, and level-up the whole team.
> Connect with me and give a 👋 in the comments and I'll be happy to share with you over a DM!

> → Reps show up on launch day ready to pitch, not panic

> → Embed the latest messaging, pricing, and competitive intel

> Sweetspot just launched AI Form Fill - the first and only autonomous form-fill agent for GovCon professionals.
> Sachin S., Philip Kung, and Andrew Arkhipov noticed that GovCon capture and proposal teams live in a sea of RFIs, PPQs, security questionnaires, vendor packets, teaming agreements, and other tedious forms across thousands of government agencies.
> This is why they built Sweetspot’s AI Form Fill. To help clear that backlog so teams can chase the next win, not the next text box.
> Some features:
> 📝 Understands & completes any form, anywhere. Sweetspot trained their own models in-house to ensure Form Fill locates every field (labeled or not) across any document, then auto-populates with your validated company data in real time.
> 🔒 Compliance you can trust. Form Fill never hallucinates; if a field needs clarification, it asks once, stores the answer, and nails it automatically on every future submission.
> 🪶 Built-in signatures & inline editing. Finalize, sign, and send without hopping between PDF tools or web portals - everything happens in one browser tab.
> 🧩 Rapid-fire templates for repeat tasks. Clone a proven response set for that weekly vendor form or recurring SLED bid and finish in seconds instead of hours.
> ⏱️ Hours back, pipeline forward. Sweetspot's beta testers reported double-digit hours saved each week, quicker proposal package turnaround times, and earlier submissions that boost win rates.
> Congrats to the team on the launch!

> Sachin S., Philip Kung, and Andrew Arkhipov noticed that GovCon capture and proposal teams live in a sea of RFIs, PPQs, security questionnaires, vendor packets, teaming agreements, and other tedious forms across thousands of government agencies.
> This is why they built Sweetspot’s AI Form Fill. To help clear that backlog so teams can chase the next win, not the next text box.
> Some features:
> 📝 Understands & completes any form, anywhere. Sweetspot trained their own models in-house to ensure Form Fill locates every field (labeled or not) across any document, then auto-populates with your validated company data in real time.
> 🔒 Compliance you can trust. Form Fill never hallucinates; if a field needs clarification, it asks once, stores the answer, and nails it automatically on every future submission.
> 🪶 Built-in signatures & inline editing. Finalize, sign, and send without hopping between PDF tools or web portals - everything happens in one browser tab.
> 🧩 Rapid-fire templates for repeat tasks. Clone a proven response set for that weekly vendor form or recurring SLED bid and finish in seconds instead of hours.
> ⏱️ Hours back, pipeline forward. Sweetspot's beta testers reported double-digit hours saved each week, quicker proposal package turnaround times, and earlier submissions that boost win rates.

## 372. Vedant Agarwala — 8mo

> Building | ex-YC founder (W23) | Early employee in unicorn companies | Angel Investor | 1x acqui-hired | 18k+ reputation on Stack Overflow.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7351340994010734592/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> We’re shutting down CodeParrot 💔
> Two and a half years ago, Royal Jain and I were just two cofounders with an idea and a shared dream. We got into Y Combinator with only the founding team and an idea we scrapped within weeks. We left India, went to Singapore to sort out our US visas, and spent three magical months in San Francisco — surrounded by people who made us believe we could build something meaningful.
> What followed was harder than we ever imagined. We didn’t raise on Demo Day. We spent the next year stuck in pivot hell, searching for what our company really wanted to be. We hired and, painfully, had to let go of both the engineers. We burned through the $500k we’d raised — and when we hit $1,500 MRR with our final pivot (Figma-to-code using LLMs), we couldn’t break through. Instead of running more experiments with dwindling runway, we decided it was time to shut shop.
> Looking back, two moments stand out:
> 1. Getting into YC.
> 2. Seeing our first real revenue hit our Stripe account.
> Those milestones kept us going when everything else was uncertain.
> There were plenty of lessons too. Startups are brutally hard — and pivot hell is the worst part by far.
> But we also learned how to build production LLM applications from scratch, without prior experience. Good prompts get you 90% there, but good evals are what really matter. And even when you’re building with cutting-edge AI, 70–80% of the codebase is still good old-fashioned software — and it deserves just as much attention.
> Right now, I’m on a short sabbatical, but my conviction in AI and LLMs has only grown. I know there’s so much more to build in this space. I’m already exploring new ideas and looking for a cofounder to take the next plunge with. The YC community is an incredible place to find both inspiration and partners.
> In the near term, I’ll be helping YC companies build and production-ize their LLM applications — and using that time to brainstorm, understand pain points, and get ready for the next shot.
> To everyone who supported us — users, friends, family, YC batchmates — thank you; especially Brad Flora and Puneet Kumar. We didn’t get where we hoped to, but I’m walking away with hard-earned lessons, gratitude, and the fire to try again.
> If you’re building, I’m cheering for you. And if you — or someone you know — is excited about AI and want to build something together, my DMs are open.

> They use a few software for production data, including:
> - BigQuery as their data warehouse.
> - Google Cloud to deploy applications.
> - Holistics Data as a business intelligence platform for analytics.
> - RudderStack & dbt Labs as customer data platform for data transformation.
> For prototyping, they use:
> - Vercel V0 for code generation & user interface.
> - Replit to develop & deploy software.
> 4️⃣ Funnel
> They convert around ~7-8% of their 200K+ monthly visits into signups. This amounts to 15,000+ new signups every month.
> To track product/website metrics, they use:
> - Mixpanel
> - Google Analytics
> To extract, transform, load & synchronise data across every department, they use Hightouch, which is what's called a 'reverse ETL platform'.
> Then, their marketing stack includes:
> - Customer.io (for email marketing)
> - Default (for lead routing)
> - Webflow (website)

> It’s for you: the aspiring or working product marketer trying to stand out in a sea of sameness.

> ✅ Clarity in my voice
> ✅ Connections I would’ve never reached out to

> Because here’s the truth:
> 🛑 Resumes show you’re a PMM.
