## 384. Tejas Shahasane — 9mo

> बी2बी | Content Marketer

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7348641918928551936/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> Piracy 🤝 AI
> In a recent case against Anthropic, it was revealed that they used mix of pirated and legally obtained books to train
> hashtag
> #Claude. While they were let off the hook, the ruling from the San Francisco judge set what might be the first major AI fair use/copyright precedent.
> The gist was that using legally obtained copyrighted material to train an AI model counts as fair use and is A-OK.
> This means you could buy a book for $10 and legally train a model on it, regardless of the licensing fees set by the publisher or author. Because the resulting output of the training is "transformative."
> But what interests me is the sheer inconvenience of legally obtaining data (books in this instance).
> hashtag
> #Anthropic spent millions buying books, then another few million having people manually digitize them. This is definitely cumbersome and not scalable. (Are you even AI-ing if you're not doing everything efficiently at scale? I mean, isn't that the whole schtick?)
> Anyways, I've read your mind. You said, "Easy solution. Amazon's got a whole-ass digital library of books it can provide to these
> hashtag
> #AI companies. Call it Kindle Enterprise Edition."
> Wrong.
> hashtag
> #Amazon would have to negotiate with individual publishers and authors to have them opt into this dataset. Because of the way they are structured like a marketplace, not a reseller. They don't own any rights apart from distribution.
> So, the only remaining option for a large, organized, and digitized repository of books is pirated P2P torrents and archives like Z-Library or Anna's Archive.
> It's already been revealed that
> hashtag
> #Meta used a pirated dataset of about 81 TB of books to train its models. That's about 45 million
> hashtag
> #books.
> The shitty thing is they probably didn't even seed after.
> …more

## 387. Sachin Jha — 9mo

> Founder ONEGTMLab | Engineering GTM for Cyber and DevTools | Product Marketing, AI GTM Engineering, and Founder-Led Growth Expert | 2X Top 100 Product Marketing Influencers

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7348638323810201602/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> How product marketing courses are scamming you.
> This week, I dropped a simple question in the private PMM group:
> “𝐻𝑎𝑣𝑒 𝑦𝑜𝑢 𝑡𝑎𝑘𝑒𝑛 𝑎 𝑝𝑟𝑜𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡 𝑚𝑎𝑟𝑘𝑒𝑡𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝑐𝑜𝑢𝑟𝑠𝑒 𝑏𝑒𝑓𝑜𝑟𝑒?”
> And suddenly, my phone blew up. I didn’t expect what came next.
> Over 10 people called me directly.
> They didn’t rant about the instructors.
> They didn’t blame the platform.
> Their real frustration? The structure.
> “𝐼 𝑠𝑝𝑒𝑛𝑡 ₹1 𝑙𝑎𝑘ℎ+ 𝑜𝑛 𝑎 𝑐𝑜𝑢𝑟𝑠𝑒. 𝐴𝑛𝑑 𝑤ℎ𝑒𝑛 𝐼 𝑎𝑐𝑡𝑢𝑎𝑙𝑙𝑦 𝑔𝑜𝑡 𝑡ℎ𝑒 𝑗𝑜𝑏, 𝐼 ℎ𝑎𝑑 𝑛𝑜 𝑖𝑑𝑒𝑎 𝑤ℎ𝑎𝑡 𝐼 𝑤𝑎𝑠 𝑑𝑜𝑖𝑛𝑔.”
> That’s the dirty secret most PMM programs won’t tell you:
> 𝐘𝐨𝐮 𝐜𝐚𝐧’𝐭 𝐥𝐞𝐚𝐫𝐧 𝐩𝐫𝐨𝐝𝐮𝐜𝐭 𝐦𝐚𝐫𝐤𝐞𝐭𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐟𝐫𝐨𝐦 𝐚 𝐬𝐭𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐜 𝐜𝐡𝐞𝐜𝐤𝐥𝐢𝐬𝐭.

> How can someone teach AI product marketing when they’ve never launched an AI product?
> Or explain how messaging evolves when they’ve never sat in a war room debugging GTM with Sales, Product, and CS?
> Let’s be honest:
> ❌ Too many “coaches” are teaching frameworks without any battle scars
> ❌ Too many “courses” are copy-pasted templates recycled across industries
> ❌ Too many "positioning lessons" skip the actual hard part: understanding the market
> 𝐏𝐌𝐌 𝐢𝐬𝐧’𝐭 𝐚𝐛𝐨𝐮𝐭 𝐦𝐞𝐦𝐨𝐫𝐢𝐳𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐬𝐥𝐢𝐝𝐞𝐬.

> So what should a real learning journey give you?
> ✅ Context: SaaS ≠ Fintech ≠ Consumer ≠ AI
> ✅ Strategy: First-principles thinking > Paint-by-numbers frameworks
> ✅ Proof: Lessons shaped by real products, real launches, real feedback
> The best PMMs don’t just know what to say.
> They know why it matters, who it’s for, and how to make it land.
> Before you sign up for another overpriced course or pretty certificate, ask yourself:
> Will this help me think like a PMM, or just act like one?
> Is this taught by someone in the arena, or someone who’s just watching from the sidelines?
> Will I walk away with insight, or another Notion template?

## 389. Obaid Durrani — 9mo

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7347898336995176449/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> When I was the first and only marketer at a SaaS company, I created a document that enabled our growth from five figures ARR to $1.5M ARR in 9 months (and helped us secure Series A funding)
> This doc informed our:
> - positioning
> - messaging
> - what our team wrote about on LinkedIn
> - what our sales team talked about with prospects
> - what we created all our content about
> I referred to this document as our "Realm of Relevancy"
> It covered every bit of critical info that was relevant to the world around our product:
> - what existing process(es) is our tool impacting or optimizing?
> - why is that existing process no longer the best or most efficient way to accomplish something?
> - if the current status quo is no longer the best method or approach, what is?
> - how do we go about implementing this new method? What all does it entail?
> - what changes, transformation, or outcomes can we expect if we successfully implement this new method?
> - how did we even get here?
> - how will this continue to evolve in the next 5-10 years?
> I gathered all this info from a 4-5 hour call with my 3 co-founders in the form of dozens of bullet points (bullet points vs. a slide deck are easier to operationalize and turn into endless topics)
> I then bucketed all the bullet points into a 3-part story:
> 1. The first part covered everything around why the current way to do things was no longer the most efficient way
> 2. The second part of the story covered everything around how we could do that thing now
> 3. And the last part of the story covered everything around what outcomes you could expect if you adapted to this new way
> I focused on communicating everything that was in my RoR to our audience through different forms of (educational, tactical, and entertaining) content
> I had our internal team (from our CTO to our CS rep) and influencers do the same
> We spent 9 months educating our TAM and changing the way people think about a semi-controversial topic such as attribution, driving over $10M in pipeline in the process
> I also used this experience to create RoR's for companies like Meta and Cognism
> If you want to see the exact doc I described in this post:
> 1. Comment below with the word "doc" and I'll send it to you
> 2. Send me a request with a note saying you want the doc and ill send it to you
> ultimately, great marketing teams are already communicating all of this through their content, it's just not all collected into one cohesive story that helps them make sense of it themselves and guides them
> Your CEO is already telling this story to other execs at dinners, she just hasn't been armed with something that explains how this complicated story should be unraveled so no important piece of info is missed and everything makes sense to the listener
> you need to build this story for them
> Comment below to see how
> P.S. I'm also working on a high-ticket service where I'll create a RoR for you, so drop me a message if that's of any interest—I'm only going to take on two at a time
> …more

## 390. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 9mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7347645925218992130/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> "How do I help my management understand why Product Managers and Product Marketers need to work closely together?"
> I hear this question a lot from PMMs who read my content.
> Let me break it to you.
> It will be an uphill climb if your organization doesn't fully comprehend the essence of a PMM role and isn't ready to change their product rituals.
> But it always starts with internal education.
> Lobby for the real impact a PMM brings to the table. There are many resources out there to help with this.
> Then, move on to gradually describe how a PM-PMM combo thinks differently. That's the first step to opening a conversation.
> Some themes to focus on:
> [1] Articulating the message is as important as building the product.
> → The words matter. The most innovative features won't do much if customers don't understand why they should care.
> [2] “Shipping” as a concept needs to be redefined. Releases don't mean much without creating awareness.
> → If users don't know a feature exists, it won't move any needles. PMs & PMMs have to partner to make sure that doesn't happen.
> [3] You haven’t solved the problem until you’ve convinced the customer to use it.
> → You don't put out a fire by "building" a fire extinguisher but by using it.
> [4] You don't need every feature when you can beat the competition by positioning.
> → Customers don't always need more bells and whistles. They need to be convinced that the product in question best fits "their" needs. More product isn't always the fix.
> [5] The value proposition isn’t complete until you bring pricing into the equation.
> → While pricing usually involves GTM stakeholders, PMs & PMMs can share intel on willingness to pay based on their research notes.
> [6] Focus on serving the few segments that will bring in 80% of the business value.
> → Building for "everyone" often means serving no one exceptionally well. It also forces messaging to be generic and flat, sinking conversions.
> [7] Understanding psychological drivers, human motivation, and context can fundamentally change what you build and how.
> → Ex: Airtable appealed to developers not by building enterprise-features, but by calling themselves a "database" instead of a "spreadsheet". Play with perception.
> [8] Sales is a valuable feedback gate that gives us unfiltered market truths.
> → Sales is often seen as a "ransom" enemy who demands features. In reality, they echo what the market wants, and allying with them judiciously creates a win-win.
> [9] Unless one learns how to work with revenue teams, it’s hard to drive meaningful business impact.
> → Enabling sales and customer success to tell the right story can tip over deals, resulting in tangible business value.
> Yes, some of this could rub folks the wrong way. Nobody likes a know-it-all who talks down at them.
> That's why the intent, tone and timing matters.
> Make it sound like a conversation, not a decree.
> --
> What would you like to add?
> …more

## 392. Richard King — 9mo

> Talking truth on leadership, growth & product marketing | 5x founder | 3x exits |

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7347511610162233345/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> STOP making 1 pagers ✋
> Logan Hendrickson, former PMM Lead at 15Five shows you why...
> How it usually goes...
> 1. Product: "We're launching a new feature!"
> 2. Sales: "Great, can I get a one-pager?"
> 3. PMM: *sighs and opens Google Docs*
> Logan's POV:
> Stop and ask this question.
> "What can a one-pager do that a web page can't?"
> As a former salesperson himself, Logan admits he's sent plenty of one-pagers in his day. But would he rather have sent a dynamic web page with video, interactive product tours, and swappable customer stories?
> 5 reasons why you should axe your 1 pagers
> 👇
> 1) Your website should be your best asset
> One pagers end up becoming a slightly different version of your website. Same pictures. Same words. The only difference? You can print it.
> Tactical approach:
> Audit your website and onepagers side by side, you'll likely find 80% duplicate content
> Instead of creating new PDFs, focus on making your product pages exceptional
> Remove navigation and footers from specific landing pages if "simplicity" is the concern
> 2) Digital assets provide measurable engagement
> With a web page, you can track views, time spent, and interaction. With a PDF? It disappears into the void.
> Tactical approach:
> Set up dedicated tracking links for sales to use when sharing pages
> Create custom URLs for different sales reps to track attribution
> Show sales teams the engagement data so they see the value
> 3) Web pages enable interactive experiences
> Self guided product tours, embedded videos, and clickable journeys are impossible in a static PDF.
> Tactical approach:
> Implement tools like Navattic or Tolstoy: AI Commerce Platform for guided product tours
> Embed demo videos directly in the page
> Create interactive comparison tables that expand on click
> Allow prospects to toggle between different use cases or personas
> 4) Web pages create natural education journeys
> One pagers end abruptly. Web pages allow prospects to continue exploring on their own.
> Tactical approach:
> Design clear "next step" pathways at the bottom of each page
> Link to relevant case studies, technical docs, or complementary features
> Create a "recommended reading" section based on the prospect's role
> Build progressive disclosure into your pages (expand/collapse sections)
> Logan's approac is to use this simple shield:
> "Let's just try our best to always say website over one-pagers. If you need something a one-pager could do, let's just make sure the website does that."
> But how do you actually implement this change with sales?
> Start here in this order:
> 1) Create a standardized request process where sales can submit needs through a form
> 2) Ask qualifying questions: "What's the specific need this request is filling?"
> 3) Ask for revenue impact: "How much revenue is attached to this? Is there a renewal on the line?"
> 4) Look for patterns in requests to identify gaps in your website
> 5) Democratize the competitive intel gathering process so it's not all on PMM
> P.S. What's your take on 1 pagers PMMs?
> …more
> Play
> 697
> 73 comments
> 22

> Self guided product tours, embedded videos, and clickable journeys are impossible in a static PDF.
> Tactical approach:
> Implement tools like Navattic or Tolstoy: AI Commerce Platform for guided product tours
> Embed demo videos directly in the page
> Create interactive comparison tables that expand on click
> Allow prospects to toggle between different use cases or personas
> 4) Web pages create natural education journeys
> One pagers end abruptly. Web pages allow prospects to continue exploring on their own.
> Tactical approach:
> Design clear "next step" pathways at the bottom of each page
> Link to relevant case studies, technical docs, or complementary features
> Create a "recommended reading" section based on the prospect's role
> Build progressive disclosure into your pages (expand/collapse sections)
> Logan's approac is to use this simple shield:
> "Let's just try our best to always say website over one-pagers. If you need something a one-pager could do, let's just make sure the website does that."
> But how do you actually implement this change with sales?
> Start here in this order:
> 1) Create a standardized request process where sales can submit needs through a form
> 2) Ask qualifying questions: "What's the specific need this request is filling?"
> 3) Ask for revenue impact: "How much revenue is attached to this? Is there a renewal on the line?"
> 4) Look for patterns in requests to identify gaps in your website
> 5) Democratize the competitive intel gathering process so it's not all on PMM
> P.S. What's your take on 1 pagers PMMs?
> …more
> Play
> 697
> 73 comments

## 394. Richard King — 9mo

> Talking truth on leadership, growth & product marketing | 5x founder | 3x exits |

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7347511481141207040/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> STOP making 1 pagers ✋
> Logan Hendrickson, former PMM Lead at 15Five shows you why...
> How it usually goes...
> 1. Product: "We're launching a new feature!"
> 2. Sales: "Great, can I get a one-pager?"
> 3. PMM: *sighs and opens Google Docs*
> Logan's POV:
> Stop and ask this question.
> "What can a one-pager do that a web page can't?"
> As a former salesperson himself, Logan admits he's sent plenty of one-pagers in his day. But would he rather have sent a dynamic web page with video, interactive product tours, and swappable customer stories?
> 5 reasons why you should axe your 1 pagers
> 👇
> 1) Your website should be your best asset
> One pagers end up becoming a slightly different version of your website. Same pictures. Same words. The only difference? You can print it.
> Tactical approach:
> Audit your website and onepagers side by side, you'll likely find 80% duplicate content
> Instead of creating new PDFs, focus on making your product pages exceptional
> Remove navigation and footers from specific landing pages if "simplicity" is the concern
> 2) Digital assets provide measurable engagement
> With a web page, you can track views, time spent, and interaction. With a PDF? It disappears into the void.
> Tactical approach:
> Set up dedicated tracking links for sales to use when sharing pages
> Create custom URLs for different sales reps to track attribution
> Show sales teams the engagement data so they see the value
> 3) Web pages enable interactive experiences
> Self guided product tours, embedded videos, and clickable journeys are impossible in a static PDF.
> Tactical approach:
> Implement tools like Navattic or Tolstoy: AI Commerce Platform for guided product tours
> Embed demo videos directly in the page
> Create interactive comparison tables that expand on click
> Allow prospects to toggle between different use cases or personas
> 4) Web pages create natural education journeys
> One pagers end abruptly. Web pages allow prospects to continue exploring on their own.
> Tactical approach:
> Design clear "next step" pathways at the bottom of each page
> Link to relevant case studies, technical docs, or complementary features
> Create a "recommended reading" section based on the prospect's role
> Build progressive disclosure into your pages (expand/collapse sections)
> Logan's approac is to use this simple shield:
> "Let's just try our best to always say website over one-pagers. If you need something a one-pager could do, let's just make sure the website does that."
> But how do you actually implement this change with sales?
> Start here in this order:
> 1) Create a standardized request process where sales can submit needs through a form
> 2) Ask qualifying questions: "What's the specific need this request is filling?"
> 3) Ask for revenue impact: "How much revenue is attached to this? Is there a renewal on the line?"
> 4) Look for patterns in requests to identify gaps in your website
> 5) Democratize the competitive intel gathering process so it's not all on PMM

## 399. Yi Lin Pei — 9mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7344363208079482882/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> So much product marketing work is misunderstood, but sometimes we might be the reason why.
> I’ve been in back-to-back calls this week helping PMMs prep for QBRs and plan for H2.
> What I have noticed is that we describe our work in ways that make total sense to other PMMs, but are not easily understood by the rest of the business.
> “We rolled out new positioning.”
> “We created 3 new battlecards.”
> “We led 2 launches.”
> These all sound solid, but to a sales leader or a C suite person, these just describe activities, not outcomes.
> So, to communicate with more impact, get more influence and buy-in, you need to translate each of your activities to “exec speak” by tying it to business outcomes, supporting it with real data, and describing why it’s important.
> I created a cheatsheet before for the most common use cases, using a format inspired by Kyle Lacy (built for CMOs).
> Check it out in the comments and let me know if you have questions!

## 401. Sachin Jha — 9mo

> Founder ONEGTMLab | Engineering GTM for Cyber and DevTools | Product Marketing, AI GTM Engineering, and Founder-Led Growth Expert | 2X Top 100 Product Marketing Influencers

> How to land a high-paying PMM role at Google, HubSpot, or Salesforce?
> Everyone’s obsessed with the question.
> But here’s the truth no one wants to hear:
> 👉 It’s not that hard.
> 👉 It’s also not that great.
> 👉 And it may not be what you actually want.
> Let me explain.
> What does it take to land those roles?
> Referrals. That’s 90% of the game.
> The rest is knowing how to speak their language in interviews.
> You don’t need a certification.
> You do need someone inside saying,
> “She’s sharp. Let’s talk to her.”
> That’s how you get the interview.
> Not with another fancy resume template.
> But should you even aim for it?
> At big companies, you’ll often do 1/17th of a job.
> → Own a feature, not a product.
> → Write copy, not strategy.
> → Ship assets, not GTM.
> Sounds stable. But it’s limiting.
> If you’re wired for ownership, you’ll feel stuck.
> So what do PMMs in startups do differently?
> Everything. Literally.
> → You lead messaging, GTM, positioning, feedback loops.
> → You build the category, not just update the slides.
> → You learn fast, break things, and own wins and losses.
> You’re not just a marketer. You’re a builder.
> So how do we play the big game then?
> Simple:
> Think bigger. Not just higher.
> → Join a startup where your scope is wide, not just your salary.
> → Build things worth writing case studies about.
> → When you’re good enough, big companies will find you.
> And if you still want to get into Google later?
> You’ll walk in with leverage, not just ambition.
> Big jobs don’t build you.
> Big problems do.

> GTM teams are winning with a THIRTY-year-old technology. New AI tools can be great, but this is tried and true (and free).
> RSS (Really Simple Syndication) feeds have been around since the 90’s and are criminally undervalued and underutilized in GTM. They give your GTM team free competitor intelligence, relevant signals, and timely updates.
> RSS feeds allow you to, essentially, ‘subscribe’ to websites so every time that website changes or gets updated, you get notified with the new content.
> The best part is that a lot of websites offer completely free RSS feeds.
> Here are just a few leading GTM teams use them:
> 1️⃣ Competitive Intelligence:
> - Monitor competitor product pages for feature launches
> - Get Slack alerts when they hire key roles
> - Track their content strategy automatically
> 2️⃣ Lead Generation:
> - Monitor government databases for new records (Form D filings, 10k reports, etc.)
> - Monitor popular industry forums for new posts
> - Industry publication job postings
> - Company blog updates from target accounts
> 3️⃣ Content Strategy:
> - Track what topics perform well in your space
> - Monitor thought leaders for trending discussions
> - Automate content research
> - Again, more websites than you think actually offer these for free.

## 405. Aatir Abdul Rauf — 9mo

> VP of Marketing @ vFairs | Newsletter: Behind Product Lines | Talks about how to build & market products in lockstep

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7343343970585911297/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> Influencing the roadmap as a product marketer is often a privilege earned, not granted.
> I used to wonder why PMMs have difficulty getting on roadmap strategy calls.
> After talking to a few PMs and experts on LinkedIn/Reddit, I learned about 5 issues PMs have with PMMs.
> Here are actual quotes:
> 1) "They bring up random ideas without focusing on current priorities."
> 2) "They make feature requests without a strong rationale."
> 3) "They use buzzwords incorrectly and oversell capabilities."
> 4) "They demand specific delivery dates without understanding complexity."
> 5) "We build. They market. Not the other way around."
> I'm sure there are other reasons as well.
> But the good news is that their complaints also contain the blueprint to win a seat at the roadmap table.
> A 7-step process to consider as a PMM:
> [1] 𝗦𝘁𝗮𝗿𝘁 𝘄𝗶𝘁𝗵 𝘂𝗻𝗱𝗲𝗿𝘀𝘁𝗮𝗻𝗱𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝗰𝘂𝗿𝗿𝗲𝗻𝘁 𝗽𝗿𝗶𝗼𝗿𝗶𝘁𝗶𝗲𝘀.
> Before you suggest anything, look into your product team's goals and metrics for the quarter. Ex: If they're focused on fixing onboarding metrics, suggesting a new integration won't stick.
> [2] 𝗕𝘂𝗶𝗹𝗱 𝘆𝗼𝘂𝗿 𝗰𝗮𝘀𝗲 𝘄𝗶𝘁𝗵 𝗲𝘃𝗶𝗱𝗲𝗻𝗰𝗲.
> This doesn't mean a Mixpanel report. Compile support tickets, sales calls, customer interviews, and market reports to identify patterns that matter.
> [3] 𝗗𝗲𝗰𝗶𝗱𝗲 𝘄𝗵𝗮𝘁 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝗼𝘂𝘁𝗰𝗼𝗺𝗲 𝗼𝗳 𝘆𝗼𝘂𝗿 "𝗶𝗻𝗳𝗹𝘂𝗲𝗻𝗰𝗲" 𝘀𝗵𝗼𝘂𝗹𝗱 𝗯𝗲.
> What does the end goal even look like? Are you lobbying for a new roadmap item? Against one?
> Note: Many people don't realize this, but "influence" doesn't mean always suggesting something different. You can also provide evidence for something ALREADY on the roadmap and show your vote of support.
> [4] 𝗗𝗼 𝗮 𝘀𝗮𝗻𝗶𝘁𝘆 𝗰𝗵𝗲𝗰𝗸.
> Is the change desirable? Check the evidence. Is it viable? Consider if a monetization story is required. Is it feasible? Consult an engineer. Ask ChatGPT with a thoughtful prompt.
> [5] 𝗦𝘁𝗶𝘁𝗰𝗵 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝗻𝗮𝗿𝗿𝗮𝘁𝗶𝘃𝗲.
> This is where your PMM skills shine. Think use cases, scenarios, or role plays with a persona. Build a story that connects customer pain points to business impact, backed by your curated evidence.
> [6] 𝗣𝗮𝗰𝗸𝗮𝗴𝗲 𝘆𝗼𝘂𝗿 𝗽𝗿𝗼𝗽𝗼𝘀𝗮𝗹 𝘁𝗵𝗼𝘂𝗴𝗵𝘁𝗳𝘂𝗹𝗹𝘆.
> You need to convey your idea in a manner that's easily shareable and memorable. These days, I'm favoring Lovable prototypes to express ideas.
> [7] 𝗡𝗲𝗴𝗼𝘁𝗶𝗮𝘁𝗲 𝗮𝗻𝗱 𝗰𝗹𝗼𝘀𝗲
> There will be some pushback. Be open to accepting a different viewpoint or another (improved) solution. Put that learning into the next attempt.
> Influence isn't about getting your way. It's the license to share a point of view for serious consideration.
> Remember: Product Managers want strategic partners, not another stakeholder with a random wish.
> …more

## 406. Josue Valles — 9mo

> Founder, CurationLabs

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7343343881196867585/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> A copywriter I know is pulling $500K/year and uses AI for 80% of his work. He writes copy for two massive companies - a $100M+ health supplement brand and a $600M+ financial publisher. This dude is making serious money. Here's what he taught me about the future of copy:
> 1. For short-form stuff (Facebook, Instagram, etc.), AI is doing most of the heavy lifting.
> His team went from writing 3 good ads per day to 15. Same time investment, 5x the output. They feed AI massive libraries of their best-performing content. What worked, what didn't, what hooks to reuse, what angles to try next. But the companies didn't fire anyone. They just expected everyone to produce 5x more.
> 2. For long-form sales pages (the $8M revenue kind), AI barely helps at all.
> When you're writing 20,000-word sales letters that generate millions, AI can help with research and small sections. But it can't write the whole thing.
> The strategy, the psychology, the flow - that's still 100% human.
> The biggest lesson?
> AI exposed who actually knows what they're doing.
> Before AI, a junior copywriter could spend weeks researching and writing one decent ad. Now AI can research and draft that same ad in 20 minutes. But if you don't know what good copy looks like, you'll just generate garbage faster.
> The entry-level "research and write basic copy" jobs?
> Yeah, those are gone.
> But the "understand consumer psychology and create compelling offers" jobs?
> Those are more valuable than ever.
> TAKEAWAY:
> AI isn't going to replace marketers who understand human psychology.
> It's going to replace marketers who just follow templates.

> Self-advocacy at work is a privilege.
> I've always been a big fan of speaking my mind. It's a skill I value deeply, even if it has earned me the affectionate and accurate moniker of "Muhfat" or "brutally honest" from colleagues at my farewell calls. And honestly, I wouldn't trade that directness for anything.

> Not everyone you meet at work is equipped to handle constructive criticism, discourse, or disagreements the way they're meant to be handled: professionally.
> And if you rub someone the wrong way, especially someone in power, you could jeopardize your career trajectory, your daily work environment, and perhaps even impact your peace of mind.
> So, here's the privilege: having the agency to walk away, to express dissent, or to escalate if necessary. It's about having the space, the support, or frankly, the power to navigate challenging situations without jeopardizing your well-being or career.

## 413. Chantal Cox — 9mo

> Director of Product at Adobe, AI agents | ex-Meta, Amazon, Credit Karma, eBay | 2x Founder | TedX Speaker

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7342941958929076227/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **lik**

> Being crisp and concise is underrated. The lack of it signals something more concerning: a thinking problem.
> When you don’t know what truly matters, you try to say 𝘦𝘷𝘦𝘳𝘺𝘵𝘩𝘪𝘯𝘨.
> When you're clear on the core point, you can say it 𝘴𝘪𝘮𝘱𝘭𝘺.
> → I’ve seen 10-page docs that could’ve been 1/2 page.
> → I’ve seen bullet-point updates that still leave the team confused.
> → I’ve seen senior folks talk for 10 minutes without saying anything.
> Unclear thinking doesn't just waste time—it confuses teams, delays progress, and makes smart people look incompetent.
> Using ChatGPT to clean up your blurb or turn it into bullet points isn’t a silver bullet.
> You can write fewer words, but if you're unclear on your core point, no amount of editing will save it.
> This isn’t just about reducing noise or improving readability. It is about clarity of thought.
> 𝗕𝗲𝗳𝗼𝗿𝗲 𝘆𝗼𝘂 𝗽𝗼𝗹𝗶𝘀𝗵 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝘄𝗼𝗿𝗱𝘀, 𝘀𝗵𝗮𝗿𝗽𝗲𝗻 𝘁𝗵𝗲 𝘁𝗵𝗶𝗻𝗸𝗶𝗻𝗴.
> The best PMs don’t just communicate well—they think clearly, then make it obvious to everyone else.

## 417. Yi Lin Pei — 9mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7341301295938473986/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> I’m not a fan of career ladders.
> I’ve coached over 200 PMMs at every level, and here’s one pattern I see over and over:
> Someone pulls up their company’s career ladder, sees 5–6 levels, and immediately asks:
> “What exactly do I need to do to get promoted?”
> They read the descriptions but are still confused.
> I’ve even spoken to hiring managers who can’t explain the difference between a Senior PMM and a Lead PMM. The lines are blurry, the expectations vague, and the bar shifts depending on who you ask.
> So here’s my take:
> Don’t climb the ladder, leap across it.
> Instead of trying to move up to the next increment, focus on making clear, major, stepwise shifts in mindset and business impact.
> That’s why my framework has just 3 levels (see graphic):
> --> PMM → Executes all core tasks well with guidance
> --> Senior PMM → Executes all task well with minimal guidance; Influences strategy and leads xfn work
> --> Director+ → Drives business impact, builds teams, and sets direction
> When clients ask me “How do I move up?”,  I don’t give them a checklist, but instead we work on answering bigger questions such as:
> --> Are you owning GTM strategy, or waiting for direction?
> -->Are you influencing roadmap decisions, or reacting to launches?
> --> Can you or would you like to coach and grow others?
> That’s what actually helps you move up.
> What’s your take,  are ladders helpful?

## 418. Yasser Elsaid — 9mo

> CEO at Chatbase

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7340585965108764672/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 3 main strategies to build an AI company now:
> 1. b2c product with tiktok marketing
> Figure out a format that can go viral then build a product around it.
> Pros:
> - b2c is more exciting than b2b
> - less competition
> - you only need a small team and a good playbook to make it work
> Cons:
> - not many products can go viral
> - hard to make smth go viral consistently
> - higher churn
> Cal AI and Jenni AI are examples of this.
> 2. b2b: AI version of existing tools
> This is what most venture backed startups are doing now.
> Pros:
> - huge TAM for horizontal software
> - more defensible long term
> (business typically need a lot of features which makes you a "thick wrapper" which is good as it increases the barrier to entry)
> Cons:
> - very competitive (most VC startups are doing this now)
> Chatbase is an example of this.
> 3. AI consulting
> Become an expert in building with AI, create content to showcase your work, charge $$$ to help companies.
> Pros:
> - more straight forward to start
> - work on your own terms
> Cons:
> - exchanging time for money
> - hard to scale
> Jason Liu is killing it doing this.
> Do one of these and ride the AI wave before it's too late.

## 419. Nikolai Golos — 9mo

> Product & Growth at Fluently AI (YC W24) | Improve your English speaking skills with AI

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7340584391783403521/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> "Learn More" is not a CTA.
> And neither is:
> • "Start Now"
> • "See Plans"
> • "Get a Demo"
> These generic buttons make users doubt because they don't know what happens next.
> A high-converting CTA:
> 1) Solves a specific problem → "Get Your Free Strategy Call"
> 2) Uses customer language → "Start Designing My Logo"
> 3) Clearly defines the next step → "Start My Free 7-Day Trial"
> CTAs help users act. Take away the doubt, guide them, and make it easy for them to take action.
> I tested this with our product Fluently: AI English tutor:
> ❌ Old CTA: "Start now" = 3.2% conversion
> ✅ New CTA: "Test my English level for free" = 11.8% conversion
> That's a 269% improvement with a simple text change.
> Your CTA isn't just a button. It's a promise of what comes next.
> Remember: people don't buy products – they buy outcomes. And your CTA is your chance to deliver a promise they actually want.
> Your thoughts?
> --
> P.S.: Are you a non-native English professional?
> Check out my AI app to boost your speaking skills 👉 https://GetFluently.app/

> While everyone’s busy building flashy AI agents, I stuck with what actually works.
> Not chasing trends. Just building GPTs that think like I do.
> 𝐁𝐞𝐜𝐚𝐮𝐬𝐞 𝐈 𝐝𝐢𝐝𝐧’𝐭 𝐰𝐚𝐧𝐭 𝐟𝐮𝐥𝐥-𝐛𝐥𝐨𝐰𝐧 𝐚𝐮𝐭𝐨𝐦𝐚𝐭𝐢𝐨𝐧 𝐭𝐡𝐚𝐭 𝐬𝐩𝐢𝐭𝐬 𝐨𝐮𝐭 𝐫𝐚𝐧𝐝𝐨𝐦 𝐧𝐨𝐢𝐬𝐞.
> I wanted tools that think, guide, and help me do product marketing the way
> 𝐼𝑡’𝑠 𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑛𝑡 𝑡𝑜 𝑏𝑒 𝑑𝑜𝑛𝑒 𝑠𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑡𝑒𝑔𝑖𝑐𝑎𝑙𝑙𝑦.
> Two bots.
> Two no-fluff PMM resources.
> Three more in the works.

> Here’s what I’ve created:
> 🧪 𝐌𝐞𝐬𝐬𝐚𝐠𝐢𝐧𝐠 𝐋𝐚𝐛 𝐁𝐨𝐭
> Built to mimic my real sprint workflow.
> → From VoC mining to Objection Busting to Core Messaging.
> This isn’t another “hook generator.”
> It helps you think like a PMM—not just sound like one.
> 🧠 𝐏𝐞𝐫𝐬𝐨𝐧𝐚𝐥 𝐁𝐫𝐚𝐧𝐝 𝐂𝐨𝐚𝐜𝐡
> Not a ghostwriter. A gut-checker.
> It sits beside you and asks:
> → “What’s the real insight here?”
> → “Would you say this if no one liked it?”
> → “Does this sound like you, or LinkedIn™?”
> 📓 𝐏𝐌𝐌 𝐈𝐧𝐭𝐞𝐫𝐯𝐢𝐞𝐰 𝐉𝐨𝐮𝐫𝐧𝐚𝐥
> No listicles. No fluff.
> Just brutal, strategic prompts that make you question your entire GTM narrative.
> → Stuff like: “Explain fine-tuning to a confused AE” or
> → “How do you build PM buy-in for a weak feature?”
> 📘 𝐀𝐈 𝐟𝐨𝐫 𝐏𝐌𝐌𝐬: 𝐀 𝐅𝐢𝐫𝐬𝐭 𝐏𝐫𝐢𝐧𝐜𝐢𝐩𝐥𝐞𝐬 𝐆𝐮𝐢𝐝𝐞
> Because I was done seeing “use ChatGPT to write content” passed off as AI literacy.
> → This is for PMMs who want to actually understand AI,
> → Speak with clarity in the room,
> → And stop being sidelined in technical convos.
> These aren’t vanity projects.
> They’re my response to the growing disconnect between AI hype and PMM reality.

## 422. Roxana Irimia — 10mo

> VP Marketing at Chili Piper 🌶️ | ex-Head of Marketing at lemlist | “she’s the marketing roxstar” | FOLLOW to save hours of Marketing, Content, Product Marketing and Branding research

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7338491826938486784/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> 99% of B2B content is made backwards.
> Sales says:
> “We need a deck.”
> “We need a one-pager.”
> “We need a case study.”
> So marketing delivers.
> The problem?
> This content is made for closers, not buyers.
> It convinces, but doesn’t educate.
> Here’s how marketing should think instead:
> → TOFU / Awareness
> Topics: Buyer pain points. Trends. Consequences of inaction.
> Goal: Spark curiosity, build trust, earn attention.
> Example: “Why most SaaS teams overpay for underused tools”
> → MOFU / Consideration
> Topics: Solutions. Options. Frameworks.
> Goal: Help people compare and explore.
> Example: “3 ways to consolidate your tool stack”
> → BOFU / Decision
> Topics: Product. Social proof. Competitive angles.
> Goal: Build trust, remove objections.
> Example: “How X company cut costs by 42% using our platform”
> Content should follow the buyer’s journey.
> If Sales asks for case studies, it means buyers need more education.
> You can run a quick audit to spot content gaps in your funnel.
> Here’s how:
> 1. List all your current content (blog posts, decks, case studies, etc).
> 2. Tag each piece as TOFU, MOFU, or BOFU.
> 3. Count how many assets you have at each stage.
> 4. Compare that to your buyer journey and sales needs.
> E.g. When I did this at Chili Piper, I saw we were heavy on TOFU and MOFU.
> But light on BOFU, product-specific, objection-handling content.
> So BOFU became a priority this quarter.
> Try it. It takes 30 minutes and shows exactly where to focus.
> …more

## 424. Kesava Mandiga found Renae McBride’s comment on this funny — 10mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7338297859764559872/) · type: `activity`

> Am I alone?
> Is it just me… or does product marketing feel a little off right now?
> A PMM with 15 years of experience DMed me with that message this week.
> And on the same day, a client told me she felt an existential crisis as a PMM, and wondered if her work is worth a penny in the age of AI.
> And honestly, I felt it too.
> The same taglines repeated on websites.
> The same posts on LinkedIn with the same hooks.
> The same expectations from B2B SaaS companies wanting the same result.
> Productivity. Time savings.
> The job title bloat.
> The pressure to perform rather than think.
> What I'm hearing and I'm seeing is this: PMMs, especially those in their mid-career, are quietly wondering if there's still a place for people like them.
> These are the people who fell in love with strategy, connecting to the customer voice, appreciating the nuances and beauty of the written word, and being the glue that holds teams together.
> They're asking, do I still belong here? Am I the only one who doesn't want to play the game? Am I still relevant? Am I alone?
> My answer is, you're not the only one. You're not alone. You are asking exactly the right questions.
> And we are not going anywhere.
> 100 years ago, people were marketing bar soap… and they needed marketers.
> 100 years later, we are still marketing bar soap… plus some more things like software.
> But the fundamentals don’t change… because as long as businesses exist, they need to market to customers. They need people to stop in their tracks to pay attention to something important that will solve their problem, and then buy it.
> And that requires PATTERN DISRUPTION, not sameness.. And creating something different, by definition, requires human ingenuity and “analog thinking” - as Anne Hadley puts it.
> So, product marketing might be called a different name in the future, but it’s not going away anytime soon, and will only be MORE important in an era of sameness.
> But realistically, what do you do if you feel stuck right now in this age of AI?
> ---> Acknowledge you are NOT alone in feeling isolated and scared by what is going on
> ---> Reframe this crisis as an INFECTION point in the long and continuing journey of marketing products
> ---> Invest time to improve how you THINK - mental models, practice by doing, seeking real feedback from experts
> ---> Embrace relationships and the HUMAN side of the craft, which no tools can ever replace.
> And just finding people whom you trust to talk to.
> As a conclusion to the story, instead of a “regular” coaching call, my client and I discussed the future of PMM, her values, and purpose. And she came out feeling a lot more assured and confident.
> ❓What’s a helpful framing for you to think about PMM in the age of AI?
> I am working on some really cool initiatives and events related to this theme. I look forward to sharing them with you in the coming weeks!
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #ai
> hashtag
> #growth
> hashtag
> #coaching
> hashtag
> #tech

## 430. Hattie the PMM — 10mo

> Next free live product marketing training April 16→ I help Product Marketers become visible, valued revenue drivers for their orgs | PMM Coach and Advisor | Coached 200+ PMMs | Take the Visible and Valued PMM Challenge

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7336808705973227521/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **likes**

> We PMMs need to become The Ideal PMM© more than ever in today's job market. Here's why... 👀
> I’ve seen it over and over.
> Workhorse PMMs who execute flawlessly but don’t have influence, getting let go in tech layoffs…
> While the yappers, the ones who talk a big game but don’t deliver, somehow survive. 🤷🏻‍♀️
> We can’t afford to be just one or the other.
> ❌ Influence without execution fades.
> ❌ Execution without influence keeps us stuck in support mode.
> ✅ IMO to be a high-impact PMM we need both.
> I built out my signature The PMM Impact Matrix© in order to visualise why it's necessary.
> It's no longer good enough, or safe to just be good at our job.
> It's important to pair up effectiveness of delivering results with high internal influence and respect.
> But too many PMMs get stuck in one of these quadrants:
> 🎤 The yapper PMM©
> They’ve got leadership’s ear but struggle to follow through. Big ideas, strong opinions, but execution gaps mean their influence fades fast.
> 👻 The ghost PMM©
> These PMMs feel the most invisible. No influence, no execution power. Always blocked, overlooked, and struggling to prove their value.
> ⚙️ The workhorse PMM©
> The go-to for execution but never part of strategic conversations. They get stuff done but have no seat at the table, stuck reacting instead of leading.
> The trick is to aim for a balance:
> ✅ The Ideal PMM©
> Drives impact, shapes decisions, and executes effectively. Leadership trusts them, cross-functional teams rely on them, and they’re seen as strategic partners.
> Here are some prompting questions to think about if you don't quite feel like you're in the ideal PMM quadrant:
> 1. Do I feel listened to and valued in meetings?
> 2. Am I influencing decisions, or just executing tasks?
> 3. Do cross-functional teams seek out my input, or do I have to fight to be included?
> This June I'll be sharing an email-exclusive masterclass, that covers how we PMMs can no longer be perceived as workhorses but respected as ideal PMMs.
> 👇 Sign up to my email newsletter to not miss out. Link is in the comments or on my profile.

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7336489915104276480/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> McKinsey Says that Product Marketing Managers (PMMs) are the Quiet Force Behind Software Success
> A new McKinsey study (link in the comments, https://lnkd.in/gE9vyDxx) has put PMMs in the spotlight, emphasizing their growing importance in the software industry.
> Its findings reinforce the ideas I've shared in my previous posts on the evolving role of PMMs in the AI era (https://lnkd.in/ggjfSawh) and their pivotal role in driving personalization and efficient growth (https://lnkd.in/erV6Nipd).
> Here are some key insights from the study:
> ► Instrumental in Product Launches - PMMs are essential in launching successful products in today's competitive market.
> ► Revenue Growth - Companies in the top quartile in revenue growth have a 25-30% higher ratio of PMMs to PMs compared to bottom quartile companies, reinforcing the value of well-resourced PMM teams.
> ► Bridging Gaps - PMMs align product value with market needs, bridge gaps between teams, and orchestrate launch processes.
> As Goksu Nebol-Perlman, VP of Business Product Marketing at Meta, said, "Product marketing is the glue that keeps the product, the market, and the customer together."
> Why are PMMs critical more than ever?
> ► Market Differentiation - With changing market dynamics and increased competition, PMMs help products stand out and succeed.
> ► AI-Powered Analytics - As data and AI-powered analytics become essential for decision-making, PMMs use these tools to guide strategies and gain deep customer insights.
> ► Efficient Growth - AI helps PMMs with analytics to make smart decisions and target efficiently for sustained growth.
> ► Cross-Functional Collaboration - In a world where teamwork is key, PMMs are at the center of collaboration to create better customer experiences.
> By leveraging AI to understand customers deeply, make data-driven decisions, and deliver personalized experiences at scale, PMMs are driving efficient growth and success in the software industry.
> Visionary companies that recognize and invest in empowering their PMMs will be better positioned to thrive in the AI era.
> Your thoughts?
> ► How is the role of PMMs evolving in your organization? Are they being recognized as more than just the Masters of the One-Pager and Battle Card Wizards?
> ► Have you empowered your PMMs to drive strategic initiatives?
> ► How are they using AI to understand the market, analyze product-market fit, prioritize top segments, craft defensible positioning and messaging, develop personas and buyer's journeys, launch products, enable sales, and align cross-functional teams?
> Interested in collaborating and learning how to inspire PMMs with what's possible with AI? Please feel free to DM me.

## 436. Wade Foster — 10mo

> Co-founder & CEO at Zapier, YC & Mizzou Alum

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7336473654123294720/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> How do we measure AI fluency at Zapier?
> That’s the top question I’ve gotten this week.
> Below are a few examples of how we assess AI fluency by role.
> Please note that these are not requirements or ‘boxes to be checked’. These are quick examples of the skill types we’re assessing to see where an applicant is on their AI journey.
> We map skills across 4 levels: Unacceptable, Capable, Adoptive, and Transformative.
> 1️⃣ Unacceptable: Resistant to AI tools and skeptical of their value.
> 2️⃣ Capable: Using the most popular tools. Likely under 3 months of hands-on experience
> 3️⃣ Adoptive: Embedding AI in personal workflows. Tuning prompts, chaining models, and automating tasks to boost efficiency.
> 4️⃣ Transformative: Uses AI not just as a tool, but to rethink strategy and deliver user-facing value that wasn’t possible two years ago
> These are evaluated through screenings, async exercises, and live interviews. Signals compound across stages.
> If you’re curious, here are some real questions we’ve asked in interviews:
> MARKETING
> - How is AI changing how you plan or execute campaigns?
> - How do you use AI to personalize messaging, generate content, or analyze performance?
> PEOPLE
> - Can you share an example of how you use AI in your daily work?
> - Can you share an example of a process or program you’ve built using AI? (Why AI, how does it work, tooling, outcomes, how you think about ROI?)
> PRODUCT
> - How is AI impacting SaaS?
> - Give an example of a time you used AI in a product feature. Did it improve with a better/faster/cheaper model?
> A few more things to note:
> 👉None of this is gospel. The best way to improve is to try things, collect data, then improve. The bar will keep rising.
> 👉AI skills vary and are heavily role-specific. These are not the only acceptable answers, just the ones we could fit in the table.
> 👉Engineering ≠ Marketing ≠ Support. Context matters. Requirements vary.
> I’ll continue to share what we’re learning as we refine what AI fluency means at Zapier.
> In the meantime, we’re hiring across a variety of roles. Excited to meet you.

> 1. Sustainability - I focus on high-trust, high-impact relationships, not chasing scale for scale’s sake. My growth is steady and strong because it’s built on word of mouth and real outcomes.  It's not built on hottakes or virality.

> From Intern to Year 2 Microsoft Product Manager 💼
> Here's what I've learned after 2 years working in Big tech
> 1️⃣ AI isn’t a side project, it's engrained in every part of the roadmap
> You should be posing questions to your team if they aren't leveraging AI tools or starting to incorporate it into your suite of products. It's our responsibility as a PM to lead these initiatives - if no one has brought it up, be the one to infuse the change. ✨
> 2️⃣ Metrics are the language of influence 📊
> Why does your product matter should come through data. Your ability to frame your work in measurable impact is what earns trust from your partner teams, leadership and ultimately reflect the value you bring to the table.
> PS. keep these data points to add to your resume
> 3️⃣ Being 'technical' isn't about coding, it's about translating complexity
> Can I write code, yes...do I at work? No. And you don't have to in all PM roles.
> But being technical also means asking the right questions in architecture reviews, de-risking your engineers from other dependencies and understanding overall architecture of your product to make more informed decisions when engineering is not there.

## 443. Yi Lin Pei — 10mo

> I help PMMs land their dream jobs & thrive in them | Founder, Courageous Careers | 3x PMM Leader | Berkeley MBA

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7336470920523300866/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **loves**

> Interviewer: “How do you use AI in your product marketing workflow?”
> More and more PMMs are getting asked this question in interviews. But the answer is NOT what you think.
> Because while it sounds like a simple question, most PMMs get it wrong.
> They either rattle off tools they’ve tried… or draw a blank.
> What interviewers really want to know is this:
> → Can you use AI to accelerate your work without outsourcing your judgment?
> Here’s how I coach my clients to answer this with credibility, depth, and clarity:
> 1️⃣ Break it down by workflow, not by tool.
> Don’t just say “I use ChatGPT for X” -  show that you understand your process.
> Example: Competitive intelligence
> “I break the workflow into four stages:
> --> Input gathering
> --> Synthesis
> --> Output creation (e.g. battlecards, positioning)
> --. Stakeholder activation
> I use AI to speed up the repetitive tasks: scanning public content, summarizing web pages, and comparing feature matrices. But I own the synthesis, framing, and storytelling, because that’s where strategy lives.”
> This shows you’re not just using AI as a gimmick but actually using it to AUGMENT a well-thought-out process.
> 2️⃣ Show you know how to prompt intentionally.
> You don’t need to be an AI engineer.
> But you should be able to say:
> “I use a structured prompting framework to reduce noise and get usable first drafts faster.”
> Some use PREP (Persona, Role, Example, Purpose) or CRAFT (Context, Role, Ask, Format, Tone).
> Whatever your version, it should reflect intention and repeatability. Even better if you can share a favorite prompt and what it helps you do.
> Example:
> “When testing messaging options, I’ll say:
> ‘Act as a skeptical customer in [industry]. I’ll give you a value prop. Challenge it with 3 objections.’
> That helps me refine our narrative from a buyer’s lens.”
> 3️⃣ Anchor it in strategic impact.
> This is where most candidates fall flat. They talk about outputs (“I used Claude to generate messaging”), not outcomes.
> You should connect your AI usage back to:
> --> Faster time-to-insight
> --> Better decision-making
> --> Higher-quality GTM assets
> Example:
> “For a recent product launch, AI helped me accelerate research and draft positioning faster, but the real impact came from how I partnered with Sales to tailor the enablement strategy, and used AI to prototype messaging variants that we then tested live with customers. That’s what helped us shorten ramp time and close two deals in the first 45 days.”
> --
> To summarize, the best candidates don’t just name tools.
> They show systems-level thinking, discernment, and fluency with new methods. And they tie everything back to business outcomes.
> What’s been a helpful framework for you to answer this question?
> ❓ If you are a hiring manager, what do you expect from candidates?
> hashtag
> #productmarketing
> hashtag
> #jobsearch
> hashtag
> #ai

## 445. Kaizad Hansotia — 10mo

> Founder & CEO, Swirl | Building Agentic Commerce OS | AI Sales Agents, LLM Agents, Pricing Agents | 3x Founder

[source](https://www.linkedin.com/feed/update/urn:li:activity:7336469614358384641/) · type: `activity` · reaction: **insightful**

> “Scaling content with AI is the biggest lie.”
> Tim’s post hit hard—and he’s right.
> You can’t take a blog post, auto-spin it into a YouTube video, slice it into 10 LinkedIn carousels, and expect any of it to land. That’s not strategy—it’s spam at scale.
> But there’s a bigger truth behind this...
> Great content isn’t scalable.
> But Great insight is. And this is precisely why AI shouldn’t scale formats. It should scale insight.
> That’s where I see the next shift happening.
> Most brands don’t fail because they lack content.
> They fail because they don’t know what their customer actually cares about.
> - They ship videos before understanding objections.
> - They optimize PDPs before understanding intent.
> - They write ads before reading 10,000 real user reviews.
> At Swirl, we’re not using AI to repurpose formats or build content factories.
> We’re building insight engines—AI agents that extract customer truth—from YouTube, Reddit, Inc., Instagram, TikTok, and product review sites for the raw signals that GTM teams need to move and give them the One piece of content they never had before:
> Because the best content doesn’t come from ChatGPT.
> It comes from listening better than your competitors.
> Imagine a product marketer who knows the top 3 objections before a new SKU even launches.
> Imagine a PDP that writes itself based on what real customers are asking—and buying.
> That’s what AI should do.
> Not scale junk.
> But, Scale confidence.
> - Confidence that your messaging matches your market.
> - Confidence that your video hits the objection before it’s voiced.
> - Confidence that your copy speaks to what people are actually searching for.
> That’s how AI scales go-to-market.
> Not by creating noise, but by clarifying the signal.
> P.S - Thanks, Tim (Image credits), for sparking the right kind of fire. The answer to “what’s worth scaling” is never content. It’s clarity.
